Divine Attributes

Al-Razi's Doxology: Divine Attributes and Transcendence
Al-Razi opens his work with an extended doxology praising God's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses doxology to systematically affirm God's essential attributes and transcendence, laying the foundation for kalam arguments.

Core Idea
Praise in kalam is systematic theology, not mere poetry.

Al-Razi opens his major theological work with an extended doxology that is far more than a rhetorical flourish. He uses the language of praise to establish the core attributes of God—such as life, power, will, wisdom, and creatorship—while simultaneously negating all imperfections from them. This passage grounds the entire ensuing discussion in a framework of divine transcendence and absolute sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with reverence
Read the Arabic doxology aloud if possible, letting its rhythm and vocabulary sink in. Then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Note the repeated use of verbs like 'تقدست', 'تنزهت', 'تعالت' indicating negation of imperfections.
2
Identify the attributes affirmed
Al-Razi explicitly names five attributes: life, wisdom, will, power, and creatorship. Find each one in the text and see what he denies about them.
He pairs each attribute with a specific negation (e.g., life above sickness, wisdom above doubt).
3
Understand the tanzih framework
Recognize that the doxology consistently distances God from any imperfection or limitation. This is a form of negative theology (tanzih) that complements positive attributes.
The phrase 'تعالت قدرته عن الاحتياج إلى الآلات' shows God's power needs no instruments.
4
Connect to the closing shahada
The doxology concludes with the testimony of faith and praise of the Prophet's miracle, the Quran. See how the entire passage leads to affirming tawhid and prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم
مقدمة المؤلف
رب أنعمت فزد
قال مولانا الداعي إلى الله، أبو عبد الله و محمد بن عمر بن الحسين: الرازي - قدس الله روحه ونور ضريحه - :
الحمد لله المتعالي بكبريائه عن علائق عقول المخلوقات، المتعظم بسناء صمديته عن مناسبة الكائنات والممكنات. مبدع المفردات والمركبات، ومخترع الذوات والصفات، وموجد الحيوان والنبات، ومقدر الحركات والسكنات، ومنشىء الباقيات والمتغيرات. داحى المدحوات، وسامك المسموكات، ومصلح أحوال الأحياء والأموات، ومحصل الأرزاق والأقوات، في جميع الأوقات. تقدست حياته عن السقم والألم والممات، وتنزهت حكمته عن الشكوك والأوهام والشبهات. وتبرأت إرادته عن الميول والأماني والشهوات، وتعالت قدرته عن الاحتياج إلى الآلات والأدوات، وتعظمت خلاقيته عن جر المنافع والخيرات، ودفع المضار والآفات. يرزق الوحوش في الفلوات، ويحفظ الحيتان في قعور البحار الزاخرات، ويعلم ذرات الأمواج المتلاطمات، ويخلص المتحيرين من معضلات البليات، ويجيب المضطرين عند الإخلاص في الدعوات. فبوجوب وجوده تقطع سلسلة الحاجات، وبجود جوده بيتناهى تصاعد درجات الافتقارات والضرورات. فسبحانه هو الله الذي لا يعزب عن فضاء قضائه مثقال ذرة في الأرض ولا في السموات. أحمده على ما أفاض علينا من الخيرات، ودفع عنا من البليات. وأشهد أن لا إله إلا الله وحده لا شريك له، شهادة تبقى آثار أنوارها على كرور الدهور والشهور والساعات وتترقى آثار بيناتها إلى مصاعد الخيرات، ومعارج البركات. وأشهد أن محمداً عبده ورسوله، المؤيد بأظهر المعجزات، وأبهر البراهين والبينات. وهو القرآن العظيم، البالغ في كمال الفصاحة إلى أبلغ الغايات، وأقصى النهايات، وعلى آله وأصحابه، صلاة توجب لهم مزيد الدرجات والكرامات. وسلم تسليما.
In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Introduction of the author. Lord, You have bestowed, so increase. Our master, the caller to God, Abu Abdullah and Muhammad ibn Umar ibn al-Husayn al-Razi – may God sanctify his soul and illuminate his grave – said: Praise be to God, elevated by His majesty above the attachments of the intellects of created beings, magnified by the glory of His self-sufficiency above any resemblance to contingent and possible beings. Originator of individual things and compounds, Inventor of essences and attributes, Creator of animals and plants, Apportioner of movements and rests, Producer of permanent things and changing things. Spreader of the expanded ones, Raiser of the raised ones, Reformer of the states of the living and the dead, Provider of provisions and foods at all times. Exalted is His life above sickness, pain, and death; Innocent is His wisdom from doubts, fantasies, and ambiguities. Free is His will from inclinations, wishes, and desires; Transcendent is His power from need of instruments and tools; Magnified is His creatorship from drawing benefits and goods and repelling harms and afflictions. He provides for wild beasts in the deserts, preserves fish in the depths of overflowing seas, knows the atoms of colliding waves, delivers the perplexed from the difficulties of calamities, and answers the distressed when they are sincere in supplication. By the necessity of His existence the chain of needs is cut off, and by the bounty of His generosity the ascending degrees of dependency and necessities do not reach an end. Glory be to Him; He is God, from whom not an atom's weight in the earth or in the heavens escapes the expanse of His decree. I praise Him for the good He has lavished upon us and the afflictions He has repelled from us. I bear witness that there is no god but God alone, no partner with Him, a witness whose traces of light remain over the passage of ages, months, and hours, and whose clear signs ascend to the ascents of good and the stairways of blessings. And I bear witness that Muhammad is His servant and messenger, supported by the most manifest miracles and the most splendid proofs and evidences. And he is the Mighty Quran, reaching in perfection of eloquence the utmost limits and furthest ends. And upon his family and companions, a prayer that secures for them increased degrees and honors. And peace and salutations.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is the opening doxology of al-Razi's work, in which he praises God and affirms His attributes. It begins with the basmala and a supplication, then al-Razi identifies himself. The praise proceeds by listing divine attributes: God's transcendence (ta'ālī) above the understanding of creatures, His self-sufficiency (ṣamadiyya) making Him unlike any contingent being, and His roles as Creator of all things, from individual entities and compounds to essences and attributes, animals and plants, and as apportioner of all movements and stillness. Al-Razi then emphasizes God's life (ḥayāh) being free from imperfection, His wisdom (ḥikmah) from doubt, His will (irādah) from base inclinations, His power (qudrah) from need of instruments, and His creatorship (khalāqiyyah) from any benefit to Himself. These negations (tanzīh) establish God's absolute perfection. Next, al-Razi describes God's providence: providing for wild beasts, preserving sea creatures, knowing the finest details, aiding the perplexed, and answering the distressed. The phrase 'by the necessity of His existence the chain of needs is cut off' highlights that all other existence is contingent upon God. The doxology concludes with praise for blessings, the shahādah affirming tawḥīd and the prophethood of Muhammad, and the mention of his miracle (the Quran) and prayer upon him and his family. The entire passage functions as a theological preamble, grounding the ensuing discussion in the recognition of God's absolute sovereignty and perfection.

تسبيح

صفات الله
حياة

خلاقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Doxologies are merely poetic expressions without theological substance.
Correction: Al-Razi's doxology systematically affirms divine attributes and employs tanzih, making it a theological preamble that establishes key principles for rational arguments about God.

Checkpoint
Which attribute does al-Razi describe as being free from 'sickness, pain, and death'?
A) Power (qudrah)
B) Life (hayat)
C) Will (iradah)
D) Wisdom (hikmah)

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's doxology is a theological blueprint that affirms God's attributes while negating all imperfections, serving as a model for understanding divine transcendence in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you praise God in your daily prayers, do you consciously affirm that His life is above all sickness and death, or have you let the words become hollow? What would it look like for your tongue to be a witness to His transcendence, not just a reciter of phrases?
?
Al-Razi describes God as the one who 'answers the distressed when they are sincere in supplication.' Think of the last time you were in distress: was your sincerity genuine, or were you merely seeking relief? How can you cultivate a heart that truly depends on Him, not just when the calamity strikes, but in every moment?
?
The doxology asserts that 'by the necessity of His existence the chain of needs is cut off.' Where in your life are you still clinging to a chain of dependence on created things—approval, wealth, control—when only His existence can truly satisfy? What is one attachment you can sever today to experience the 'cut' He alone provides?

God is a Free Agent, Not Necessitated by Essence
Responding to the objection that divine action requires necessity by essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends God's free will by distinguishing human originated will from God's eternal will, and by turning a further objection back onto the opponent's own view of God's knowledge.

Core Idea
God's eternal will is self-sufficient; human will needs an external preponderator.

This passage responds to a key objection: if God's action depends on something, doesn't that make Him a necessitated cause? The author explains the difference between human and divine will, and then turns a further objection back on the opponent. Understanding this argument is crucial for grasping the Ash'ari position on divine freedom and the role of will.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial answer
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is stated and the initial answer begins. Notice the claim that God might be necessitated by essence, and the response that action depends on an incentive and a preponderating will.
2
Identify the key distinction
Focus on the contrast between human will (originated, needs a preponderator) and God's will (eternal, independent). This is the core of the first answer.
Human will is muhdath, God's will is qadim azali.
3
Understand the second objection
The opponent reformulates: could God's will have attached to a different time for creation? If yes, a preponderator is needed; if no, God is necessitated. Read this part carefully.
4
See the counter-objection on knowledge
The author answers by turning the same dilemma onto God's knowledge. If the opponent's theology accepts that God's eternal knowledge does not imply necessity, the same applies to will.
This is a dialectical move showing consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثالث : وهو قوله : إن الذي ذكرتم يقتضي كونه تعالى موجبا بالذات ، لا فاعلا بالاختيار ) فجوابه : إنا قد دللنا على أن الفعل يتوقف على حصول الداعية والإرادة المرجحة . ثم لما تأملنا أن كل ما للعبد من الدواعي والإرادات فهو محدث . فلا جرم افتقرت دواعي العباد وإرادتهم إلى مرجح آخر . فأما إرادة الله تعالى فإنها قديمة أزلية ، فلا جرم استغنت عن إرادة أخرى . فظهر الفرق بين البابين . فإن قالوا : هب أنه ظهر الفرق من الوجه الذي ذكرتم . إلا أنا نلزمكم هذا الإلزام من وجه آخر. فنقول : إرادة الله تعالى تعلقت بإحداث العالم في الوقت المعين. فهل كان يمكن أن تتعلق بإحداث العالم في وقت آخر ، بدلا عن التعلق الأول ، أو يمكن ؟ فإن كان الأول لزم افتقاره إلى مرجح ، وإن كان الثاني لزم أن يكون موجبا لا مختارا والجواب : إن هذا الإشكال الذي أوردتم علينا في الإرادة : وأورد عليكم في العلم . فإن لزم من هذا القدر كونه تعالى موجبا ، فهو أيضا وارد عليكم بسبب العلم
As for the third question: namely his statement: 'What you have mentioned necessitates that He, exalted, is necessitated (mujib) by essence, not an agent by choice (fa'il bil-ikhtiyar).' Its answer: We have already proved that action depends on the presence of the incentive (da'iyah) and the preponderating will (al-iradah al-murajjihah). Then, when we reflected that everything of the servant's incentives and wills is originated (muhdath), inevitably the servant's incentives and wills are in need of another preponderator (murajjih). As for the will of God, exalted, it is eternal (qadimah azaliyyah), inevitably it is independent of another will. So the difference between the two domains appears. If they say: 'Suppose the difference appears from the aspect you mentioned, but we oblige you with this obligation from another aspect.' We say: The will of God, exalted, is attached to bringing the world into existence at the determined time. Could it have been attached to bringing it into existence at another time, instead of the first attachment, or could it? If the first, then it would need a preponderator; if the second, then He would be necessitated (mujib), not free. The answer: This difficulty you raised against us regarding will is also raised against you regarding knowledge. For if this entails that He, exalted, is necessitated, then it also applies to you because of knowledge.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is an answer to the third objection, which claims that the author's earlier argument implies God is necessitated (mujib bi al-dhat) rather than a free agent (fa'il bil-ikhtiyar). The author first summarizes his earlier proof: action depends on an incentive and a preponderating will. Since all human incentives and wills are created (muhdath), they require another preponderator. But God's will is eternal and self-sufficient, needing no further will. This establishes a distinction. The opponent then reformulates: God's will is attached to creating the world at a specific time; could it have been attached to another time? If yes, then God would need a preponderator to choose that time; if no, then God is necessitated. The author responds by turning the objection back on the opponent: the same dilemma applies to God's knowledge (if His knowledge is eternal and specific, does He have alternatives?). Thus, if the opponent's own theology accepts that God's eternal knowledge does not imply necessity, the same applies to His will. This is a classic dialectical move, showing consistency in the author's position.

Key Terms
موجب بالذات
فاعل بالاختيار
داعية
إرادة مرجحة
مرجح
قديم أزلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's eternal will implies He has no choice and is forced to act in a specific way.
Correction: Eternality itself avoids the need for an external preponderator; God's will is self-sufficient and freely chooses, as it is not caused by anything else.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference the author draws between human will and God's will in answering the third objection?
A) Human will is eternal, God's will is created.
B) Human will needs a preponderator because it is originated, while God's will is eternal and independent.
C) God's will is not attached to specific actions.
D) Human will is free, God's will is necessitated.

Key Takeaway
God's eternal will does not necessitate His actions; rather, it is the basis of His free choice, unlike human will which requires an external cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision, do you rely on external circumstances to tip the scale, or do you act from a settled will? How does the contrast between human and divine will in this lesson expose your dependence on God for even your smallest choices?
?
The objection raised against divine will is turned back using divine knowledge. Is there an area of your life where you hold God to a standard you wouldn't apply to yourself? How does this lesson challenge your intellectual honesty before God?
?
God's will is independent and needs no preponderator. Yet you constantly seek preponderators for your own actions—approval, comfort, logic. What would it mean for you to act with a will that is truly settled in obedience to God, not needing external validation?

First Proof: Faith Would Entail God's Ignorance
A brief proof by reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that faith cannot occur from those God knows will not believe, because it would require God's knowledge to become ignorance.

Core Idea
Faith from the foreknown would turn God's knowledge into ignorance, which is impossible.

This lesson presents a crisp logical argument from the text: if someone God knows will not believe were to believe, then God's knowledge would no longer match reality, becoming ignorance. Since that is impossible for God, the occurrence of such faith is also impossible. We will walk through the proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once slowly. Let the words sink in before analyzing them. Notice the key terms: علم الله, جهل, محال.
The passage is only three sentences but packed with meaning.
2
Understand the logical structure
Identify the conditional statement, the claim of impossibility, the principle applied, and the conclusion. This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this argument is important for the doctrine of God's unchangeable knowledge. It guards against thinking that God's knowledge is affected by human choices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإيمان منه يستلزم انقلاب علم الله جهلاً. وهذا محال . ومستلزم المحال محال . فيلزم : أن يكون صدور الإيمان عنه محالاً
Faith from him would necessitate the transformation of God's knowledge into ignorance. That is impossible. And what leads to the impossible is impossible. So it follows that the occurrence of faith from him is impossible.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a brief proof by reductio ad absurdum. It begins by stating the conditional consequence: if someone whom God knows will not believe were to believe, then God's knowledge would become ignorance, because knowledge must match reality. This consequence is impossible (محال) for God. Then it applies the principle that whatever leads to the impossible is itself impossible. Therefore, the occurrence of faith from that person is impossible. The argument relies on the definition of knowledge as immutable correspondence to reality.

Key Terms
علم الله
جهل
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that God's knowledge changes as events unfold, so if someone believed, God would simply update His knowledge.
Correction: This argument shows that God's knowledge is unchangeable and always true; if it could change, it would have been ignorance before the change.

Checkpoint
What contradiction is concluded in the first argument?
A) God's power would be limited
B) God's knowledge would become ignorance
C) Faith and disbelief cannot coexist
D) God's word would be false

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is immutable; if a foreknown unbeliever could believe, God's knowledge would become ignorance, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever live as if God's knowledge of your actions is flexible — as if you can 'change His mind' by your choices? How does this proof confront that subtle arrogance, and what will you do today to submit to His perfect knowledge?
?
If you truly believe that it is impossible for a foreknown unbeliever to have faith, does that make you question the sincerity of your own faith? Or does it drive you to thank God that He granted you faith? What specific prayer of gratitude will you offer right now?
?
When you sin, do you assume God 'didn't see it coming' or that He will adjust His plan? This lesson says that would turn His knowledge into ignorance. How does that realization change the way you approach repentance tonight?

Second Proof: Contradiction of Existence and Non-Existence
The author presents the second proof that faith cannot coexist with God's knowledge of its non-occurrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's knowledge of non-occurrence makes the occurrence of faith impossible, based on the correspondence definition of knowledge.

Core Idea
True knowledge demands that the known state actually holds.

This lesson examines a second rational proof against the compatibility of faith with God's foreknowledge. It hinges on the nature of knowledge as correspondence (mutaabaqa). Understanding this proof clarifies a key point in the qadar debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Familiarize yourself with the logical flow.
Notice the key term 'mutabaqa' (correspondence).
2
Identify the definition of knowledge
The proof begins by asserting that knowledge is only knowledge if it corresponds to the known. This is the foundational premise.
Knowledge is truth-tracking.
3
Follow the logical deduction
Trace the conditional: If God knows faith will not occur, then non-occurrence must be true. If faith occurs despite that, a contradiction arises (simultaneous existence and non-existence).
The conclusion: such coexistence is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن وجود الإيمان، يستحيل أن يحصل مع العلم بعدم الإيمان لأنه إنما يكون علماً ، لو كان مطابقاً للمعلوم. والعلم بعدم الإيمان ، إنما يكون مطابقاً لو حصل عدم الإيمان. فلو وجد الإيمان مع العلم بعدم الإيمان ، يلزم أن يجتمع في الإيمان كونه موجوداً ومعدوماً معاً . وهو محال . أنه تعالى لما علم منه عدم الإيمان كان وجود الإيمان منه محالاً . ولو علم منه وجود الإيمان ، كان عدم الإيمان منه محالاً
The second: The existence of faith cannot occur alongside knowledge of the non-occurrence of faith, because knowledge is only knowledge if it corresponds to the known. And knowledge of the non-occurrence of faith only corresponds if the non-occurrence of faith actually obtains. So if faith existed together with knowledge of its non-occurrence, it would necessitate that faith be both existent and non-existent simultaneously, which is impossible. Since He, exalted, knew from him the non-occurrence of faith, the existence of faith from him is impossible. And if He knew from him the occurrence of faith, then the non-occurrence of faith from him would be impossible.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the nature of knowledge. It begins by defining knowledge as correspondence (مطابقة) between knowledge and the known. God's knowledge of the non-occurrence of faith requires that the non-occurrence actually be the case. If faith were to occur despite this knowledge, then faith would both exist (by actual occurrence) and not exist (because that is what God knows), a contradiction in terms. Hence, faith cannot arise from someone God knows will not believe. The same logic applies in reverse: if God knows faith will occur, then non-occurrence is impossible.

Key Terms
علم
مطابقة
معلوم
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume God's knowledge is passive and does not impose constraints on possibility.
Correction: This argument shows that God's knowledge, being true, requires the known state to be fixed, making any alternative contradictory.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, why is faith impossible alongside God's knowledge of its non-occurrence?
A) God would be ignorant
B) Faith would be both existent and non-existent
C) God's power would be negated
D) The person would be compelled

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge, being true, necessitates the reality of what is known, making any alternation impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly believe that God already knew you would sin, and that your action is part of His unchanging knowledge? How does that affect the sincerity of your repentance? What would change if you truly internalized that God's knowledge is not affected by your actions but rather confirms them?
?
If God knows that you will never have faith, does that mean you have no choice? Or does this passage reveal something deeper about the link between knowledge and reality? What does this mean for your daily struggle to increase in faith and good deeds?
?
Consider a moment when you felt your actions could 'surprise' God. What does this lesson say about that assumption? How can you now approach your relationship with God's knowledge with greater humility and seriousness?

Fourth Proof: Knowledge of Disbelief Makes Faith Impossible
Argument that God's command to those He knows will never believe entails a contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical contradiction in God commanding those He knows will never believe to have faith, and how this establishes the impossibility of faith for such individuals.

Core Idea
God's foreknowledge creates a logical barrier to faith for those He knows will never believe.

This lesson presents the fourth proof in a series refuting the possibility of what God has foretold will not happen. The author argues that if God commands faith to those He knows will never believe, then faith would require them to affirm that very foreknowledge, leading to a contradiction. Understanding this argument deepens one's grasp of the relationship between divine knowledge and human obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the argument's flow. Notice the two statements about those informed they will not believe.
2
Identify the key components
Identify the obligation (تكليف) of faith, the requirement of faith to affirm God's information, and God's information that they will never believe.
3
Trace the contradiction
Understand why believing that one will never believe is contradictory with having faith. Faith requires affirmation of God's truth, including that they will never believe, so they must affirm disbelief.
The two opposites are faith and the affirmation that one will never have faith.
4
Consider the conclusion
See how this impossibility establishes that faith cannot occur from those God has informed will not believe.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إنه تعالى كلف هؤلاء الذين أخبر عنهم بأنهم لا يؤمنون البتة : بالإيمان، والإيمان يعتبر فيه تصديق الله في كل ما أخبر عنه . ومما أخبر عنهم : أنهم لا يؤمنون قط . فقد صاروا مكلفين بأن يؤمنوا بأنهم لا يؤمنون قط . وهذا تكليف بالجمع بين الضدين. وذلك لا يوجد البتة ، ويمتنع وجوده . فثبت : أن هؤلاء الذين أخبر الله عنهم أنهم لا يؤمنون : يستحيل صدور الإيمان عنهم ، والذين أخبر عنهم أنهم لا يؤمنون : يستحيل عدم الإيمان عنهم
Fourth: He, exalted, imposed an obligation on those whom He informed that they will never believe: to believe. And faith requires affirming God in all that He informed about. Among what He informed about them: that they will never believe at all. So they became obligated to believe that they will never believe at all. And this is an obligation to combine two opposites. That does not exist at all, and its existence is impossible. So it is established: that those whom God informed that they will not believe: the occurrence of faith from them is impossible, and those whom He informed that they will not believe: the absence of faith from them is impossible.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument in a sequence refuting the possibility of what God has foretold will not happen. The author argues that God commands those He has informed will never believe to have faith. Faith entails affirming God in everything He informs. Since God has informed that they will never believe, faith would require them to affirm that very statement—i.e., to believe that they will never believe. This is a command to believe two contradictory things simultaneously (that they believe and that they will never believe), which is impossible. Thus, the impossibility of faith for these individuals is established.

Key Terms
تكليف
ضدين
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument proves that God's knowledge negates human free will.
Correction: The argument does not address free will; it highlights a logical contradiction in commanding something that contradicts one's own foreknowledge.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue is contradictory about God's command to those He knows will never believe?
A) They are commanded to believe and to disbelieve simultaneously.
B) They are commanded to believe that they will never believe, which is a contradiction.
C) They are commanded to obey and disobey at the same time.
D) They are commanded to know the future, which is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The command to believe in one's own eternal disbelief is logically impossible, so faith is impossible for those God knows will never believe.

Fifth Proof: Attempts to Change God's Words
The author argues that God's rebuke of disbelievers for trying to change His words proves the impossibility of what He foretold.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's censure of attempts to change His words demonstrates the impossibility of what He foretold.

Core Idea
Trying to change God's word reveals that His news must come true.

This lesson presents the fifth proof from divine attributes. The author uses a verse and logical reasoning to show that what God has informed about is necessarily bound to occur. The argument hinges on the concept of changing God's speech. We'll see how the author draws a clear conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Pay attention to the verse quoted.
The verse refers to disbelievers wanting to change God's words.
2
Identify the argument's components
Identify the premise (God's censure), the supporting verse, the logical deduction, and the conclusion.
3
Understand the key logical step
The author reasons that intending to bring about what God said would not occur is equivalent to intending to change God's speech, which is forbidden.
This step links divine foreknowledge to impossibility.
4
Consider the conclusion
Reflect on the conclusion: what God knows and informs will not happen is impossible to occur.
It does not deny free will but asserts logical consequence.
5
Address the common misunderstanding
Think about the misconception that this proves determinism. The author is showing a logical implication of divine foreknowledge, not causal determinism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : إنه تعالى عاب الكفار على أنهم حاولوا فعل شيء على خلاف ما أخبر الله عنه . فقال : ( يريدون أن يبدلوا كلام الله . قل : لن تتبعونا . كذلكم قال الله من قبل ) فثبت : أن القصد إلى تكوين ما أخبر الله عن عدم وقوعه : قصد إلى تبديل كلام الله . وذلك منهي عنه [ فلما أخبر الله عنهم : أنهم لا يؤمنون ، كان القصد إلى تكوينه قصدا إلى تبديل كلامه . وذلك منهي عنه ] وكان ذلك حاصلاً، سواء حاول تكوين الإيمان أو لم يحاول . فثبت : أن الذي علم الله أنه لا يقع ، وأخبر أنه لا يقع ، كان وقوعه ممتنع الحصول .
Fifth: He, exalted, censured the disbelievers for attempting to do something contrary to what God informed about. He said: 'They want to change the words of God. Say: You will not follow us. Thus God has said before.' So it is established: that the intention to bring about what God informed would not occur is an intention to change God's speech. And that is forbidden. [So when God informed about them that they will not believe, the intention to bring it about is an intention to change His speech. And that is forbidden.] And that obtains whether they attempt to bring about faith or not. So it is established: that what God knew would not occur, and informed that it would not occur, its occurrence is impossible of attainment.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument. The author cites God's censure of disbelievers who try to act against His decree, quoting the verse that says they want to change God's words. The author reasons that intending to bring about something God has informed will not happen is equivalent to intending to change God's speech, which is forbidden. This applies whether or not one actually attempts the act. Therefore, anything God knows and informs will not happen is impossible to occur.

Key Terms

ممتنع الحصول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof shows that God's foreknowledge compels people to act, i.e., determinism.
Correction: The proof is about the logical impossibility of contradicting God's true information, not about causal compulsion. Free will is not denied; it is simply that what God knows will not happen cannot happen, but people still choose.

Key Takeaway
God's censure of those who try to change His words proves that His informed events are impossible to alter.

Divine Knowledge and the Possible
Author's second proof: knowledge does not alter the possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second proof demonstrates that divine knowledge does not alter the intrinsic nature of possible things like faith and disbelief.

Core Idea
Knowledge conforms to reality; it does not create it.

This lesson presents the second proof from the author, which argues from the nature of knowledge itself. The key insight is that knowledge relates to its object exactly as that object is. This has profound implications for understanding God's foreknowledge and human free will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the conditional structure.
2
Identify the two conditional statements
Look for 'إن كان ممكناً' and 'إن كان واجباً'. These show how knowledge relates to different types of things.
The principle is that knowledge follows the known.
3
Consider the example of faith and disbelief
Apply the principle: faith and disbelief are possible in themselves. God knows them as possible, not necessary.
If they were made necessary, knowledge would be effective, which is impossible.
4
Understand the conclusion
The proof shows that divine knowledge does not change the possible. It simply knows things as they are.
This refutes the misconception that foreknowledge forces events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن العلم يتعلق بالمعلوم على ما هو عليه . فإن كان ممكناً ، علمه ممكناً وإن كان واجباً ، علمه واجباً . ولا شك أن الإيمان والكفر ، بالنظر إلى ذاته من باب الممكنات . ولو صار واجب الوجود ، لصار العلم مؤثراً في المعلوم . وقد بينا أنه محال .
The second: Knowledge relates to the known as it is. If it is possible, He knows it as possible; and if it is necessary, He knows it as necessary. There is no doubt that faith and disbelief, with respect to their essence, are from the category of possible things. If they were to become necessary of existence, then knowledge would become effective in the known. And we have shown that this is impossible.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This is a proof from the nature of knowledge. The author states that knowledge (al-ʿilm) relates to the known (al-maʿlūm) as it is. For example, if the known is possible (mumkin), God knows it as possible; if it is necessary (wājib), He knows it as necessary. Faith and disbelief, in themselves, are possible things (mumkināt). If God's knowledge were to make them necessary, then knowledge would be a cause affecting the known, which is impossible—a point already established.

Key Terms
العلم
المعلوم
الممكن
الواجب
الإيمان
الكفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's foreknowledge forces events to happen, eliminating free will.
Correction: Knowledge relates to the known as it is; it does not change the nature of the known. Possible things remain possible in themselves; foreknowledge does not make them necessary.

Checkpoint

A) God's knowledge is identical to His will.
B) Knowledge relates to the known as it is, and faith/disbelief are inherently possible.
C) God does not know future contingents.
D) Faith and disbelief are impossible by nature.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge does not change the nature of possible things; it simply knows them as they are.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Third Proof: Knowledge Does Not Prevent Action
Reductio argument that knowledge not preventing, using human agency intuition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third rational argument proving that God's knowledge does not prevent human actions, based on the absurd consequence that humans would have no power and the intuitive evidence of blame and praise.

Core Idea
If knowledge prevented action, humans would be like stones — but we know we aren't.

This passage presents the third proof against the claim that God's knowledge prevents human actions. The author uses a reductio ad absurdum: if knowledge truly prevented, then everything known by God would be either necessary or impossible for humans, stripping them of all power. But our daily experience of blame, praise, and voluntary choice shows this conclusion is false, thereby proving that knowledge does not prevent action.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once with the literal translation. Notice the logical progression from premise to conclusion.
The passage uses a conditional structure (لو...لما).
2
Identify the reductio structure
Identify the conditional premise, the deduction, the absurd consequence, and the intuitive rebuttal.
The author assumes the opposite to show it leads to an impossibility.
3
Examine the intuitive example
Study the example of the hired object (stone) versus voluntary punch. Understand why blame differs.
Intuition (badiha) is the foundation of the rebuttal.
4
Connect to daily experience
Reflect on how praising good and blaming evil, commanding and rebuking, presuppose genuine human choice.
These practices are universal among rational people.
5
State the conclusion
Conclude that since the absurd consequence is false, the original premise is false: knowledge does not prevent action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو كان العلم والخبر مانعاً ، لما كان العبد قادراً على شيء أصلا . لأن الذي علم الله وقوعه كان واجب الوقوع . والواجب لا قدرة عليه ، والذي علم علمه كان ممتنع الوقوع ، والممتنع لا قدرة عليه . فوجب أن لا يكون العبد قادرا على شيء أصلا ، فكانت حركاته وسكناته ، جارية مجرى حركات الجمادات ، ومجرى الحركات الاضطرارية للحيوانات . لكنا بالبديهية نعلم فساد ذلك . فإن من رمى إنساناً بالأجرة حتى شجة ، فإنه يذم الرامي ، ولا تذم الأجرة ، ويدرك بالبديهة تفرقة بين ما إذا سقطت الأجرة عليه ، وبين ما إذا لكمه إنسان بالاختيار. ولذلك فإن العقلاء ببدائه عقولهم . يدركون الفرق بين مدح المحسن وذم المسيء ، ويلتمسون ويأمرون ويعاتبون ، ويقولون : لم فعلت ؟ ولم تركت ؟ فدل ذلك على أن العلم والخبر غير مانعين من الفعل.
The third: If knowledge and report were preventing, then the servant would not be able to do anything at all. Because what God knows will occur is necessary of occurrence; and the necessary cannot be acted upon, and what He knows will not occur is impossible of occurrence; and the impossible cannot be acted upon. So it would necessarily follow that the servant is not able to do anything at all, and his movements and stillnesses would run in the course of movements of inanimate objects and the course of involuntary movements of animals. But we know by intuition the corruption of that. For if someone throws a hireling [a stone?] at a person until he wounds him, he blames the thrower, not the hireling, and one perceives intuitively a distinction between when the hireling falls on him and when a person punches him voluntarily. Therefore, the rational ones by the intuitions of their minds perceive the difference between praising the good-doer and blaming the evil-doer, and they seek [explanations], command, rebuke, and say: 'Why did you do that?' and 'Why did you leave it?' So that indicates that knowledge and report are not preventive of action.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This is a reductio ad absurdum. The author argues that if God's knowledge or a report prevented an action (i.e., made it necessary or impossible), then humans would have no power over anything. Whatever God knows will happen is necessary, and the necessary cannot be brought about by human power; whatever He knows will not happen is impossible, also beyond power. Then all human actions would be like the movements of inanimate objects or involuntary animal motions. But we know by intuition (bi-l-badīha) this is false. For example, if someone throws a hired object that wounds another, the thrower is blamed, not the object. We intuitively distinguish between a stone falling and a voluntary punch. Rational people naturally praise good and blame evil, command and rebuke, asking 'Why did you do that?' This shows that divine knowledge does not prevent human acts of power.

Key Terms
العلم
الخبر
المانع
العبد
القدرة
الوجوب
الامتناع
البديهة
المدح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God knows all, humans are compelled in their actions.
Correction: The proof shows that divine knowledge does not prevent action because we intuitively experience blame, praise, and choice.

Checkpoint
In the third argument, what would follow if knowledge prevented action?
A) Humans would be rewarded for good deeds.
B) Humans would have no power and be like inanimate objects.
C) God would be unjust.
D) Prophethood would be impossible.

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge does not prevent human action; otherwise, blame and praise would be meaningless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Fourth Proof: Knowledge Does Not Prevent
Author's fourth proof that divine knowledge does not prevent, based on the command to believe being meaningful.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fourth proof demonstrates that divine knowledge does not prevent human action, by showing the absurd consequence of the opposing view.

Core Idea
If knowledge prevented, God's command to believe would be a command to make Him ignorant.

This passage presents a powerful reductio ad absurdum argument. The author imagines what would follow if divine foreknowledge truly prevented the occurrence of events. He shows that such a view would make God's own commands nonsensical, because they would require the impossible—eliminating God's knowledge. This argument helps clarify that divine knowledge and human responsibility coexist without contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the core conditional
The argument begins with a conditional: if knowledge of non-existence prevented existence, then... Ask yourself: what does 'knowledge of non-existence' mean in this context? It refers to God's foreknowledge that something will not happen.
This is a hypothetical scenario to test the claim.
2
Follow the consequence for God's command
If that knowledge prevented existence, then God's command to the unbeliever to believe would be a command to eliminate God's knowledge of his unbelief, i.e., make God ignorant. Notice the chain of reasoning.
The consequence is drawn logically from the premise.
3
Recognize the absurdity
The author then points out that eliminating God's essence and attributes is beyond human capacity. So commanding it would be foolish and vain, which is impossible for a Wise God. Therefore, the initial conditional must be false.
This establishes the impossibility of the premise.
4
Connect to the lesson's goal
Realize that this proof defends the compatibility of divine knowledge and human free will. The command to believe remains meaningful even though God knows the outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : لو كان العلم بالعدم، مانعاً من الوجود . لكان أمر الله الكافر بالإيمان : أمراً له بإعدام علمه وبتجهيله وبتكذيبه . وكما أنه لا يليق بحكمته أن يأمر عباده بأن يعدموه ، فكذلك لا يليق بحكمته أن يأمرهم بأن يعدموا علمه . لان إعدام ذات الله تعالى وصفاته ، ليس في طاقته . فكان الأمر سفهاً وعبثاً . وهو على الحكيم محال . فدل هذا على أن العلم بالعدم لا يكون مانعا من الوجود .
The fourth: If knowledge of non-existence prevented existence, then God's command to the unbeliever to believe would be a command to him to eliminate His knowledge and to render Him ignorant and to deny Him. And just as it is not befitting His wisdom that He command His servants to eliminate Him, likewise it is not befitting His wisdom that He command them to eliminate His knowledge. For the elimination of the essence of God Almighty and His attributes is not within [human] capacity. So the command would be foolish and vain. And that is impossible for the Wise. So this indicates that knowledge of non-existence is not preventive of existence.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This is a proof by considering the consequence of the opponent's view. If God's knowledge that the unbeliever will not believe (ʿadam) prevented existence (wujūd) of belief, then God's command to the unbeliever to believe would be a command to eliminate God's own knowledge of that unbelief, i.e., to make God ignorant and deny Him. But eliminating God's essence and attributes is not within human power. So such a command would be foolish (safah) and vain (ʿabath), which cannot be attributed to the Wise (al-ḥakīm). Therefore, God's knowledge of non-existence does not prevent existence; otherwise, His command would be absurd.

Key Terms
العلم بالعدم
المانع من الوجود
أمر الله
الإيمان
إعدام العلم
الحكيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's foreknowledge of unbelief makes the command to believe contradictory or impossible.
Correction: The proof shows the command is meaningful because knowledge does not prevent; otherwise, the command would be absurd.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, if God's knowledge of non-existence prevented existence, what would His command to the unbeliever entail?
A) A command to do the impossible.
B) A command to eliminate God's own knowledge, making Him ignorant.
C) A command to act against God's will.
D) A command that is easily fulfilled.

Key Takeaway
Divine foreknowledge does not prevent human action; otherwise, God's commands would be absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Possible Cannot Become Necessary by Knowledge
Author's fifth proof using contradiction to show knowledge does not necessitate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine knowledge does not transform possible things into necessary things, based on the impossibility of contradiction.

Core Idea
Knowledge reveals possibility; it does not create necessity.

In this fifth proof, the author presents a crisp logical argument that God's knowledge of possible things does not change their inherent nature. The proof hinges on a simple but powerful contradiction: if knowledge made faith necessary, faith would be both possible and impossible at the same time. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow from premise to conclusion.
The argument is tight; each sentence builds on the previous.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the terms for 'possible', 'necessary', 'knowledge', and 'ignorance'. Understand how they relate.
These terms are the building blocks of the proof.
3
Trace the contradiction
Follow the conditional: 'if knowledge made it necessary, then the same thing would be both possible and not possible.' Reflect on why this is impossible.
This is the heart of the argument.
4
Apply the lesson
Consider a real example (e.g., a possible action). Does God's foreknowledge force it to happen? Explain using the proof.
The proof defends human responsibility and divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : إن الإيمان في نفسه من قبيل الممكنات ، نظراً إلى ذاته وعينه فوجب أن يعلمه الله من الممكنات الجائزات . إذ لو لم يكن كذلك ، لكان ذلك العلم جهلاً ، وهو محال . وإذا كان علم الله من الممكنات الجائزات ، التي لا يمتنع وجوده ولا عدمه - ولو صار بسبب العلم واجباً - لزم أن يجتمع على الشيء الواحد : كونه من الممكنات ، وكونه ليس من الممكنات . وهو محال .
The fifth: Faith in itself is from the category of possible things, with regard to its essence and very self. So it is necessary that God knows it as one of the possible contingent things. For if it were not so, that knowledge would be ignorance, which is impossible. And since God's knowledge is of possible contingent things, which neither their existence nor their non-existence is impossible—if it were to become necessary due to knowledge—then it would necessarily follow that the same thing would combine being from the possible things and being not from the possible things. And that is impossible.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This is a proof from the logical impossibility of contradiction. Faith is inherently possible (mumkin). Therefore, God must know it as possible, otherwise His knowledge would be ignorance (jahl). But if, due to God's knowledge, faith became necessary (wājib), then the same thing (faith) would be both possible (as it is in itself) and not possible (if made necessary). That is a contradiction, hence impossible. So knowledge does not make the possible necessary.

Key Terms
الإيمان
الممكنات
الجائزات
الواجب
العلم
الجهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's foreknowledge makes events necessary, eliminating human freedom.
Correction: This proof shows that knowledge does not change the ontological nature of the known. God knows possible things as possible; they remain contingent. Thus, human choice is preserved.

Checkpoint
What would happen if God's knowledge made faith necessary?
A) Faith would be both possible and impossible.
B) Faith would become an act of worship.
C) God's knowledge would become perfect.
D) Human responsibility would increase.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of possible things does not make them necessary; otherwise, a logical contradiction would arise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own faith (imān), do you sometimes treat it as if God’s knowledge of it fixes it in place—an unchangeable fate—rather than approaching it as a possible thing that you actively choose and nurture? How will you take responsibility for its increase or decrease today, knowing that God’s knowledge does not make it necessary?
?
If you have ever used the notion that 'God already knows what I will do' as an excuse to sin or to avoid effort, how does this proof challenge that excuse? Contradicting the nature of the possible by claiming knowledge necessitates it is logically absurd. Will you reject that assumption and own your choices now?
?
The argument shows that a possible thing cannot become necessary through knowledge without contradiction. How does this truth free you from the illusion of fatalism? What specific area of your life—prayer, charity, a bad habit—will you change today, knowing that God’s knowledge does not force your hand?

The Sixth Proof: Why Commanding the Impossible Destroys Religious Certainty
Sanusi's sixth argument that allowing commanding the impossible leads to total skepticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that if God could command the impossible, then all revelation becomes unreliable, and why this consequence is impossible.

Core Idea
If commanding the impossible is allowed, nothing in religion is trustworthy.

This lesson presents a powerful reductio ad absurdum argument from Imam al-Sanusi. If God could command what is impossible, then the entire edifice of revelation collapses — miracles could be false and scriptures could be lies. The argument shows why this cannot be, reinforcing the necessity of Allah's wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's logical flow come through.
Notice the structure: premise, conditional, consequences, conclusion.
2
Understand the premise: commanding the impossible is absurd
The argument starts with the claim that commanding the impossible (al-muḥāl) is 'folly and frivolity' (عبث وسفه). This is intuitively unacceptable for God.
This premise is taken as self-evident in Islamic theology.
3
Follow the chain of consequences
If God could command the impossible in any case, then by extension, anything absurd becomes possible in religion: false miracles, false scriptures, and total uncertainty.
The proof uses a slippery slope argument.
4
Grasp the conclusion and its implication
Since the consequences are false (prophethood and Quran are certainly true), the initial premise that commanding the impossible is allowed must be false. Therefore, divine foreknowledge does not make belief impossible.
This shows that knowledge of unbelief does not make belief impossible for the individual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : إن الأمر بالمحال : عبث وسفه ، فلو جاز ورود الشرع به في بعض الصور ، الجاز وروده به في بقية الصور. فوجب أن لا يمتنع من الباري تعالى : أن يظهر المعجزات على أيدي الكاذبين ، وأن لا تمتنع أقوال الكتب المشتملة على الكذب والأضاليل . وعلى هذا التقدير لا يبقى وثوق بصحة نبوة الأنبياء عليهم السلام ، ولا بصحة القرآن . بل يجوز أن يكون كله كذباً . ولما بطل ذلك ، علمنا : أن العلم بعدم الإيمان، والخبر عن عدم الإيمان ، لا يقتضي انقلاب الإيمان ممتنعاً .
The sixth: Commanding the impossible is folly and frivolity. If it were permissible for the Law to come with it in some cases, it would be permissible for it to come with it in the remaining cases. Then it would necessarily be not impossible for the Exalted Creator to show miracles at the hands of liars, and for the statements of books containing lies and misguidances not to be impossible. On this assumption, there remains no confidence in the validity of the prophethood of the prophets (peace be upon them), nor in the validity of the Quran. Rather, it is possible that it is all lies. And when that is invalid, we know that knowledge of non-belief, and report about non-belief, do not necessitate the conversion of belief into something impossible.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This is an argument from the disastrous consequences of accepting that God can command the impossible (al-muḥāl). If one allows that God can command a person to do what is impossible (like believing despite foreknowledge of unbelief), then by extension, one could allow any absurdity in revelation. This would lead to the possibility that false prophets could perform miracles, that scriptures could be false, and no trust would remain in prophethood or the Quran. Since this is absurd (the falsity of prophethood is impossible), we conclude that God commanding the impossible is also false, and therefore divine knowledge of unbelief does not make belief impossible.

Key Terms
الأمر بالمحال
العبث
السفه
المعجزات
الكاذبين
الكتب
النبوة
القرآن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a single instance of commanding the impossible (e.g., commanding the one known to disbelieve) is an exception that does not affect the rest of revelation.
Correction: The proof shows that if one instance is allowed, by the same logic any absurdity in revelation becomes possible, thereby destroying all certainty. Therefore no exception is possible.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth argument, if commanding the impossible were allowed in some cases, what would be the consequence?
A) Humans would be unable to obey God.
B) Miracles could be performed by liars, and no revelation would be trustworthy.
C) God's power would be limited.
D) Faith would become necessary.

Key Takeaway
The sixth proof shows that allowing the impossible in divine commands would undermine all certainty in revelation, so it is impossible, and divine foreknowledge does not make belief impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever doubted the reliability of the Quran or the authenticity of prophethood because of the apparent contradiction between divine knowledge and human choice? How does the reductio argument in this lesson confront that doubt and call you to trust in the consistency of Allah's commands?
?
Think of a command from Allah that you find extremely difficult—perhaps in your own life you question whether you can truly obey it. Does the impossibility arise from your own weakness, or do you assume Allah has commanded something beyond your ability? How does this proof demand that you re-examine that assumption and take responsibility for your response?
?
If you truly accept that Allah never commands what is impossible, how must that change your reaction when you face a challenging obligation? Will you now see it as a call to action rather than an excuse for failure? What specific step will you take today to act on that conviction?

The Seventh Proof: Absurd Consequences of Commanding the Impossible
Author's seventh proof using absurd examples to refute commanding the impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh proof against commanding the impossible, which uses absurd examples to show that God's command to the unbeliever is not impossible.

Core Idea
Commanding the impossible would lead to absurdities like ordering a blind person to dot the Quran.

This proof employs a reductio ad absurdum. If commanding the impossible were permissible, then even the most absurd commands—like telling a blind person to dot the Quran—would be allowed. Since reason rejects such absurdities, commanding the impossible is not permissible. The proof then uses consensus (ijmāʿ) to confirm that God commands those He knows will not believe, showing that His foreknowledge does not make belief impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the examples and the logical flow.
The examples are deliberately extreme to highlight absurdity.
2
Identify the logical structure
Notice the conditional: 'If it were permissible... then it would be permissible...' followed by specific absurd commands. Then the negation: 'Since none of that is permissible... we know...'
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Examine the three examples
The author gives three examples: a blind person dotting the Quran, a paralyzed person flying, and a bound person being blamed for not flying. Each is clearly impossible.
The examples are chosen to be self-evidently impossible to any rational person.
4
Understand the role of consensus
The author then invokes consensus that God commanded those He knew would not believe. This shows that the command was not impossible, just foreknown.
Consensus (ijmāʿ) is a strong proof in Islamic theology.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that God's knowledge of non-belief does not make the act of belief impossible. The command remains possible.
This refutes the idea that foreknowledge entails impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : لو جاز ورود الأمر بالمحال في هذه الصورة ، لجاز ورود أمر الأعمى بنقط المصحف ، وورود أمر الزمن بالطيران في الهواء ، وأن يقال لمن قيد يداه ورجلاه ، وألقى من شاهق جبل : لم لم يطر إلى الفوق ؟ ولما لم يجز شيء من ذلك في العقول ، علمنا : أنه لا يجوز ورود الأمر بالمحال . ثم إنا أجمعنا على أن الله تعالى أمر من علم منه أنه لا يؤمن . فدل ذلك على أن العلم بالعدم ، لا يدل على الامتناع .
The seventh: If it were permissible for the command of the impossible to occur in this form, then it would be permissible for the command of a blind person to dot the Quran [with diacritical points], and the command of a paralyzed person to fly in the air, and one could say to someone whose hands and feet are tied and is thrown from a mountain peak: 'Why did you not fly upwards?' And since none of that is permissible in the intellects, we know that the command of the impossible is not permissible. Then we have consensus that God Almighty commanded those from whom He knew they would not believe. So that indicates that knowledge of non-existence does not indicate impossibility.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This is a reductio ad absurdum using clear examples. If commanding the impossible were allowed in the case of commanding the unbeliever to believe despite knowledge of unbelief, then equally absurd commands would be allowed: commanding a blind person to dot the Quran, commanding a paralyzed person (zamīn) to fly in air, or rebuking a bound person thrown from a mountain for not flying. These are obviously impossible and not permitted by reason. Since we have consensus (ijmāʿ) that God did command those He knew would not believe, it follows that God's knowledge of non-belief does not make belief impossible, because the command is not a command of the impossible.

Key Terms
الأمر بالمحال
الأعمى
الزمن
نقط المصحف
الطيران
القيد
العقول
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's command to the unbeliever is a command of the impossible because God knows they will not believe.
Correction: The proof shows that such a command is not impossible; it only seems so if one confuses foreknowledge with impossibility. The examples demonstrate that true impossibilities are things like a blind person dotting the Quran, which are clearly irrational.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh argument, if commanding the impossible were permissible, what absurd command would be valid?
A) Commanding a bird to stop flying.
B) Commanding a blind person to dot the Quran.
C) Commanding a rich person to give charity.
D) Commanding a healthy person to walk.

Key Takeaway
The seventh proof uses absurd examples to show that commanding the impossible leads to irrationality, and God's foreknowledge of unbelief does not make the command impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever made excuses for not obeying a command of Allah because you thought it was too difficult or impossible for you? How does this lesson challenge that assumption?
?
When you face a command of Allah that seems beyond your ability, do you truly believe that Allah does not command the impossible, and that He knows your capacity? What would change in your life if you lived with that certainty?
?
Consider one area where you have given up or stopped trying to obey Allah because you thought it was impossible for you to succeed. Based on this lesson, what is the truth about that command, and what step will you take today to respond to it?

The Eighth Proof: Commanding the Impossible Leads to Mockery
Eighth proof using reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the eighth proof uses a reductio ad absurdum to show that commanding the impossible would lead to mockery of religion, thus proving that God does not command the impossible.

Core Idea
If commanding the impossible were allowed, religion would become absurd.

This lesson presents the eighth and final proof in the series. It shows that allowing the impossible to be commanded would lead to consequences that are clearly unworthy of God—such as sending prophets to inanimate objects. This absurdity demonstrates that God's commands are never impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Notice the logical flow: a conditional statement leading to an absurd result.
The passage is very concise; every word matters.
2
Identify the reductio structure
The argument begins with a hypothesis (if impossible commands allowed) and derives an unacceptable consequence (prophets to inanimate objects). This consequence is labelled as mockery. Recognise this as a reductio ad absurdum.
The absurdity is what makes the proof effective.
3
Understand the theological conclusion
Since the consequence is false (God does not mock), the original hypothesis must be false. Therefore, commanding the impossible is not permissible. This reinforces that God's commands are always possible for the one commanded.
This connects to the broader discussion of divine justice and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن : لو جاز ورود الأمر بالمحال ، لجاز بعثة الأنبياء الى الجمادات ، وإنزال الكتب عليها ، وإنزال الملائكة عليها ، لتبليغ التكاليف إليها ، حالاً حال . ومعلوم أن ذلك سخرية ، وتلاعب بالدين .
The eighth: If it were permissible for the command of the impossible to occur, then it would be permissible to send prophets to inanimate objects, and to send down books upon them, and to send angels upon them, to convey obligations to them, case by case. And it is known that this is mockery and playing with religion.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This is a brief argument completing the reductio. If commanding the impossible is allowed, then God could send prophets to inanimate objects (jamādāt), send books and angels to them to convey religious obligations. That would be mockery (sukhriyya) and playing with religion (talāʿub bi-dīn). Since that is impossible (God is not mocking), commanding the impossible is false. Hence, God's command to the unbeliever is not a command of the impossible.

Key Terms
الأمر بالمحال
بعثة الأنبياء
الجمادات
الكتب
الملائكة
التكاليف
السخرية
تلاعب بالدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that because God is all-powerful, He could command anything, even the impossible, and that this proof limits His power.
Correction: The proof does not limit God's power; it shows that commanding the impossible would lead to absurdity, which is incompatible with God's wisdom. God's power is exercised wisely, not arbitrarily.

Checkpoint
What consequence of commanding the impossible does the eighth argument present?
A) Humans would be forced to obey.
B) Prophets could be sent to inanimate objects, which is mockery.
C) Angels would become unnecessary.
D) Revelation would be clearer.

Key Takeaway
The eighth proof shows that God's commands are always possible because otherwise religion would be absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever treated a command of Allah as if it were impossible for you, thus making it a mockery in your heart? What is one command you have labeled 'too hard' that you need to recommit to today?
?
If sending prophets to rocks is absurd, how much more absurd is it to claim that Allah commands something without purpose? How does this truth change the way you approach every obligation in your life—from prayer to charity?
?
When you hear about someone abandoning a religious duty because they find it 'unreasonable,' do you recognize that attitude as a form of playing with religion? What will you do this week to defend the seriousness of Allah's commands?

Ninth Proof: Knowledge Does Not Necessitate
Author's ninth proof that knowledge cannot necessitate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ninth proof that divine knowledge does not necessitate the existence of things, and how this distinguishes Islamic theology from the philosophers' view of necessary emanation.

Core Idea
If knowledge necessitated, actions would not prove God's will and power.

This lesson presents a concise proof from the author to refute the philosophers who claim that God's knowledge alone necessitates the existence of things. The argument shows that if knowledge were sufficient, divine will and power would be redundant, and the existence of contingent actions would no longer point to God's attributes. Let's examine the logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the author's concise argument.
Notice the conditional structure: 'If... then...'
2
Identify the conditional
The proof is structured as: if knowledge necessitated, then knowledge alone would suffice, eliminating the need for will and power.
3
Unpack the consequence
If will and power are redundant, then the occurrence of actions would no longer indicate that God is powerful and willing.
4
Connect to the philosophers
The argument targets the philosophers (falāsifa) who held that God is a necessary emanator (al-mūjib).
Their view is that knowledge necessitates, which the author refutes.
5
Absorb the conclusion
Since we know from revelation and reason that God is powerful and willful, knowledge cannot necessitate. This proof safeguards divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع : إن العلم بوجود الشيء ، لو اقتضى وجوبه ، لأغنى هذا العلم عن الإرادة والقدرة ، فوجب أن لا تدل حدوث الأفعال على كونه تعالى قادراً مريدا . وذلك قول الفلاسفة القائلين بالموجب .
The ninth: If knowledge of the existence of a thing necessitated its being obligatory, then this knowledge would dispense with [divine] will and power. Then it would necessarily follow that the occurrence of actions would not indicate that He (Exalted) is powerful and willing. And that is the saying of the philosophers who affirm necessary emanation.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This is a proof from divine attributes. If God's knowledge of a thing's existence made that thing necessary (wājib), then knowledge alone would be sufficient, eliminating the need for divine will (irāda) and power (qudra). Consequently, the fact that actions occur (ḥudūth al-afʿāl) would no longer prove that God is powerful and willing—a role attributed to the philosophers (al-falāsifa) who hold that God is a necessary emanator (al-mūjib). Since we know from revelation and reason that God is powerful and willful, knowledge cannot necessitate.

Key Terms
العلم
الوجوب
الإرادة
القدرة
حدوث الأفعال
الفلسفة
الموجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some confuse divine knowledge with causal necessity, thinking that if God knows something will happen, it must happen necessarily, leaving no role for will and power.
Correction: This proof shows that if knowledge necessitated, then will and power would be meaningless, and creation would not indicate God's attributes. Since we know God is powerful and willing, knowledge does not necessitate.

Checkpoint
According to the ninth argument, if knowledge made things necessary, what would be the consequence for proving God's attributes?
A) God's power would be unlimited.
B) The occurrence of actions would not prove God's power and will.
C) Human responsibility would increase.
D) Prophethood would be easier.

Key Takeaway
The ninth proof shows that if divine knowledge necessitated actions, then actions would not prove God's power and will, contradicting the philosophers' view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on God's knowledge of your actions, do you sometimes feel that your choices are predetermined because God already knows them? How does this proof that knowledge does not necessitate your actions challenge that feeling, and what difference does that make for how you approach making a difficult decision today?
?
The philosophers' view that God's knowledge necessitates existence leads to a denial of God's will and power. Is there an area of your life where you have inadvertently accepted a similar logic—believing that God's 'knowing' your problems means they must remain as they are, without His power to change them? What step will you take today to affirm both His knowledge and His power in that area?
?
This proof shows that God's attributes of knowledge, will, and power are distinct and each necessary for creation. In your worship, do you sometimes address God as 'All-Knowing' but neglect to ask Him to will and empower change? How will you intentionally praise and rely on all three attributes today?

Divine Knowledge Does Not Prevent Faith
The author refutes the sixth objection and concludes that divine knowledge does not prevent faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine knowledge does not prevent faith and how the author refutes the sixth objection using a Quranic verse.

Core Idea
God's knowledge does not create impossibility—it merely informs.

The sixth objection from the Mu'tazila argues that if God commanded faith while knowing it would never happen, He would be unjust. The author counters by citing the Quranic verse that praises God as the best Protector and Helper, showing that such a command would contradict that praise. This lesson unpacks that argument and its conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation without analysis to get the author's rhythm.
Notice the use of the Quranic verse at the beginning.
2
Understand the objection and refutation
Identify the conditional argument: If God commanded faith with an impediment, He would not be excellent. Then see the absurdity that results.
The conclusion is that divine knowledge does not make faith impossible.
3
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider how this argument shows that divine knowledge is about the future, not a cause of events.
This refutes the idea that foreknowledge equals predestination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : إنه تعالى قال : ( فنعم المولى ونعم النصير ) ولو أنه أمر بالإيمان ، مع حصول المانع منه : لما كان نعم المولى ونعم النصير، بل كان بئس المولى وبئس النصير . ومعلوم : أن ذلك : كفر . فثبت بهذه الوجوه : أن علم الله بعدم الإيمان ، وخبره عن عدم الإيمان : لا يدل على كون ذلك الإيمان ممتنع الحصول .
The sixth: He, exalted, said: 'So excellent a Protector and excellent a Helper' (Quran 22:78). And if He had commanded faith while there is an impediment to it, then He would not be an excellent Protector and excellent Helper, but rather an evil Protector and evil Helper. And it is known that that is disbelief. So it is established by these points that God's knowledge of the absence of faith and His report about the absence of faith do not indicate that such faith is impossible to obtain.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth objection raised by the Mu'tazila against the argument that divine knowledge of disbelief would prevent faith. The author quotes the Quranic verse 22:78 to argue that if God commanded faith while there was an impediment (i.e., His knowledge that it would not happen), then God would not be praised as an excellent Protector and Helper; rather, He would be condemned. Since such condemnation is disbelief, the original position must be false. The author concludes that divine knowledge of disbelief does not imply the impossibility of faith.

Key Terms
المولى
النصير
المانع
علم الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge of disbelief makes faith impossible.
Correction: The passage shows that God's knowledge does not make faith impossible, only that it will not occur.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue in the sixth objection?
A) That God's knowledge of disbelief proves faith is impossible
B) That if God commanded faith despite His knowledge of disbelief, He would not be an excellent Protector
C) That the Quranic verse proves faith is mandatory
D) That God's report of disbelief prevents faith

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge about a lack of faith does not make faith impossible; it merely informs about its future non-occurrence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
