Two Mu'tazili Methods on Divine Knowledge and Human Action
The author presents two Mu'tazili methods for reconciling divine knowledge with human free will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main Mu'tazili arguments that reconcile divine foreknowledge with human free will.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila defend free will by redefining how divine knowledge relates to actual events.

The Mu'tazila faced a dilemma: if God knows everything in advance, how can humans act freely? This passage presents two sophisticated answers from leading Mu'tazili scholars. As you read, note how each method avoids attributing change or ignorance to God while preserving human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the two methods.
Pay attention to the names of the scholars associated with each method.
2
Identify the two methods
Notice that the passage explicitly presents two distinct methods. Identify the key proponents of each and the core principle they advocate.
First method: Abu 'Ali, Abu Hashim, al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar. Second method: al-Ka'bi, Abu al-Husayn al-Basri.
3
Understand the first method's logic
The first method argues against the notion that God's knowledge would turn into ignorance if a contrary event occurred. It claims this attaches the impossible (knowledge turning to ignorance) to the possible (the event occurring), which is an error.
They also say one should refrain from both affirming and denying the transformation.
4
Understand the second method's principle
The second method states that knowledge follows the known. So if the servant chooses faith, God's knowledge from eternity is of faith; if disbelief, then it is of disbelief. This supposes a different knowledge, not a change.
This avoids the problem of God's knowledge changing or becoming ignorant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب على سبيل التفصيل : فاعلم : أن للمعتزلة فيه طريقين : الأول : طريقة « أبي علي » و « أبي هاشم » و « القاضي عبد الجبار » إذ قالوا : إن قول القائل : لو وقع خلاف معلوم الله ، لانقلب علم الله جهلاً . قالوا : خطأ من يقول : إله ينقلب علمه جهلا . لأن هذا تعليق المحال بالممكن . فإن انقلاب علم الله جهلاً محال . ووقوع ما علم الله أنه لا يقع ممكن . فقولنا : لو وقع ما علم الله أنه لا يقع تعليق المحال بالممكن فكان خطأ . وخطأ أيضاً : قبول من يقول : إنه لا ينقلب جهلاً . ولكن يجب الإمساك عن القولين . الثاني : طريقة « الكعبي » واختيار « أبي الحسين البصري » وهو أن العلم تبع للمعلوم . فإذا فرضنا أن الصادر من العبد هو الإيمان ، كان الحاصل في أزل الله تعالى هو العلم بوجود الإيمان . وإذا فرضنا أن الواقع منه هو عدم الإيمان ، كان الحاصل في الأزل هو العلم بعدم الإيمان . فهذا فرض علم بدلاً عن علم آخر ، لا أنه ينقلب جهلا . فهذان الجوابان هما اللذان عليهما اعتماد المعتزلة . والله أعلم.
As for the detailed response: Know that the Mu'tazila have two methods regarding this. The first: the method of Abu 'Ali, Abu Hashim, and al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar, for they said: The statement of one who says: 'If something contrary to God's knowledge were to occur, then God's knowledge would turn into ignorance' is an error. For this is an attachment of the impossible to the possible. For the transformation of God's knowledge into ignorance is impossible. And the occurrence of what God knows will not occur is possible. So our statement: 'If what God knows will not occur were to occur' is attaching the impossible to the possible, and thus it is an error. It is also an error to accept that it does not turn into ignorance, but one must abstain from both statements. The second: the method of al-Ka'bi and the choice of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, which is that knowledge follows the known. So if we suppose that what issues from the servant is faith, then what obtains in God's pre-eternity is knowledge of the existence of faith. And if we suppose that what actually occurs from him is the absence of faith, then what obtains in pre-eternity is knowledge of the absence of faith. This is a supposition of one knowledge instead of another knowledge, not a transformation into ignorance. These two answers are the ones upon which the Mu'tazila rely. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage details two Mu'tazili responses to the problem of divine knowledge and human action. The first method, attributed to Abu 'Ali, Abu Hashim, and al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar, argues that it is a mistake to say that if something contrary to God's knowledge were to occur, His knowledge would turn into ignorance. They claim that this 'if' attaches something impossible (knowledge turning to ignorance) to something possible (the occurrence of the known event). They say both the statement that it would turn into ignorance and the statement that it would not are wrong; one should refrain from both. The second method, from al-Ka'bi and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, holds that knowledge is dependent on the known (العلم تبع للمعلوم). So if we suppose the servant performs faith, then from eternity God knows that faith occurs; if we suppose he performs disbelief, then from eternity God knows that disbelief occurs. This is not a change in knowledge but a different knowledge corresponding to a different actuality. The author notes that these are the main Mu'tazili positions.

Key Terms
العلم
المعلوم
انقلاب
المحال
الممكن
تبع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila deny divine foreknowledge.
Correction: The passage shows they affirm foreknowledge but argue it is dependent on actual events, avoiding any change or ignorance in God.

Checkpoint
According to the first Mu'tazili method, what is the error in the statement 'If something contrary to God's knowledge occurs, His knowledge turns to ignorance'?
A) It implies God is ignorant.
B) It attaches the impossible (knowledge turning to ignorance) to the possible (the event occurring).
C) It denies human free will.
D) It contradicts the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila reconcile divine foreknowledge and human free will either by denying that a possible event can make God's knowledge impossible, or by making knowledge dependent on the actual outcome.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
Which of these two Mu'tazili methods resonates more with your own understanding of divine foreknowledge and human free will? And what does your preference reveal about your own need to control outcomes versus surrendering to God's eternal decree?

The Author's First Response: Impossibility of Contradiction
The author presents the first aspect of his response: reaffirming the proof and its self-evident premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends his argument that divine knowledge of disbelief prevents the occurrence of faith, based on the principle that knowledge must correspond to reality.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge and its object cannot contradict; they must match.

The author now responds to the Mu'tazili objections that divine knowledge does not prevent faith. He gives two aspects. In this lesson, we examine the first aspect: his original proof that knowledge of the absence of faith requires the absence of faith, and that the simultaneous existence of faith would be a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once, noting the two aspects introduced. Focus on the first aspect where the author reaffirms his proof.
The passage contains a self-evident premise and a conclusion about contradiction.
2
Understand the key premise
The author states that knowledge of the absence of faith only occurs when there is absence, because knowledge must correspond to the known. This is called a self-evident premise.
The Arabic term for self-evident is 'مقدمة بديهية'.
3
Grasp the conclusion about contradiction
If faith existed while God knows its absence, it would require the conjunction of two contradictories, which is impossible. The author asserts this is beyond doubt.
This is the core of the first response: the logical impossibility of contradiction.

God's Knowledge of Disbelief Prevents Belief
First general refutation of Mu'tazilite objections on divine knowledge and belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical necessity that God's knowledge of disbelief rules out the occurrence of belief, based on the axioms of correspondence and non-contradiction.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of disbelief makes belief impossible.

In this passage, the author defends his earlier proof that God's knowledge of a person's disbelief prevents that person from ever believing. He argues from two self-evident principles: knowledge must match reality, and opposites cannot coexist. The Mu'tazilite objections, he concludes, cannot overturn this simple logic. Let's walk through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage aloud
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for the argument's flow. Then read the English translation. Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'فإن' and 'لزم'.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two key premises
Find the two premises the author uses: (1) knowledge requires correspondence to its object, (2) two opposites cannot coexist. Notice how he states each as self-evident ('بديهية', 'لا مجال للعقل في أن يتشكك').
The first premise is about knowledge; the second about impossibility of contradiction.
3
Trace the conclusion
Given the two premises, the author concludes that God's knowledge of disbelief makes the occurrence of belief impossible. Understand why this follows: if belief occurred, knowledge would not correspond (violating premise 1) and two opposites would coexist (violating premise 2).
The conclusion is that the Mu'tazilite objections are invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أما الوجوه التي استدلوا بها على أن علم الله بعدم الإيمان وإخباره عن عدم الإيمان : لا يمنع من وجود الإيمان فالجواب عنها من وجهين : الأول : إن الدليل الذي ذكرناه في كونه مانعـاً منه وهو دليل تلك المقدمات : صحيح . أما التركيب فكيف يقدح ما ذكر نموه في صحته ؟ وبيانه : وهو أنا قلنا : إن العلم بعدم الإيمان ، لا يحصل إلا عند عدم الإيمان . فهذه مقدمة بديهية . لأن شرط العلم : أن يكون مطابقاً للمعلوم . فالعلم بعدم الإيمان ، لا يكون علماً ، إلا إذا كان مطابقاً . ولا يكون إلا إذا حصل عدم الإيمان . فثبت : أن العلم بعدم الإيمان، لا يحصل إلا عند عدم الإيمان . ثم نقول : لو حصل وجود الإيمان مع العلم بعدم الإيمان ، لزم اجتماع النقيضين ، وهذا كلام ، لا مجال للعقل في أن يتشكك فيه . وإذا كان الأمر كما ذكرنا ، لم تكن الوجوه التي ذكروها قادحة في صحة هذا الكلام البنة
The answer: As for the aspects they used as evidence that God's knowledge of disbelief and His informing about non-occurrence of faith do not prevent the occurrence of faith, the answer to them is from two aspects. The first: Indeed the proof we mentioned concerning its being preventive [of faith] — and it is the proof of those premises — is sound. As for the composition, how can what they mentioned [unclear] detract from its correctness? Its explanation: We said that knowledge of disbelief does not obtain except when there is no belief. This is an axiomatic premise, because the condition of knowledge is that it correspond to the known. So knowledge of disbelief is not knowledge unless it is corresponding, and it is not corresponding unless there occurs non-belief. Thus it is established that knowledge of disbelief does not obtain except when there is no belief. Then we say: If the occurrence of belief occurred together with knowledge of disbelief, the two opposites would coexist, and this is a statement that reason has no room to doubt. And if the matter is as we have described, the aspects they mentioned are in no way conclusive against the correctness of this statement.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents the first general refutation of the Mu'tazilite objections. The author states that the proof he earlier established — that God's knowledge of disbelief prevents belief — is sound. He explains that knowledge must correspond to its object; therefore knowledge of a lack of belief only occurs when that lack exists. To suppose the opposite (that belief could occur despite God's knowledge of its absence) would entail the impossible conjunction of two opposites. Hence the Mu'tazilite objections do not undermine this proof. The argument is structured as a chain of self-evident premises leading to the conclusion that God's knowledge of disbelief makes belief impossible.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's knowledge of disbelief causes the disbelief, making God responsible for human unbelief.
Correction: The argument does not claim causality; it merely states that the impossibility of contradiction prevents belief from coexisting with knowledge of its absence. God's knowledge is not the cause but the logical correlate of the fact.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of disbelief logically prevents belief because knowledge must match reality and opposites cannot coexist.

God's Knowledge Does Not Supersede His Power
The author refutes the first objection to his argument about divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's knowledge of an event does not eliminate the need for His power and will.

Core Idea
Knowledge follows the known; it does not replace the cause.

In this passage, the author addresses an important objection to the role of divine knowledge. The objection claims that if God's knowledge of disbelief prevents belief, then God would be unable to act. The author clarifies that knowledge is not a substitute for power and will; rather, it is dependent on the causal process.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author begins with a statement of method (answering each objection in detail).
2
Identify the objection
The objection is that if God's knowledge of disbelief prevents belief, then God cannot do anything at all. Find where the author states this objection.
The objection is presented in the phrase 'يلزم أن لا يقدر الله تعالى على شيء أصلا'.
3
Analyse the refutation
The author rejects the premise and explains that knowledge follows the known. The known event occurs only through its causes (power and will). Therefore, knowledge does not eliminate the need for these causes.
Key phrase: 'العلم يتبع المعلوم'.
4
Reflect on the analogy
The author gives the example of a body's motion: God's knowledge that the body moves due to a cause does not make the cause unnecessary. Understand how this parallels the main argument.
The analogy reinforces that knowledge is not a substitute for causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني : أن نجيب عن كل واحد من تلك الوجوه ، على التفصيل . أما السؤال الأول: وهو قوله: « يلزم أن لا يقدر الله تعالى على شيء أصلا، قلنا: لا نسلم. وذلك لأن الله تعالى إنما يعلم وقوع ذلك الممكن، لو كان هو في نفسه واقعاً. لأن العلم يتبع المعلوم، ووقوع ذلك الممكن. لا بد وأن يكون لأجل مؤثر يؤثر فيه، وهو القدرة والإرادة. فالعلم إنما تعلق بوقوعه، لأنه علم أنه واقع باتباع القدرة والإرادة. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، فكيف يعقل أن يقال: إن هذا العلم يغنيه عن القدرة والإرادة؟ ومثاله: أن علمه تعالى، لما تعلق بأن الجسم إنما تحرك لقيام معنى، به يوجب المتحركية، لم يكن هذا مغنياً لتلك المتحركية عن علتها. فكذا [ههنا].
And the second aspect: that we answer each one of those aspects in detail. As for the first question: which is his statement: 'It would necessitate that God Most High is not able to do anything at all,' we say: we do not accept that. That is because God Most High only knows the occurrence of that possible [thing] were it in itself occurring. For knowledge follows the known, and the occurrence of that possible [thing] must be due to an influence that influences it, which is power and will. So knowledge attaches to its occurrence only because He knows it occurs through the following of power and will. And if the matter is so, how can it be reasonable to say that this knowledge suffices Him from power and will? Its example: His knowledge Most High, since it attaches that the body moves only because of a meaning that necessitates motility, this did not suffice that motility from its cause. So too here.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents the second aspect of the author's response: a detailed refutation of each objection. The first objection claimed that if God's knowledge of disbelief prevents belief, then God would become unable to do anything. The author rejects this premise, explaining that God's knowledge is not a substitute for power and will. Knowledge follows the known: God knows that something will occur if and when it is brought about by His power and will. Therefore, knowledge does not eliminate the need for causal factors. He gives the analogy of a body's motion: God's knowledge that the body moves due to a cause does not eliminate the cause itself.

Key Terms
العلم
القدرة
الإرادة
الممكن
المعلوم
المتحركية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge of something makes it happen without the need for His power and will.
Correction: Knowledge follows the known; it is dependent on the event occurring through its proper causes, including power and will.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the claim that his argument would make God unable to act?
A) He denies that God's knowledge prevents anything.
B) He explains that knowledge follows the known and does not replace the need for power and will.
C) He argues that God's power is unlimited.
D) He concedes but modifies the argument.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge does not replace His power and will; rather, it is dependent on them as causes of events.

Knowledge Does Not Replace Power and Will: An Analogy
The author proves divine knowledge does not supplant power and will by analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine knowledge (ʿilm) does not eliminate the need for divine power (qudra) and will (irāda), and how an analogy from physical causation illustrates this.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is not the cause; power and will are.

This passage tackles a common theological confusion: if God knows everything, does He still need power and will to act? The author uses a logical analogy to show that knowledge and causation are distinct. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct beliefs about God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage in Arabic once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the rhetorical question and the analogy.
The Arabic uses the term 'المؤثر' meaning 'the influencer'.
2
Identify the key terms
Notice the roles of 'العلم' (knowledge), 'القدرة' (power), 'الإرادة' (will), and 'المؤثر' (influencer).
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Follow the analogy
The author compares divine knowledge to knowledge of a body's movement due to a cause. See how the analogy works: knowledge of the cause does not replace the cause itself.
The analogy is: just as knowledge of movement does not make the cause unnecessary, so divine knowledge does not make power and will unnecessary.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the rhetorical question 'how can it be reasonable to say that this knowledge suffices it from power and will?' and why it undermines the opposing view.
The conclusion 'so likewise here' ties the analogy back to divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأن يكون لأجل مؤثر يؤثر فيه، وهو القدرة والإرادة. فالعلم إنما تعلق بوقوعه، لأنه علم أنه واقع باتباع القدرة والإرادة. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، فكيف يعقل أن يقال: إن هذا العلم يغنيه عن القدرة والإرادة؟ ومثاله: أن علمه تعالى، لما تعلق بأن الجسم إنما تحرك لقيام معنى، به يوجب المتحركية، لم يكن هذا مغنياً لتلك المتحركية عن علتها. فكذا ههنا.
And that it be due to an influencer that influences it, which is power and will. For knowledge only relates to its occurrence because it knows that it occurs through the following of power and will. If the matter is so, how can it be reasonable to say that this knowledge suffices it from power and will? An example: His knowledge, exalted, when it related that the body moves only due to the establishment of a meaning that necessitates mobility, this did not dispense that mobility from its cause. So likewise here.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy that divine knowledge (ʿilm) does not obviate the need for power (qudra) and will (irāda). The author first asserts that an event's occurrence depends on an influencer, namely power and will. Knowledge then relates to the event because it knows it will happen through that power and will. The rhetorical question challenges the notion that knowledge alone suffices. The analogy illustrates: just as knowledge that a body moves due to a specific cause does not eliminate that cause, so divine knowledge does not replace the causal efficacy of qudra and irāda. The conclusion is 'likewise here'.

Key Terms
العلم
القدرة
الإرادة
المؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's foreknowledge alone makes events necessary, so power and will are redundant.
Correction: The passage argues that knowledge is not the cause; power and will are the true influencers.

Checkpoint
What does the author use as an analogy to show that knowledge does not replace power and will?
A) Knowledge of a body's movement due to a meaning that necessitates mobility
B) The relationship between fire and burning
C) The necessity of creation
D) The impossibility of change in God

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge is not the cause of events; power and will are the true influencers, and knowledge merely reflects that causation.

Refutation of the First Answer: Knowledge Cannot Become Ignorance
Sanusi refutes the first answer by showing it leads to absurdity or is irrelevant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi rejects the opponents' first response and how the principle 'what leads to the impossible is impossible' is applied to defend God's knowledge from change.

Core Idea
If God's knowledge were contradicted, it would be ignorance—impossible for God, so the antecedent is impossible.

In this passage, Sanusi tackles the first objection against his proof that God's knowledge of future events cannot change. The opponents claim that both saying it indicates change and saying it does not are wrong. Sanusi dissects their statement, showing it either implies an absurdity or is irrelevant to his argument. He then restates his own proof: if God knows a person will not believe but that person actually believes, God's knowledge would not match reality—that is ignorance. Since ignorance is impossible for God, the scenario that leads to it must also be impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the opponents' claim and Sanusi's immediate critique.
2
Identify the two possible meanings of the opponents' answer
Sanusi considers two interpretations: either they claim both contradictories are false, or they claim one is true but they do not verbalize it. Reject the first as absurd, concede the second as irrelevant.
3
Understand Sanusi's restated proof
Sanusi repeats his original argument: if God knows X will not happen, but X happens, then His knowledge does not conform to reality—which is exactly ignorance. This shows that the assumption that X happens would entail ignorance for God.
Key phrase: 'no meaning of ignorance except this'.
4
Apply the principle 'what leads to the impossible is impossible'
Since ignorance is impossible for God, the supposition that leads to it (the person believing contrary to God's knowledge) must also be impossible. This is the core logical move.
This principle is a standard tool in kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : جوابهم الأول . وهو قولهم : ( خطأ قول من يقول : إن وقوع ما علم الله أنه لا يقع : يدل على انقلاب علم الله جهلا ، وخطأ قول من يقول : إنه لا يدل ، : فضعيف . وذلك لأنهم إن أرادوا أن كلا النقيضين باطل في نفس الأمر . فهذا لا يقوله عاقل . وإن أرادوا به أن أحدهما حق ، ولكن لا ينطقون به [ ولا يتلفظون به (٤)] فهذا مسلم . إلا أن الزامنا غير مبني على نطقهم ، ولا على لفظهم وعبارتهم . فإنا لما بينا أنه تعالى إذا علم أنه لا يؤمن فلو آمن ذلك الشخص . فإن على هذا التقدير ، لم يكن علمه مطابقاً للمعلوم ولا معنى للجهل إلا هذا . فثبت منه : أنه يلزم انقلاب علم الله جهلا . ولما كان هذا محالاً ، وجب أن يكون ذلك حالاً لأن المؤدي إلى المحال محال
We say: Their first answer—which is their statement: 'The opinion of one who says that the occurrence of what God knows will not occur indicates the transformation of God's knowledge into ignorance is wrong, and the opinion of one who says it does not indicate is wrong'—is weak. That is because if they mean that both opposites are false in themselves, no sane person would say that. And if they mean that one of them is true but they do not utter it [nor express it verbally], this is conceded. However, our argument is not based on their utterance or their words and expressions. For when we have shown that if He, exalted, knows that he will not believe, then if that person were to believe, on this supposition His knowledge would not be in conformity with the known, and there is no meaning of ignorance except this. So it is established from this that the transformation of God's knowledge into ignorance would be entailed. And since this is impossible, that situation must be impossible, because what leads to the impossible is impossible.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the opponents' first response. Sanusi examines their claim that both saying it indicates change in God's knowledge and saying it does not are wrong. He dissects their statement: if they mean both contradictories are false, that is absurd; if they mean one is true but they do not verbalize it, that is granted but irrelevant. His own argument does not rely on their wording. He then restates his proof: if God knows a person will not believe, but that person actually believes, then God's knowledge would not match reality—and that is precisely ignorance. Since ignorance is impossible for God, the supposition that the person believes is impossible. The argument is that an impossible consequence proves the antecedent impossible.

مطابقة العلم للمعلوم

Correction: Sanusi shows the answer either leads to the absurdity of both opposites being false, or it is irrelevant because his argument does not depend on their wording.

Checkpoint

B) Because they refuse to speak about it.

D) Because they have no answer at all.

Key Takeaway

Refuting the Conditional Argument
Sanusi refutes the second answer by showing the conditional does not avoid the conclusion that God's knowledge would change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a conditional proposition does not avoid the conclusion that God's knowledge would be subject to change if human actions were not predetermined.

Core Idea
Even a conditional argument forces a definite knowledge in God, making change impossible.

Sanusi continues his refutation by addressing the opponents' second answer, which claimed that the conditional 'If X, then God would know X' avoids the problem. In this passage, he demonstrates that despite the conditional form, one of the two possible knowledges must be actual, and any change in the known would turn God's knowledge into ignorance—an impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the full argument. Notice the conditional structure and Sanusi's response.
The passage is dense; take time to follow each step.
2
Understand the conditional form
The opponents use a conditional statement: 'If Zayd believes, God knows; if not, God knows not.' Sanusi agrees it is conditional, but argues that does not eliminate the problem.
A conditional does not imply both alternatives are possible simultaneously; one must be actual.
3
Identify the two key arguments
First argument: One of the two knowledges (that Zayd will believe or not) is actual; if the opposite of that known occurs, God's knowledge becomes ignorance. Second argument: If the known does not occur in eternity, then the eternal knowledge would turn into ignorance at that eternal moment, implying change in the past.
Both arguments lead to impossibilities.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Sanusi concludes that these answers are extremely weak and his original proof stands unrefuted. Consider why the conditional cannot rescue the opponents' position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما جوابهم الثاني : فضعيف أيضاً : لأن قولنا : لو كان الداخل في الوجود هو إيمان « زيد ، لعلم الله في الأزل أنه يؤمن . ولو كان الداخل عـدم إيمانه ، لعلم الله في الأزل أنه لا يؤمن . فهذا قضية شرطية . لأن قولنا : لو كان كذا ، لكان كذا : لا شك أنه قضية شرطية. لكنا نقول : إنه لا بد مع حصول هذه القضية الشرطية ، أنه تعالى [ كان (١) ] عالماً في الأزل بأنه يؤمن ، أو كان عالماً بأنه (۲) ] لا يؤمن . فثبت : أن أحد هذين العلمين حاصل . وإذا كان ذلك حاصلاً ، امتنع أن يقع نقيض ذلك المعلوم ، وإلا لزم أن يصير علمه جهلا وهذا محال . وأيضاً : إن ذلك العلم ، لما كان علماً في الأزل ثم قدرنا أن المعلوم لم يقع في الأزل ، فحينئذ ينقلب ذلك العلم جهلا في الأزل . وهذا مفضي إلى وقوع التبديل فيها حصل في الزمان الماضي ، وكل ذلك محال . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن هذه الأجوبة في غاية الضعف ، وأن البرهان الذي عولنا عليه ، مما لا يمكن دفعه بوجه من الوجوه
As for their second answer, it is also weak. Because our statement: 'If what enters existence is the faith of Zayd, God would know in eternity that he would believe; and if what enters is his non-faith, God would know in eternity that he would not believe' — this is a conditional proposition. For our statement 'If it were so, it would be so' is undoubtedly a conditional. However, we say: It is necessary, along with this conditional, that He, exalted, was in eternity knowing that he would believe or knowing that he would not believe. So it is established that one of these two knowledges is actual. And when that is actual, it is impossible for the opposite of that known to occur, otherwise His knowledge would become ignorance—and this is impossible. Also, that knowledge, since it was knowledge in eternity, and then we suppose that the known did not occur in eternity, then that knowledge would turn into ignorance in eternity. This leads to the occurrence of change in what happened in past time, and all of that is impossible. So it is established from what we have mentioned that these answers are extremely weak, and that the proof on which we relied cannot be repelled in any way.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage refutes the opponents' second answer, which claimed that the conditional statement 'If X, then God would know X' does not entail a definite knowledge. Sanusi points out that although the statement is conditional, it is certain that one of the two conditions is actual: either God knows in eternity that Zayd will believe or knows that he will not believe. Thus, one definite knowledge exists. If the opposite of that known were to occur, God's knowledge would become ignorance—impossible. Moreover, if at the time of eternity the known did not occur, then the eternal knowledge would become ignorance in that very moment, implying change in the past. All these are impossible. Therefore, the opponents' answers are futile, and his earlier proof stands unrefuted.

علم أزلي
نقيض المعلوم
تبديل
زمان ماض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think conditional propositions avoid the need for definite knowledge in God, leaving both alternatives open without actuality.
Correction: Sanusi shows that even with a conditional, one of the two knowledges must be actual, because one of the conditions will be realized. The conditional does not prevent the existence of actual knowledge.

Checkpoint

A) Because conditional statements are invalid in logic.
B) Because even if it is conditional, one of the two knowledges must be actual, and if the opposite occurs, God's knowledge would become ignorance.
C) Because the opponents do not understand conditionals.
D) Because the conditional implies both alternatives are possible.

Key Takeaway
The conditional form does not prevent the conclusion that God's knowledge would be subject to change if human actions were not predetermined, which is impossible.

God's Eternal Knowledge Implies He Creates Actions
Analyzes the concept of preponderance to prove God creates actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's eternal knowledge of human actions logically entails that He is their creator and necessitator.

Core Idea
God's eternal knowledge of future human actions proves He creates them.

In this passage, the author presents a subtle philosophical argument linking divine foreknowledge to divine causation of human acts. He starts from the concept of 'preponderance' (tarjīḥ)—the idea that a future action will occur at a specific time. This concept exists in God's eternal knowledge. But for it to exist eternally, it must have an eternal cause. By eliminating other possibilities, he shows that only God's power and will can be that cause. Let's walk through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Get a feel for the flow of the argument.
Notice the logical connectors like 'فإن لم تكن' and 'فثبت'.
2
Identify the starting premise
The argument begins with the concept of preponderance (ترجيح) existing in eternity. Locate that phrase and understand that 'concept' refers to the mental reality of the future action in God's knowledge.
The author distinguishes between the actual occurrence later and the eternal concept.
3
Follow the elimination process
The author rules out three potential causes: the essence of the concept itself (would make it necessary), the servant's power, and the servant's will. Why are the last two impossible?
Because they are non-existent in eternity (معدوم).
4
Trace the conclusion and the philosophical principle
Once God's power and will are established as the cause, the argument shows that if they did not necessitate the concept, God would be ignorant. Then the author reveals that this reasoning is based on a philosophical rule: certainty about a contingent requires knowledge of its cause.
This ties the theological issue to a broader metaphysical principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووقوع الترجيح، وإن كان لا يحصل إلا في ذلك الوقت، إلا أن المفهوم من قولنا: سيترجح: كان حاصلا في الأزل. فهذا المفهوم لما كان حاصلا في الأزل وجب أن يحصل له ما يقتضي حصوله. لكن المقتضي لحصوله، ليس هو ذاته، وإلا لكان واجب الحصول لذاته. ولا قدرة العبد، ولا إرادته، لأن كل ذلك معدوم. فوجب أن يكون المقتضي له: هو قدرة الله وإرادته. وذلك يقتضي أن يكون وقوع أفعال العباد بقدرة الله وإرادته. فإن لم تكن قدرة الله وإرادته، يقتضيان ما هو المفهوم من قولنا: إن الفعل الفلاني سيترجح في الوقت الفلاني، وليس له نقيض آخر. وجب أن لا يحصل هذا المفهوم. وإذا لم يحصل هذا المفهوم، كان اعتقاد حصوله جهلا. فثبت: أن على هذا التقدير أنه تعالى لو لم يكن موجداً لأفعال العباد ولا لما يكون موجباً لأفعال العباد، لامتنع كونه تعالى عالماً بوقوعها في الأزل. ولما كان هذا باطلاً، ثبت أن الحق ما ذكرناه، واعلم: أن هذه النكتة إنما استنبطناها من مسألة حكمية. وهي: أن الجزم بوقوع الممكن، لا يمكن إلا بواسطة العلم بعلته وموجبه. وبالله التوفيق.
And the occurrence of preponderance, even if it only occurs at that time, yet the concept from our saying 'it will preponderate' was existent in eternity. So this concept, since it was existent in eternity, it must have that which necessitates its existence. But the necessitator of its existence is not its essence, otherwise it would be necessary of existence by itself. Nor is it the capacity of the servant nor his will, because all of that is non-existent. So it must be that the necessitator of it is the power of Allah and His will. And that necessitates that the occurrence of the actions of servants is by the power of Allah and His will. For if the power of Allah and His will did not necessitate what is the concept of our saying 'such-and-such an action will preponderate at such-and-such a time' and it has no other opposite, then this concept would not occur. And if this concept does not occur, then believing in its occurrence is ignorance. So it is established that, on this estimation, if He, exalted, were not the creator of the actions of servants nor the causer of the actions of servants, it would be impossible for Him, exalted, to be knowing of their occurrence in eternity. And since this is false, it is established that the truth is what we mentioned. And know that this point we have extracted from a philosophical issue, which is: certainty of the occurrence of the possible cannot be attained except through knowledge of its cause and necessitator. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God must be the creator and necessitator of human actions in order for His eternal knowledge of those actions to be possible. The argument begins with the concept of preponderance (tarjīḥ): even if the actual preponderance occurs later, the concept that 'it will preponderate' exists in God's eternal knowledge. Since this concept exists eternally, it requires a cause for its existence. That cause cannot be the action itself (since it is contingent and would otherwise be necessary), nor can it be the servant's power or will, because those are non-existent at the time of the eternal concept. Therefore, the cause must be God's power and will. If God's power and will did not necessitate this concept, then the concept would not exist, leading to ignorance in God—an impossibility. Hence, God must be the creator and necessitator of actions. The author notes that this insight is derived from a philosophical principle: certainty about a future contingent requires knowledge of its cause.

Key Terms
ترجيح
قدرة
إرادة
علة
موجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes that God's knowledge is caused by the future event.
Correction: Actually, the argument shows that the known future event must have a cause in order to be knowable. The cause is God's power and will, not a dependence of God on the event.

Checkpoint

A) Because they are non-existent in eternity
B) Because they are not self-sufficient
C) Because they are not decisive
D) Because they are created

Key Takeaway
To know future human actions eternally, God must be their creator and necessitator.

The Obligatoriness of an Action Cannot Be Self-Caused
Sanusi eliminates two possible sources of an action's obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the obligatoriness of a contingent action cannot be intrinsic to itself, and why it cannot be caused by the servant.

Core Idea
An action possible in itself cannot be necessary in itself; its necessity must come from another.

Sanusi begins his proof by examining the very fact that an action is obligated to occur at a specific moment in time. He quickly eliminates two possibilities: that the obligation is self-caused, and that it is caused by the human servant. This sets the stage for tracing the ultimate cause back to a divine attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening disjunction
The passage starts by asking: what is the source of the obligation (وجوب) for an action to occur at a specific time? Read the Arabic and translation carefully.
Notice the author uses logical division: either self-caused or caused by another.
2
Analyze the refutation of self-causation
The author rejects self-causation because the action is 'possible in itself' (ممكن لذاته) and cannot be 'necessary in itself' (واجب لذاته). Ask yourself why that distinction matters.
This is a fundamental axiom in kalam: contingent beings cannot necessitate their own existence or attributes.
3
Consider the refutation of the servant as cause
Next, the author argues that the servant cannot be the cause, because God's eternal knowledge already determined the action's occurrence, making its obligation eternal. Ponder why a temporal being cannot cause an eternal obligation.
The core point: God's foreknowledge fixes the action's necessary status eternally, so no temporal being can confer that eternal obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوقوع في ذلك الوقت المعين. فإما أن يكون وجوبه لذاته وهو محال لأن الممكن لذاته لا يكون واجباً لذاته. وإما أن يكون وجوبه لغيره وذلك الغير إما العبد وإما الله والأول باطل لأن الفعل الذي علم الله وقوعه في الوقت الفلاني محكوم عليه في الأزل بأنه واجب أن يقع في الوقت الفلاني لا يزال فالوجوب بهذا التفسير حاصل في الأزل بما لا يكون حاصلاً في الأزل محال ولما بطل هذا ثبت أن المؤثر في حصول الوجوب بالتفسير الذي ذكرناه صفة من صفات الله تعالى. وتلك الصفة إما العلم وإما غيره والأول باطل لأن العلم يتعلق بالمعلوم على ما هو عليه وهو يتبع المعلوم ولا يستتبعه فيمتنع أن يكون المقتضي لهذا الوجوب هو علم الله فثبت أن المقتضي لهذا الوجوب صفة من صفات الله باشرها في ترجيح أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر وتلك الصفة ليست إلا القدرة أو الإرادة. فثبت أن المؤثر في وقوع أفعال العباد ليس إلا قدرة الله إما ابتداء أو بواسطة وذلك هو المطلوب.
The occurrence at that specific time. So either its obligatoriness (وجوبه) is for itself, and that is impossible because the possible in itself (الممكن لذاته) cannot be necessary in itself (واجباً لذاته). Or its obligatoriness is for another, and that other is either the servant or God. The first is false because the action which God knew would occur at such-and-such time is being judged in pre-eternity as being necessary to occur at such-and-such time; it does not cease; so obligatoriness in this interpretation is obtained in pre-eternity; what is not obtained in pre-eternity is impossible. When this is invalidated, it is established that the effecting factor (المؤثر) in obtaining obligatoriness in the interpretation we mentioned is an attribute of God Most High. And that attribute is either knowledge (العلم) or other than it; the first is false because knowledge relates to the known as it is, and it follows the known and does not require it; so it is impossible that the requirer (المقتضي) of this obligatoriness is God's knowledge. So it is established that the requirer of this obligatoriness is an attribute of God that directs it in preferring one of the two sides of the possible over the other, and that attribute is only power (القدرة) or will (الإرادة). So it is established that the effecting factor in the occurrence of the actions of servants is only God's power, either initially or through mediation, and that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a proof (برهان) establishing that the cause of the occurrence of human actions is God's power. The author begins by considering the obligatoriness (وجوب) of an action at a specific time. He argues it cannot be necessary by itself because the action is possible in itself (ممكن لذاته) and thus cannot be necessary in itself (واجب لذاته). Therefore, its obligatoriness must come from another. That other cannot be the servant, because God's eternal knowledge determined the action's occurrence, making its obligatoriness eternal — which cannot be caused by a temporal servant. Hence, the effecting factor (المؤثر) must be an attribute of God. It cannot be knowledge, because knowledge follows the known and does not necessitate it. Therefore, it must be either power (قدرة) or will (إرادة). The conclusion is that God's power is the sole effecting factor in human actions, either directly or through intermediaries.

Key Terms
وجوب
ممكن لذاته
واجب لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that an action's obligation can be intrinsic to the action itself, independent of any cause.
Correction: The author argues that since the action is possible (contingent), it cannot be necessary by itself; its obligation must come from an external cause.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject that the obligatoriness of the action is for itself?
A) Because the action is impossible
B) Because the action is possible in itself and cannot be necessary in itself
C) Because it depends on God's knowledge
D) Because it is eternal

Key Takeaway
An action's obligation cannot be self-caused nor caused by the servant; it must come from a divine source.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the obligatoriness of your every action is eternally determined by God's knowledge, do you feel a sense of comfort or a sense of accountability? What does that tell you about your relationship with God's decree?
?
When you perform an act of worship, do you consider that its very obligation was already fixed in pre-eternity? How does that realization affect the quality of your worship—does it make you more humble or more heedless?
?
If an action's obligation cannot come from your own intention or effort, then what role do your choices really play? Do you accept that your actions are ultimately caused by God, or do you resist that idea? How does this teaching change the way you think about sin and repentance?

The Effector Is a Divine Attribute: Power or Will
Sanusi eliminates knowledge and concludes the effector is power or will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribute causing an action's obligation must be either power or will, and why God's knowledge is excluded.

Core Idea
Knowledge follows the known, so it cannot cause necessity; only power or will can.

Having established that the cause of an action's obligation is a divine attribute, Sanusi now narrows down which attribute it is. He eliminates knowledge because it merely conforms to reality, and concludes that the effector must be either power or will. This final step confirms that human actions ultimately depend on God's power.

Guided Path
1
Read the second disjunction
The passage splits the divine attribute into knowledge or something else. Read the Arabic and see how the author rejects knowledge.
The key phrase: 'العلم يتعلق بالمعلوم على ما هو عليه' — knowledge relates to the known as it is.
2
Understand why knowledge cannot be the cause
Knowledge is descriptive, not prescriptive; it follows the known, it does not make the known necessary. Reflect on the difference between knowledge and causal power.
This refutation relies on the epistemological principle that knowledge is subordinate to its object.
3
Identify the conclusion
After eliminating knowledge, the author concludes that the attribute must be power or will, which directly bring about preference between possibilities. This leads to the final thesis: God's power is the ultimate effector.
The phrase 'باشرها في ترجيح أحد طرفي الممكن' indicates that this attribute directly performs the act of preferring one possibility over another.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوقوع في ذلك الوقت المعين. فإما أن يكون وجوبه لذاته وهو محال لأن الممكن لذاته لا يكون واجباً لذاته. وإما أن يكون وجوبه لغيره وذلك الغير إما العبد وإما الله والأول باطل لأن الفعل الذي علم الله وقوعه في الوقت الفلاني محكوم عليه في الأزل بأنه واجب أن يقع في الوقت الفلاني لا يزال فالوجوب بهذا التفسير حاصل في الأزل بما لا يكون حاصلاً في الأزل محال ولما بطل هذا ثبت أن المؤثر في حصول الوجوب بالتفسير الذي ذكرناه صفة من صفات الله تعالى. وتلك الصفة إما العلم وإما غيره والأول باطل لأن العلم يتعلق بالمعلوم على ما هو عليه وهو يتبع المعلوم ولا يستتبعه فيمتنع أن يكون المقتضي لهذا الوجوب هو علم الله فثبت أن المقتضي لهذا الوجوب صفة من صفات الله باشرها في ترجيح أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر وتلك الصفة ليست إلا القدرة أو الإرادة. فثبت أن المؤثر في وقوع أفعال العباد ليس إلا قدرة الله إما ابتداء أو بواسطة وذلك هو المطلوب.
The occurrence at that specific time. So either its obligatoriness (وجوبه) is for itself, and that is impossible because the possible in itself (الممكن لذاته) cannot be necessary in itself (واجباً لذاته). Or its obligatoriness is for another, and that other is either the servant or God. The first is false because the action which God knew would occur at such-and-such time is being judged in pre-eternity as being necessary to occur at such-and-such time; it does not cease; so obligatoriness in this interpretation is obtained in pre-eternity; what is not obtained in pre-eternity is impossible. When this is invalidated, it is established that the effecting factor (المؤثر) in obtaining obligatoriness in the interpretation we mentioned is an attribute of God Most High. And that attribute is either knowledge (العلم) or other than it; the first is false because knowledge relates to the known as it is, and it follows the known and does not require it; so it is impossible that the requirer (المقتضي) of this obligatoriness is God's knowledge. So it is established that the requirer of this obligatoriness is an attribute of God that directs it in preferring one of the two sides of the possible over the other, and that attribute is only power (القدرة) or will (الإرادة). So it is established that the effecting factor in the occurrence of the actions of servants is only God's power, either initially or through mediation, and that is the desired conclusion.

Commentary
This passage is a proof (برهان) establishing that the cause of the occurrence of human actions is God's power. The author begins by considering the obligatoriness (وجوب) of an action at a specific time. He argues it cannot be necessary by itself because the action is possible in itself (ممكن لذاته) and thus cannot be necessary in itself (واجب لذاته). Therefore, it must be either power (قدرة) or will (إرادة). The conclusion is that God's power is the sole effecting factor in human actions, either directly or through intermediaries.

Refutation of the Three-Premise Proof for Divine Unique Power
The author refutes a tripartite proof for God's exclusive power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's three-premise proof that only God is powerful by essence and how the author refutes it by denying the consistency of those premises.

Core Idea
Refutation often begins by exposing the shaky foundations of an argument.

This passage presents a concise refutation of a rationalist proof. The author first lays out the opponent's three premises, then declares that these premises have never been established. By doing so, he undermines the entire argument without addressing each premise individually. Understanding this technique is key to following many theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the structure: a summary of an argument followed by a critique.
The passage starts with a phrase that may reference previous discussion.
2
Identify the three premises
Locate the three premises the author attributes to the opponent. They are introduced with 'أولها', 'ثانيها', 'ثالثها'. Understand each premise.
The premises are: (1) the being must be a body, (2) all bodies are similar, (3) things identical in quiddity share all concomitants.
3
Understand the refutation
The author concludes that the inconsistency of these premises has not been established at all. This is a dismissal of the entire proof. Ask yourself: why is it enough to deny the premises rather than refute each one?
4
Connect to broader theology
Consider why someone would argue that only God can be powerful by essence. This argument relates to the uniqueness of divine attributes. The refutation protects the possibility that other beings could have power by essence, though that is not the author's position to assert.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأحكام اللازمة، فلم لا يجوز مثله في الذاتين المتفاوتين؟ والحاصل: إن دليلهم على أن غير الله، يمتنع أن يكون قادراً لذاته مبني على ثلاث مقدمات: أولها: إن ذلك المغاير يجب أن يكون جسماً. وثانيها: إن الأجسام متماثلة. وثالثها: إن المتماثلات في تمام الماهية، يجب تساويها في جميع اللوازم. وقد ظهر: أن تنافي هذه المقدمات، لم يتقرر البتة.
The necessary judgments, so why is its like not permissible in the two differing essences? The summary: Their proof that other than God cannot be powerful by essence is based on three premises: First: that other entity must be a body. Second: that bodies are similar. Third: that things similar in the entirety of quiddity must be equal in all necessary concomitants. And it has become clear that the inconsistency of these premises is not established at all.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage refutes an opponent's proof that nothing other than God can be powerful by essence. It first presents the opponent's argument, which rests on three premises: (1) the entity in question must be a body, (2) all bodies are similar in nature, and (3) things identical in quiddity must share all properties. The author then rejects the entire argument by stating that these premises are contradictory and have never been established. The refutation is decisive but not detailed here; it suffices to deny the validity of the premises.

Correction: The author lists the premises only to show their inconsistency; he does not endorse them.

Refuting the Four Premises on Acquired Power
Rejects four premises arguing acquired powers cannot create bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author systematically refutes each of the four premises in the opponent's argument that acquired powers cannot create bodies.

Core Idea
Impossibility needs no cause; shared rulings do not require shared causes.

This lesson examines a second argument against acquired power, which claims that all acquired powers share an inability to create bodies, so there must be a shared cause—namely, being power itself. The author dismantles this by showing that each of its four premises is flawed. We will follow his refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice the structure: the author states the opponent's claim, then lists four premises and refutes each.
2
Examine the first premise refutation
The opponent says impossibility must have a cause. The author replies that impossibility is pure non-existence, which needs no cause.
This relies on the axiom that non-existence is not an effect.
3
Examine the second premise refutation
The opponent says a shared ruling must have a shared cause. The author gives the example of ugliness (qubh) according to the Mu'tazila: different actions share ugliness but each is caused by its specific nature, not a single cause.
This shows the principle is false.
4
Examine the third and fourth premises
Third: the opponent claims the only common factor is 'being power'—the author counters that power may be divided into types, and the inability to create might be due to a specific attribute. Fourth: the opponent uses the principle 'the ruling of a thing is the ruling of its like'—the author says this has already been shown problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني: لم لا يجوز أن يكون قادراً بالقدوة؟ أما قوله: و القدرة الحاصلة، عندما لا يصلح شيء منها، لإيجاد الجسم. فهذا الامتناع حكم مشترك، فلا بد من علة مشتركة ولا مشترك إلا كونها قدراً، قلنا: هذا الكلام أيضاً مبني على أربع مقدمات: أولها: قوله: « هذا الامتناع لا بد له من علة، وهذا باطل لأن الامتناع عدم محض، والعدم لا علة له. وثانيها: قوله: الحكم المشترك لا بد له من علة مشتركة، وهذا أيضاً باطل. بدليل: أن القبح عند المعتزلة، وصف مشترك فيه بين الظلم والجهل، والكذب والعبث. ثم إن قبح الظلم عندهم معلل بخصوص كونه ظلماً، وقبح الجهل معلل بخصوص كونه جهلا. فهذا حكم مشترك فيه. وهو غير معلل بعلة مشترك فيها. وثالثها: قوله: و لا شركة إلا كونها قدراً، وهذا أيضاً ممنوع. فلم لا يجوز أن يقال القدر على قسمين قسم لا يصلح لخلق الجسم، وقسم يصلح لذلك. والقسم الذي لا يصلح لخلق الجسم: مشترك في وصف واحد، ولأجل ذلك الوصف يتعذر فعل الجسم بها. وذلك الوصف غير حاصل في القسم الثاني؟ وأقصى ما في الباب: أنا لا نعرف ذلك. لكن عدم العلم بالشيء، لا يدل على العلم بعدم الشيء. ورابعها: إن كون القدرة قدرة، لما كانت على هذا الحكم، وجب أن تكون كل قدرة مانعة من هذا الحكم. وهذا بناء على أن حكم الشيء حكم مثله. وقد بينا ما في هذه المقدمة.
The second station: Why is it not permissible that one be powerful by acquired power? As for his saying: 'The acquired power, when none of it is fit for the origination of a body. So this impossibility is a shared ruling, thus it must have a shared cause, and there is no shared thing except its being power', we say: This speech too is based on four premises: First: his saying 'this impossibility must have a cause', and this is false because impossibility is pure nonexistence, and nonexistence has no cause. Second: his saying 'the shared ruling must have a shared cause', and this is also false. Evidence: that ugliness (qubh) according to the Mu'tazila is a shared attribute between injustice, ignorance, lying, and vainness. Then the ugliness of injustice according to them is caused by its being specifically injustice, and the ugliness of ignorance is caused by its being specifically ignorance. So this is a shared ruling, yet it is not caused by a shared cause. Third: his saying 'and there is no sharing except its being power', and this is also denied. So why is it not permissible to say that power is of two types: a type not fit for creating a body, and a type fit for that. And the type not fit for creating a body is shared in one attribute, and because of that attribute the action upon the body is impossible by it. And that attribute is not present in the second type? The utmost of the matter is that we do not know that. But lack of knowledge of something does not indicate knowledge of the non-existence of something. And fourth: that because power being power was upon this ruling, it must be that every power is preventive of this ruling. And this is based on the saying that the ruling of a thing is the ruling of its like. And we have already shown what is in this premise.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage refutes an opponent's second argument, which claims that acquired powers cannot create bodies. The opponent argues that all such powers share the impossibility of creation, so they must have a shared cause, namely being power. The author responds by exposing four flawed premises in this reasoning. (1) The claim that impossibility needs a cause is false; impossibility is non-existence and needs no cause. (2) The claim that a shared ruling requires a shared cause is false, as demonstrated by the Mu'tazila's treatment of ugliness: different actions share ugliness but each is caused by its specific nature. (3) The claim that the only common factor is 'being power' is denied; power can be divided into types, and the inability to create may be due to a specific attribute not present in all powers. Our lack of knowledge of that attribute does not prove its absence. (4) The claim that because power as such entails a certain ruling, all powers must share that ruling is based on the principle 'the ruling of a thing is the ruling of its like', which has already been shown to be problematic. Thus the opponent's argument collapses.

قدرة حاصلة
امتناع
علة مشتركة
قدر
قبح
علة
مقدمات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that impossibility must have a cause, or that shared rulings require a shared cause.
Correction: The author shows impossibility is non-existence and needs no cause, and the Mu'tazila's example of ugliness demonstrates that shared rulings can have different causes.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the first premise (impossibility must have a cause) false?
A) Impossibility is a real attribute and needs no cause
B) Impossibility is pure nonexistence and nonexistence has no cause
C) Everything has a cause, so it is true
D) The premise is not about impossibility

Key Takeaway
The four-premise argument fails because impossibility needs no cause, shared rulings do not require a shared cause, the common factor may not be power, and the principle of like rulings is unsupported.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author argues that impossibility needs no cause. When you confront a personal struggle that feels impossible (e.g., overcoming a sin or habit), do you assume a cause for that impossibility? Will you instead look to God's power and not let your own perception of impossibility become an excuse?
?
The opponent assumed shared rulings require a shared cause. In your relationships, do you judge people's actions by assuming the same motive for similar behaviors? How can you apply this lesson to avoid false assumptions and show more mercy?
?
The author says 'lack of knowledge of something does not indicate knowledge of the non-existence of something.' Where in your faith are you tempted to deny something simply because you don't understand it (e.g., divine decree, unseen matters)? Will you humbly admit your ignorance and affirm what Allah has revealed?

The Tenth Proof: Faith is Created by God Alone
A syllogistic proof that faith is created by God, not the servant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that faith cannot be created by the servant because it would make the servant nobler than God.

Core Idea
Faith is too noble to be created by anyone but God.

This passage presents a concise logical proof. The author uses a premise about relative nobility to argue that faith, being the noblest creation, must be directly from God. Let's read the proof and see how the logic unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation to grasp the full argument. Note the premise, the induction, and the conclusion.
Pay attention to the term 'ashaaf' (nobler) and its role.
2
Identify the key logical steps
Break down the argument: Premise (agent nobler act = nobler), Evidence (complete induction), Identification (faith is noblest), Hypothetical (if servant created faith), Consequence (servant nobler than God), Rejection (false by consensus), Conclusion (faith from God).
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: What does this proof affirm about divine causation? It affirms that the noblest human act is directly from God, not from human power.
It does not deny human agency entirely, only the creation of faith itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان العاشر إن كل فاعلين ، يكون فعل أحدهما أشرف من فعل الثاني ، كان فاعل الأشرف أشرف من فاعل الفعل الأخس . ودليل صحة هذه المقدمة : الاستقراء التام في جميع الأفعال ، وجميع الفاعلين . ولا شك أن أشرف المخلوقات الإيمان بالله تعالى . ولو كان هذا واقعاً بتخليق العبد ، لزم أن يكون مخلوق العبد أشرف من جميع مخلوقات الله تعالى . وذلك يقتضي أن يكون العبد أشرف من الله . ولما كان باطلاً بالإجماع ، علمنا : أن إيمان العبد ليس خلق للعبد ، بل هو خلق الله تعالى وهو المطلوب وبالله التوفيق
The tenth proof: Whenever there are two agents, and the act of one of them is nobler ([أشرف]) than the act of the second, the agent of the nobler act is nobler than the agent of the baser act. And the proof of the soundness of this premise is complete induction ([استقراء تام]) to all acts and all agents. There is no doubt that the noblest of created things is faith ([إيمان]) in Allah, the Exalted. And if this [faith] were occurring by the creation ([تخليق]) of the servant, it would necessarily follow that the creation of the servant is nobler than all the creations of Allah, the Exalted. And that requires that the servant be nobler than Allah. And since it is false by consensus ([إجماع]), we know that the servant's faith is not a creation of the servant, but rather it is the creation of Allah, the Exalted — and that is what is sought. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof (البرهان العاشر) regarding the creation of human acts. The author begins by stating a general premise: whenever there are two agents, and the act of one is nobler than the act of the other, then the agent of the nobler act is himself nobler than the agent of the baser act. The evidence for this premise is complete induction across all acts and agents. Next, he identifies the noblest of created things as faith in God. If faith were created by the servant, then the servant's creation would be nobler than all of God's creations, which would imply that the servant is nobler than God. Since this conclusion is false by consensus (of Muslims), it follows that faith cannot be created by the servant; rather, it is created by God alone. The proof is thus concluded.

Key Terms
البرهان العاشر
أشرف
فاعل
استقراء تام
إيمان
تخليق
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof denies human agency entirely; humans have no role in faith.
Correction: The proof only denies that faith is created by the servant's own power, but does not negate human acquisition or responsibility. It affirms that faith is a divine creation, not that humans are passive.

Checkpoint
What is the evidence for the premise that the agent of the nobler act is nobler than the agent of the baser act?
A) Divine revelation
B) Complete induction across all acts and agents
C) Consensus of the Mu'tazila
D) Logical intuition

Key Takeaway
Faith is created by God alone, not by the servant's own power, as demonstrated by a logical argument based on comparative nobility and consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever taken pride in your faith as if it were your own achievement? How does realizing that faith is created by God alone shatter that pride and force you to thank Him for every moment of belief?
?
When you struggle to increase your faith, do you turn to your own efforts or do you truly cry out to God to create iman in your heart? What specific habits come from a belief that faith is your own doing?
?
If faith is the noblest creation of God, how should that change the way you treat your own faith when it weakens? Will you despair or turn to the only One who can renew it, knowing He alone is the source?

beginner
5 min
Faith is created by God, not the servant
Proof that faith, being the noblest creation, must be created by God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that faith (iman) is created by God alone, not by any created being.

Core Idea
If faith were created by us, our creation would surpass God's—impossible.

This passage presents a concise but powerful logical proof that faith is entirely God's creation. The argument uses a reductio ad absurdum, showing that if humans created faith, they would be greater than God, which is absurd. As you read, pay attention to how the author establishes the premises and draws the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic passage aloud or silently, then read the literal translation. Get a feel for the argument's flow.
The passage is a single logical unit with three main steps.
2
Identify the first premise
The author states that faith is the noblest of all created things. Why is this premise important?
3
Trace the hypothetical argument
Assume the servant creates faith. Then the servant's creation would be nobler than all of God's creations, implying the servant is nobler than God.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Confirm the conclusion
Since the implication is false by consensus, the original assumption is false. Therefore, faith must be created by God.
The phrase 'wa huwa al-matlub' signals the end of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا شك أن أشرف المخلوقات الإيمان بالله تعالى . ولو كان هذا واقعاً بتخليق العبد ، لزم أن يكون مخلوق العبد أشرف من جميع مخلوقات الله تعالى . وذلك يقتضي أن يكون العبد أشرف من الله . ولما كان باطلاً بالإجماع ، علمنا : أن إيمان العبد ليس خلق للعبد ، بل هو خلق الله تعالى وهو المطلوب
There is no doubt that the noblest of created things is faith in God Most High. If this were actually brought about by the servant's creation, then the servant's created thing would be nobler than all of God's created things. And that would require that the servant be nobler than God. And since this is false by consensus, we know that the servant's faith is not a creation of the servant, but rather it is a creation of God Most High. And that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a proof that faith (iman) is created by God, not by the human servant. The argument proceeds by two key premises. First, faith is acknowledged as the noblest of all created things. Second, if faith were created by the servant, then the servant's creation (faith) would be nobler than all of God's creations, since faith is the noblest. This would imply that the servant himself is nobler than God, because the creator of a noble thing is nobler (by the principle that the creator shares in the nobility of its creation). But this implication is false by unanimous consensus of Muslims. Therefore, the original assumption that faith is created by the servant is false. Hence, faith must be created by God alone. The passage concludes with 'That is what was sought' (wa huwa al-matlub), a standard phrase indicating the completion of the proof.

Correction: This passage clarifies that creation (takhlīq) belongs solely to God; human acquisition does not imply creation.

Third Proof: God's Power Over All Possibles Through Possibility
The author proves that God is powerful over all possible things through the concept of imkan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that God's power covers all possible things, based on the concept of imkan (possibility).

Core Idea
Possibility is the key that unlocks all possibles to God's power.

This lesson presents a formal proof from classical Islamic theology demonstrating that God is powerful over all possible things. The argument proceeds by examining why some possibles are objects of divine power and then generalizing from the inherent property of possibility. It carefully eliminates other factors (necessity and impossibility) to isolate possibility as the determining cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
First, read the Arabic passage for retention, then the translation to grasp the logical flow.
Notice the use of conditional reasoning.
2
Identify the initial premise
The proof starts with an accepted truth: God is powerful over some possible things. Consider why this premise is taken as evident.
3
Analyze the elimination argument
Follow the reasoning: if possibility is removed, what remains? Necessity and impossibility, which prevent something from being an object of power. Understand why that matters.
The author uses a disjunctive syllogism.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since possibility is the only remaining factor, it must be the cause. Then argue that possibility is universal to all possibles, so all are valid objects of power.
This is the core inference.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this proof avoids the need for an external determiner. How does it establish God's power over all possibles?
The final step uses the impossibility of an external determiner for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة على أنه تعالى قادر على مقدورات العباد : وهي : إنه لا شك أن الله تعالى قادر على بعض الممكنات . فكون ذلك البعض ، بحيث أن يكون مقدوراً الله تعالى ، وجب أن يكون معللاً بإمكانه . لأنا لو رفعنا يصح الإمكان ، بقي . إما الامتناع ، وإما الوجوب . وهما يحيلان المقدورية . وما كان مانعا من الشيء ، لا يكون علة لحصوله . ولما خرج الوجوب والامتناع عن أن يكونا علة لصحة المقدورية، بقي الإمكان مانعاً لهذه العلية . فثبت : أن الذي لأجله. يصح في بعض الممكنات أن يكون مقدوراً الله تعالى ، حاصل في الممكنات كلها، فوجب القطع بأن جميع الممكنات يصح عليها أن تكون مقدورة لله تعالى . ولما ثبت عموم هذه الصحة فلو تعلقت قدرة الله ببعضها دون بعض لزم الافتقار إلى المخصص ، وهو محال . فثبت : أنه تعالى قادر على جميع الممكنات.
The third proof that He Most High is powerful over the objects of power of the servants: namely, there is no doubt that God Most High is powerful over some possible things. So that some [things] being such that they are an object of power of God Most High must be caused by their possibility. For if we remove possibility, what remains is either impossibility or necessity. And both render [the thing] impossible for being an object of power. And what is a preventer of a thing is not a cause for its occurrence. So when necessity and impossibility are excluded from being the cause for the validity of being an object of power, possibility remains as the preventer of this causality. So it is established that that on account of which it is valid for some possible things to be an object of power of God Most High is present in all possible things, so it is necessary to decide that all possible things are valid to be objects of power of God Most High. And when the universality of this validity is established, if the power of God were attached to some to the exclusion of others, dependence on a determiner would be necessary, which is impossible. So it is established that He Most High is powerful over all possible things.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage presents the third formal proof (al-hujja al-thalitha) demonstrating that God is powerful over the actions of His servants. The proof proceeds in stages: First, it is undeniable that God is powerful over some possibles. The fact that these particular possibles are objects of His power must have a cause, and that cause is their inherent possibility (imkan). The author argues by elimination: if possibility is removed, only impossibility (imtina') or necessity (wujub) remain, and both prevent something from being an object of power. What prevents a thing cannot be the cause of its occurrence. Thus the cause must be possibility. Since possibility is common to all possible things, all possibles share the quality of being valid objects of God's power. Consequently, God's power must attach to all possibilities; otherwise, a specific determination would require an external determiner, which is impossible for God. The conclusion is that God is powerful over all possible things, including human actions.

Key Terms
الإمكان
الامتناع
الوجوب
المقدورية
المخصص
الممكنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that possibility alone is insufficient to establish that God actually wills to create something; the argument only establishes that it is in God's power, not that it is willed.
Correction: This proof is specifically about God's power (qudra), not His will (irada). It demonstrates that all possibles are within His power, but it does not address whether He wills to bring them into existence. The proof is valid for establishing the extent of power.

Checkpoint

Necessity
Impossibility
Possibility (imkan)
The thing's essence

Key Takeaway
By eliminating necessity and impossibility, this proof establishes that possibility (imkan) is the reason any possible thing falls under God's power, and since all possibles share possibility, God is powerful over all of them.

?
The proof shows that possibility alone is the condition for God's power, not your worthiness or effort. Does your heart fully trust that God can do anything He wills, even if it seems impossible to you? What specific dua will you make today to affirm this belief?
