Objection and Answer: Creator vs Originator
Objector distinguishes khaliq from muhdith; answer analyzes proper noun 'Allah'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that distinguishes khaliq (creator) from muhdith (originator) and the classical Ashʿarī response that analyzes the name 'Allah' as a proper noun to refute this distinction.

Core Idea
The distinction between creator and originator fails because 'Allah' is a proper noun, making exclusivity refer to the attribute.

This passage presents a sophisticated theological debate: the objector concedes that only Allah is called 'creator' in a precise sense, but argues that humans can still be 'originators' of their actions. The answer, however, uses a subtle logical analysis of how proper nouns function in Arabic to show that the verse's exclusivity must refer to the attribute of creatorhood, not the name. This reframes the entire debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the objector's concession: the verse indicates no creator but Allah, but the servant can still be an originator (muhdith) of his own actions.
Notice the objector defines creation (khalq) as precise estimation (taqdir).
2
Examine the definition of khaliq
The objector argues that a creator is one whose action occurs exactly as estimated, which only applies to Allah. Humans often estimate one thing but another occurs.
3
Analyze the answer's premise
The answer begins by stating that 'Allah' is a proper noun (ism ʿalam) that only indicates a specific essence, not a description.
Think about how proper nouns function: they point, not describe.
4
Follow the logical deduction
If the exclusivity referred to the proper noun, the verse would be a tautology (that specific entity is nothing but itself). Therefore it must refer to the word 'creator'.
This forces the exclusivity to be about creatorhood, not the name.
5
Connect to the conclusion
Thus the verse means 'He is the Creator' and establishes exclusive creatorhood for Allah, refuting the objector's distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عن سلمنا : أن هذه الآية تفيد أنه لا خالق إلا الله . إلا أنا نقول : العبد محدث لأفعال نفسه وموجد لها . ولا نقول : إنه خالق لها . لأن الخلق عارة التقدير . فالخالق هو الذي يقع فعله مقدراً بالتقدير الذي يقدره به ، على الوجه الذي يوقعه عليه. وهذا لا يصح إلا من الله تعالى . فأما العبد فإنه في أكثر الأمر يقدر شيئاً، ويقع بخلافه . فلا جرم لم يجز إطلاق اسم الخالق عليه . والله أعلم والجواب : إنا قد دللنا في كتاب : لوامع البينات . في تفسير الأسماء والصفات ) : إن الله اسم علم . وثبت أن اسم العلم لا يفيـد فـائـدة ســــوى دلالته على الذات المخصوصة . ولهذا السبب قالوا : إن أسماء الأعلام قائمة مقام الإشارات. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لا يمكن أن يكون الحصر عائدا إلى مفهوم قولنا و الله : هو تلك الذات المخصوصة ، فلو عاد الحصر إليه ، لصار معنى الكلام : إن ذلك المعين ليس إلا ذلك المعين ، ومعلوم : أن هذا الكلام عبث، لأن كل شيء معين ، فذاك ليس إلا ذاك فلا يبقى لتخصيص ذات الله بهذا الحصر فائدة . فثبت : أن هذا الحصر لا يمكن عوده إلى مفهوم قولنا و الله ، فوجب أن يكون هذا الحصر عائداً إلى المفهوم من لفظ الخالق . وحينئذ يصير تقدير الآية : هو الخالق . وذلك يقتضي حصر الخالقية فيه
Regarding [the concession]: we concede that this verse indicates that there is no creator except Allah. But we say: the servant is the originator (muhdith) of his own actions and the one who brings them into existence (mujid). And we do not say that he is a creator (khaliq) of them, because creation (khalq) is an expression of estimation (taqdir). So the creator is the one whose action occurs in an estimated manner according to the estimation by which He estimates it, in the manner He brings it about. And this is not valid except from Allah Almighty. As for the servant, in most cases he estimates something and it occurs contrary to it. Therefore it is not permissible to apply the name 'creator' to him. And Allah knows best. The answer: We have demonstrated in the book 'Lawami' al-Bayyinat' in the commentary on the names and attributes that 'Allah' is a proper noun (ism 'alam). And it is established that a proper noun does not convey any benefit except its indication of the specific essence (al-dhat al-makhsusa). For this reason they said that proper nouns stand in the place of pointing. If this is established, we say: it is not possible for the exclusivity (hasr) to refer back to the concept of our saying 'Allah' — that specific essence. For if the exclusivity referred back to it, the meaning of the statement would become: that specific entity is nothing other than that specific entity. And it is known that such speech is nonsense, because every specific entity is nothing other than itself. So there would remain no benefit in specifying the essence of Allah with this exclusivity. Therefore it is established that this exclusivity cannot refer back to the concept of our saying 'Allah'; therefore it is necessary that this exclusivity refers back to the concept of the word 'creator' (al-khaliq). At that point the estimation of the verse becomes: 'He is the Creator.' And that necessitates the exclusivity of creatorhood in Him.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer. The objector begins by conceding that the verse 'there is no creator except Allah' is valid, but argues that the servant is only the originator (muhdith) of his actions, not the creator (khaliq), because creation is defined as precise estimation (taqdir) that always comes to pass as intended—something that does not consistently occur for humans. The answer refutes this by analyzing the word 'Allah'. The author argues that 'Allah' is a proper noun referring to a specific essence, so if the exclusivity (hasr) in the verse referred to the proper noun, it would be a tautology: 'that specific entity is nothing but that specific entity.' Since such a statement is meaningless, the exclusivity must instead refer to the descriptive term 'creator'. Therefore the verse means 'He is the Creator' and thereby establishes that creatorhood belongs exclusively to Allah. This argument uses logical reasoning about the function of proper names in Arabic to defend the orthodox Ash'ari position that God alone is the true creator.

موجد (mūjid)
خلق (khalq)
تقدير (taqdīr)
اسم علم (ism ʿalam)
حصر (ḥaṣr)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans can be originators (muhdith) of their own actions without being creators, so the verse 'no creator but Allah' does not negate human origination.
Correction: The answer shows that the exclusivity refers to the attribute 'creator', not the name, so the verse establishes that all true creation/estimation is exclusive to Allah, including the precise origination of actions.

A) The proper noun 'Allah'
B) The word 'creator' (al-khaliq)
C) Both the proper noun and the word 'creator'
D) The specific essence of Allah

Key Takeaway
The exclusivity in 'no creator but Allah' must refer to the attribute of creatorhood, not the name, thus refuting any attempt to limit the verse to mere naming.

?
When your carefully made plans fail, do you honestly attribute that to your own lack of estimation, or do you recognize that only Allah's estimation is perfect? How does this lesson change the way you view your daily successes and failures?

The objector tried to preserve a sphere of independent human action by redefining terms. In what areas of your life do you subtly resist acknowledging that Allah is the sole Creator of your actions, including your intentions and outcomes? What would it cost you to surrender that illusion?
?

Second Objection: Creator as Determiner
Second objection defines creator as determiner, forcing the conclusion that humans would be creators if they originate actions, with a concluding nuance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining 'creator' as 'determiner' (muqaddir) leads to the conclusion that humans cannot originate their own actions, and how the nuance about the truth of names applies.

Core Idea
If humans truly originate actions, they would be creators—contradicting divine exclusivity.

This passage presents the second objection and its answer. The objector claims that the word 'creator' means 'determiner' (muqarrir), and that human actions sometimes occur contrary to intention. The response argues that if humans originate actions, they determine them in every detail, making them creators—which would invalidate the Quranic verse stating that Allah alone is the Creator. A concluding nuance clarifies that a name is considered true even if its referent occurs only in some instances, so occasional failure does not exempt humans from being called creators.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and translation to understand the flow: the objection is implied, then the answer begins with 'As for the second question...'
Look for the definition of 'creator' as 'determiner'.
2
Analyze the logical implication
Notice the conditional: if humans originate actions, they determine measures (number, time, place) → they are determiners → they are creators → exclusivity is broken.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: accepting the premise leads to a contradiction.
3
Understand the concluding nuance
The author adds that even if humans sometimes fail to estimate correctly, the name 'creator' is still true in instances where they do determine. This counters a potential objection.
Key concept: 'the name is true on the side of existence (thubut).'
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this argument strengthens the case that humans do not truly originate their actions. The definition of 'creator' is linked to determination, which is exclusively Allah's attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثاني . فجوابه : إن الخالق بمعنى المقرر هو الذي يـوقـع الشيء على مقدار مخصوص . فلو كان العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه ، لكان العبد موجداً لها على مقاديرها المخصوصة في العدد والزمان والمكان . فكان مقدراً لها ، فوجب كونه خالقاً لها ، وحينئذ يبطل الحصر الذي دلت الآية عليه · أقصى ما في الباب : أن أفعال العباد قد تقع في بعض الأوقات ، بخلاف تقديراتهم . إلا أن كون الاسم حقيقة في جانب الثبوت ، يكفي في حصولــه حصول ذلك المسمى في بعض الصور ، ولا يتوقف على حصول ذلك المسمى في كل الصور . وبالله التوفيق
As for the second question, its answer: that the creator in the sense of the determiner (al-muqarrir) is the one who brings about a thing upon a specific measure. So if the servant were the one who brings his own actions into existence, then the servant would be bringing them into existence upon their specific measures in number, time, and place. Then he would be a determiner (muqaddir) of them, and therefore he would necessarily be a creator of them, and at that point the exclusivity indicated by the verse would be invalidated. The utmost point in this matter is that the actions of servants sometimes occur contrary to their estimations. However, the fact that a name is true (haqiqa) on the side of existence (thubut) is sufficiently realized by the occurrence of that named thing in some instances, and it does not depend on the occurrence of that named thing in all instances. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage contains a second objection and its answer. The objector apparently claimed that the word 'creator' (khaliq) means 'determiner' (muqarrir), and that human actions sometimes occur contrary to human intention, so humans are not creators. The answer argues that if the servant truly originates his own actions (as the objector claims), then he brings them about with specific measures in number, time, and place—i.e., he determines them. Therefore he would necessarily be a creator (khaliq) according to the objector's own definition, which would contradict the verse's exclusivity. The author then adds a concluding nuance: sometimes human actions occur contrary to their estimation, but that does not affect the definition. A name is considered true (haqiqa) if its referent occurs in at least some instances; it does not require occurrence in every instance. So even if humans are not perfect determiners, they still meet the definition in some cases, making them creators in those cases—but this is impossible, so the initial premise that humans originate actions must be false.

Key Terms
مقرر (muqarrir)
مقدر (muqaddir)
مقدار مخصوص (miqdār makhṣūṣ)
حقيقة (ḥaqīqa)
ثبوت (thubūt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If humans sometimes fail to estimate correctly, then they are never called creators.
Correction: The name 'creator' (or 'determiner') is true (haqiqa) even if it applies only in some instances, not all. Occasional failure does not negate the application when it does occur.

Checkpoint
According to the second answer, what would be the consequence if the servant truly originated his own actions?
A) He would be a creator according to the definition
B) He would still not be a creator because of occasional failure
C) The verse would still not be contradicted
D) He would be a creator only if he never fails

Key Takeaway
The second objection is refuted by showing that if humans originate actions, they would be creators (determiners) in some instances, contradicting the Quranic exclusivity of creation to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan your day meticulously and things go according to your measures, do you ever feel a sense of ownership over that outcome? How does the author's argument that determination of measures is a form of creation—exclusive to Allah—challenge that feeling? What would it look like to genuinely attribute every detail to Allah without diminishing your effort?
?
The passage notes that your actions sometimes occur contrary to your estimations. Think of a recent failure where your plans did not materialize. In that moment, did you feel the limits of your control? Did it bring you closer to admitting that you are not the ultimate determiner? If not, why?
?
If the name 'creator' applies even when the intended measure only sometimes happens, then your very act of intending and partially achieving makes you a 'creator' in that sense—unless you deny that you originate the action. Are you willing to admit that you do not truly originate any action, or do you cling to a sense of origination despite the theological cost? What would change in your daily life if you fully internalized that Allah alone brings actions into existence and you only 'acquire' them?

Sixth Proof: Surah al-Furqan on Creation of Everything
Two-argument proof from Qur'an 25:2 for exclusive creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments from Surah al-Furqan 25:2 that demonstrate Allah creates everything, including human actions.

Core Idea
The verse 'He created everything' encompasses all actions, refuting any other creator.

In this lesson, we examine the sixth proof from the Qur’an. The verse from Surah al-Furqan provides two powerful arguments that Allah is the sole creator, including of human actions. The author shows how the verse’s wording itself prevents any interpretation that would allow creatures to create.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic verse and its translation carefully. Pay attention to the structure: the verse first negates partnership, then asserts creation, then determination.
The verse is from Surah al-Furqan 25:2.
2
Understand the first argument
The first argument: the verse says 'He created everything'. Human actions are things, so they are included. If 'create' were interpreted as 'estimate', the verse would be redundant.
This refutes the idea that 'khalq' could mean 'taqdir'.
3
Understand the second argument
The second argument: the verse begins by denying partners in sovereignty. To prevent the objection that servants might still create their own actions, the verse adds 'He created everything'. If only sovereignty were meant, the first clause would suffice.
This shows the additional phrase is necessary to refute human creation.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Consider how these two arguments together firmly establish that Allah is the creator of all actions, leaving no room for any other creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : قوله تعالى في أول سورة الفرقان : ( ولم يكن له شريك في الملك ، وخلق كل شيء ، فقدره تقديراً ) وجه الاستدلال بهذه الآية : من وجهين : الأول : إنه تعالى قال : وخلق كل شيء ) وأفعال العباد : أشياء . فوجب كونه خالقاً لها . ولا يمكن أن يكون المراد من هذا الخلق : التقدير . وإلا لصار الكلام هكذا : وقدر كل شيء فقدره تقديراً. الثاني : إنه تعالى ذكر أولا قوله : ( ولم يكن له شريك في الملك ) فكأنه .. قيل : هب أنه لا شريك له في ملك السموات والأرض . ولكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال : العبيد يخلقون بعض الأشياء ، وهي أفعال أنفسهم ؟ فذكر تعالى ما يزيل هذا الخيال . فقال : ( وخلق كل شيء . فقدره تقديراً ) ولو لم يكن المراد ما ذكرناه ، لكان قوله : ( ولم يكن له شريك في الملك ) مغنيا عن قوله : فقدره تقديراً )
The sixth proof: His statement, exalted is He, at the beginning of Surah al-Furqan: 'And He had no partner in sovereignty, and He created everything, then determined it with [precise] determination.' The aspect of inference from this verse is from two aspects: First: He, exalted, said: 'and He created everything' — and the actions of servants are things. So it is necessary that He is their Creator. And it is not possible that the intended meaning of this 'creation' is estimation (taqdir). Otherwise the speech would become: 'and He estimated everything, then determined it with determination.' Second: He, exalted, mentioned first His statement: 'and He had no partner in sovereignty' — as if it were said: grant that He has no partner in the sovereignty of the heavens and the earth, but why is it not permissible to say that the servants create some things, namely their own actions? Then the Most High mentioned what removes this illusion. So He said: 'and He created everything, then determined it precisely.' And if the intended meaning were not what we have mentioned, then His statement 'and He had no partner in sovereignty' would have sufficed, obviating His statement 'then He determined it precisely.'
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof for the exclusive creation of God. It cites Qur'an 25:2: 'He had no partner in sovereignty, and He created everything, then determined it precisely.' The author gives two arguments from this verse. First, since the verse says 'He created everything,' and human actions are things, it follows that God created them as well. Moreover, the word 'create' (khalq) cannot merely mean 'estimation' (taqdir) here, because if it did, the verse would redundantly say 'He estimated everything and then determined it'—an obvious tautology. Second, the verse begins by denying any partner in sovereignty. This seems to invite the question: granted He has no partner in sovereignty, but could not servants still create some things, namely their own actions? The verse immediately answers by saying 'He created everything'—so no other agent, including human beings, can create anything. If the verse's purpose were simply to assert that God is the sole sovereign, the first clause would have sufficed; the addition of 'He created everything' specifically refutes the idea that creatures can create their own actions.

Key Terms
خلق (khalq)
تقدير (taqdīr)
شريك (sharīk)
ملك (mulk)
أفعال العباد (afʿāl al-ʿibād)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'creation' in the verse could mean 'estimation' (taqdir), not bringing into existence.
Correction: The first argument shows that if 'creation' meant estimation, the verse would be redundant: 'He estimated everything and then determined it precisely' is a tautology. Therefore, 'creation' must mean bringing into existence.

Checkpoint
In the sixth proof, what is the first argument against interpreting 'creation' as 'estimation'?
A) It would make the phrase 'He created everything' mean 'He estimated everything', leading to redundancy with 'He determined it'
B) The verse uses the word 'everything' which cannot be estimated
C) The word 'creation' always means bringing into existence
D) The context of sovereignty requires literal creation

Key Takeaway
Through two arguments from one verse, the Qur'an affirms that Allah creates everything, including human actions, leaving no room for any other creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah creates every action you perform, including your acts of worship and your sins, how does that change the way you view your own achievements and failures? Will you still feel pride in your good deeds or despair over your mistakes?
?
You often assume you are the independent agent of your choices. But this lesson says Allah creates even your actions. How does this reshape your understanding of your need for Allah's help in every moment? What would it look like to live today with that awareness?
?
When you sin, do you attribute it solely to your own weakness or to Allah's decree? How can this verse drive you to sincere repentance and reliance on Allah's mercy, rather than self-condemnation or excuse?

Seventh Proof: The Argument from the Verse 'Is There Any Creator Other Than Allah?'
The author presents the seventh proof using Q. 35:3, then refutes an objection by expanding the concept of provision and appealing to the opponent's view on angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse 'Is there any creator other than Allah?' is used as a proof for tawhid and how an objection distinguishing 'creator' from 'originator' is refuted.

Core Idea
The verse proves only one creator; claiming angels create leads to contradiction.

This lesson examines the seventh proof in the text, based on Qur'an 35:3. The author presents the verse, then addresses an objection that distinguishes between 'creator' and 'originator'. The response ingeniously uses the concept of 'providing' to show that the opponent's own beliefs about angels lead to the very conclusion the verse denies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and the English translation once without analyzing. Let the flow of the argument settle in your mind.
Note the footnote marker (2) after 'heaven'.
2
Identify the structure
Observe that the passage has three parts: the verse citation, the objection (with two sub-points), and the answer to the first part of the objection.
The objection has two parts: scope of the verse and definition of 'creator'.
3
Analyze the objection
Understand the objector's claim: the verse only denies a creator who provides from heaven, not any creator. Also note the concession that the verse would contradict a creator but humans are only 'originators'.
The objector is making a semantic distinction to avoid the proof.
4
Follow the answer
The author expands the meaning of 'providing' to include enabling benefit, using the example of a sultan. Then applies this to angels: they enable rain, so they are providers. Since the opponent believes angels create their own actions, they become creators.
This creates a contradiction with the verse, so the objection fails.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument forces the opponent to accept that if angels create their actions and provide, there is a creator other than Allah, which the verse denies. Thus the verse proves tawhid without qualification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : قوله تعالى : هل من خالق غير الله ، يرزقكم من السماء (۲) ) فإن قيل : هذا يدل على أنه لا خالق غير الله يرزقنا من السماء ، ولا يدل على أنه لا خالق غير الله. سلمنا : أن هذه الآية تنافي إثبات خالق غير الله ، لكن العبد نسميه بالموجد والمحدث ، ولا نسميه بالخالق ، لأن الخلق عبارة عن التقدير العاري عن وجوه الخلل . وذلك في حق العبد محال والجواب عن الأول : إنه لو وجد خالق غير الله ، لوجد خالق غير الله يرزقنا من السماء ، لأنه يقال : رزق السلطان فلانا ، إذا أمكنه من التصرف فيها جعله رزقاً له . فكذا ههنا ، الملائكة الذين هم سكان السموات ، إذا سعوا في إنزال الأمطار ، فقد مكنوا أهل الأرض من الانتفاع بها . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : على مذهب الخصم : الملائكة يخلقون أفعال أنفسهم . وهم أيضاً يرزقون أهل الأرض على التفسير الذي ذكرناه ، وحينئذ يلزم القطع بأنه وجد خالق غير الله
The seventh proof: His statement, exalted is He: 'Is there any creator other than Allah who provides for you from the heaven?' [35:3]. If it is said: This indicates that there is no creator other than Allah who provides for us from the heaven, but it does not indicate that there is no creator other than Allah at all. We concede: this verse contradicts the affirmation of a creator other than Allah, but we call the servant 'the originator' (al-mujid) and 'the bringer into existence' (al-muhdith), and we do not call him 'the creator' (al-khaliq), because creation is an expression of estimation free from defects (al-taqdir al-‘ari ‘an wujuh al-khalal). And that is impossible with respect to the servant. The answer to the first [part]: If there existed a creator other than Allah, then there would exist a creator other than Allah who provides for us from the heaven, because it is said 'the sultan provided for so-and-so' when he enables him to dispose of something, making it a provision for him. Similarly here, the angels who are inhabitants of the heavens, when they strive in sending down rains, they have enabled the people of the earth to benefit from them. If this is established, we say: according to the doctrine of the opponent, the angels create their own actions. And they also provide for the people of the earth according to the interpretation we mentioned. At that point it necessarily follows that a creator other than Allah exists.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof using Qur'an 35:3: 'Is there any creator other than Allah who provides for you from the heaven?' An objector responds that the verse only denies a creator who also provides from heaven, not any creator at all. The objector also repeats the earlier distinction: humans are not called 'creators' but 'originators'. The answer refutes by focusing on the word 'provide' (yarzuq). It argues that providing includes enabling benefit. For example, when a sultan gives someone the use of something, he is said to provide for him. Therefore, if angels cause rain to fall, they enable people to benefit, so they would be providers. According to the opponent's own position that angels create their own actions, they would also be creators (since they determine their actions). Consequently, there would be creators other than God, which contradicts the verse. Thus the objector's position leads to an absurdity.

Key Terms
خالق (khāliq)
رزق (rizq)
موجد (mūjid)
محدث (muḥdith)
تقدير عار عن الخلل (taqdīr ʿārī ʿan al-khalal)
ملائكة (malāʾika)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse only denies a creator who provides from heaven, not any creator at all.
Correction: The answer shows that if there were a creator other than Allah, that creator would also provide in some way. Moreover, if angels provide (by enabling rain) and create their own actions, they would be creators, so the verse implicitly denies any creator other than Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in the seventh proof, why would the opponent's position force the conclusion that angels are creators?
A) Because angels cause rain and are providers
B) Because angels are called 'creators' in the Qur'an
C) Because angels are inhabitants of the heavens
D) Because the verse explicitly says angels are creators

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic proof against multiple creators holds strong because any attempt to limit its scope or redefine terms leads to contradictions based on the opponent's own premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Two Quranic Proofs that Only God Creates
The author cites two Quranic verses as proofs that creation belongs solely to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two Quranic proofs from Surat al-A'raf and Surat al-Baqarah that establish that creation belongs exclusively to Allah.

Core Idea
Only God creates — the Quran itself says so in two clear verses.

The author presents two Quranic verses as direct evidence for the doctrine that Allah alone is the Creator. The first verse from Surat al-A'raf (7:54) uses the phrase 'to Him belongs the creation and the command,' and the author argues that this implies a restriction (hasr) on creation. The second from Surat al-Baqarah (2:29) simply states that Allah created everything on earth for humankind. Let’s examine both proofs carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read both proofs in Arabic and English
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author numbers these as the eleventh and twelfth proofs.
2
Analyze the eleventh proof
Focus on the phrase 'ألا له الخلق' and how the author derives a restriction from it. Note his acknowledgment of the word 'الأمر' and his response.
The author admits they deviate from the apparent meaning of 'command' due to another proof, but insists the restriction on creation remains.
3
Analyze the twelfth proof
The second proof is simpler: 'خلق لكم ما في الأرض جميعا.' Consider how this verse alone suffices to show that God alone created everything on earth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الحادية عشر : قوله تعالى : ( ألا له الخلق والأمر (۱) ) فقوله : ألا له الخلق ) يفيد أنه لا خلق إلا لله. وغاية ما في الباب : أنا خالفناه هذا الظاهر ، في قوله تعالى : ( والأمر ( لدليل قام عليه ، فوجب أن يبقى هذا الحصر معتبراً في جانب الخلق . الحجة الثانية عشر : قوله تعالى : ( خلق لكم ما في الأرض جميعاً (۲) )
The eleventh proof: His saying, exalted: (Surely to Him belongs the creation and the command). So His saying: (Surely to Him belongs the creation) indicates that there is no creation except for Allah. And the most that can be said in this regard is that we have deviated from this apparent meaning in His saying (and the command) due to a proof that has been established, so it is necessary that this restriction remain considered in the aspect of creation. The twelfth proof: His saying, exalted: (He created for you all that is on the earth).
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct Quranic proofs (ḥujja) that only God creates. The eleventh proof cites verse 7:54 'Surely to Him belongs the creation and the command.' The author argues that the phrase 'to Him belongs the creation' implies a restriction (ḥaṣr) that creation is exclusively God's. He acknowledges that in the same verse, 'the command' is also attributed to God, and they might deviate from the apparent meaning of 'the command' due to a proof, but the restriction on 'creation' must remain intact. The twelfth proof cites verse 2:29 'He created for you all that is on the earth,' which affirms that God is the sole creator of everything on earth. Both verses are used to support the Ashʿarī position that God alone is the Creator, and human actions are not created by humans.

الخلق
الأمر
الحصر

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The author argues that the restriction (hasr) applies specifically to creation, while the command is attributed to God but we may deviate from its apparent meaning due to another proof.

Key Takeaway
The Quran explicitly states that creation is exclusively Allah's, as seen in Surat al-A'raf 7:54 and Surat al-Baqarah 2:29.

Exclusive Divine Creation from 'A-la lahu al-khalq'
The author cites Quranic verse to argue exclusive divine creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse 'To Him belongs the creation and the command' serves as a proof for exclusive divine creation and how the apparent meaning is preserved for creation despite a figurative interpretation for 'command'.

Core Idea
The verse proves creation is exclusively God's, even if 'command' is interpreted otherwise.

In this proof, the author draws on a clear Quranic verse that explicitly assigns creation solely to God. He acknowledges that the same verse also mentions 'the command', which some interpret figuratively, but argues that this does not affect the restrictiveness regarding creation. Let's examine how this argument works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the author's use of 'الحصر' (restriction) and how he handles the two parts of the verse.
The verse is from Surah Al-A'raf (7:54).
2
Identify the two elements: creation and command
Notice that the verse mentions both 'الخلق' (creation) and 'الأمر' (command). The author argues that the restriction applies to creation despite a possible different interpretation of command.
3
Understand the logical move
The author concedes that they have interpreted 'the command' figuratively (due to another proof), but insists that this does not undermine the apparent restriction for creation. This is a key nuance.
The Arabic phrase 'لما خالفناه هذا الظاهر في الأمر' shows the concession.
4
Reflect on the stronger implication
Consider why this argument is effective: even if one part of the verse is interpreted metaphorically, the clear meaning for creation remains intact, providing a strong proof for Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الحادية عشر : قوله تعالى : ( ألا له الخلق والأمر ) فقوله : ( ألا له الخلق ) يفيد أنه لا خلق إلا لله. وغاية ما في الباب : أنا خالفناه هذا الظاهر ، في قوله تعالى : ( والأمر ) لدليل قام عليه ، فوجب أن يبقى هذا الحصر معتبراً في جانب الخلق .
The eleventh proof: His saying, exalted: 'Indeed, to Him belongs the creation and the command.' So His saying: 'Indeed, to Him belongs the creation' indicates that there is no creation except for God. And the utmost in this matter is that we have opposed this apparent meaning in the case of His saying: 'and the command' because of a proof that has been established, so it is necessary that this restriction remains effective with respect to creation.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents a Quranic argument for the exclusive divine creation of all things. The verse 'To Him belongs the creation and the command' is taken as an explicit declaration that creation is God's alone. The author acknowledges that the phrase also includes 'the command', and they admit that they have interpreted the 'command' part figuratively due to a proof (likely referring to human free will in matters of command). However, this does not affect the clear meaning regarding creation; the restriction of creation to God remains intact. Thus the verse is used to support the Ash'ari position that all actions are created by God.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'To Him belongs the creation and the command' definitively establishes that all creation is exclusively God's, even if the term 'command' is understood in a different sense.

Answering Objections on Divine Purpose and Human Action
Author refutes objections about divine purpose and human action, emphasizing God's sovereignty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari response to objections regarding God's creation of falsehood and the nature of divine purpose, and the crucial distinction between creating falsehood and creating for a false purpose.

Core Idea
God creates not for a purpose but by sovereignty; only creating for a false purpose is denied.

In this passage, the author addresses several objections raised against the Ash'ari view that God is the sole creator of all actions. A key move is the careful reading of the Quranic text: God does not create for a false purpose, but His creation is not contingent upon any purpose. This distinction safeguards divine sovereignty while answering the objection about falsehood. The major fallacy about human moral responsibility is acknowledged and deferred.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first objection and its refutation
Read the opening lines where the author deals with the objection that actions cannot be attributed to the earth if God is not a body. He refers to a previous discussion that established that actions are truly on earth.
This shows the interconnected nature of theological arguments.
2
Analyze the second objection about falsehood
Examine how the author responds to the claim that scripture says God did not create falsehood. He points out the grammatical structure: 'bāṭilan' is an object of purpose.
The Arabic grammar is crucial here.
3
Grasp the distinction between two statements
Understand the difference between 'He does not create falsehood' and 'He does not create for a false purpose'. Scripture only denies the latter.
This distinction is the core of the refutation.
4
Recognize the principle of divine action
The author asserts that God creates by virtue of His divinity and sovereignty, not for any purpose at all. Therefore, He cannot have a false purpose.
This establishes God's absolute freedom.
5
Note the major fallacy deferred
The opponents' main argument—that if God creates all actions, human moral responsibility collapses—is acknowledged and postponed to a later discussion.
This indicates the systematic structure of the work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : قوله : ( الشيء لا يوصف بأنه بين السماء والأرض ، إلا إذا كان جسماً ، فجوابه : ما تقدم في بيان أفعال العباد : أنه يصدق عليها أنها في الأرض . قوله : ( النص دل على أنه تعالى ما خلق الباطل ، قلنا : النص دل على أنه تعالى ما خلق شيئاً لغرض باطل . لأن قوله : ( باطلا ، مفعول له وعندنا : أن الأمر كذلك. لأن عندنا أنه تعالى لا يخلق شيئاً لغرض اصلاً . وإذا لم يكن له غرض ، لم يكن له غرض باطل . بل عندنا أنه تعالى يخلق الأشياء بحكم الإلهية والمالكية . فإنه ليس لأحد عليه أمر ولا نهي وبالجملة : ففرق بين قولنا : لا يخلق الباطل ، وبين قولنا : لا يخلق شيئاً لغرض باطل . والمذكور في النص هو الثاني ، لا الأول . وذلك لا ينفعهم ولا يضرنا . وأما بقية أسئلتهم فهي إشارة إلى أن العبد لو لم يكن موجداً لأفعال نفسه ، لامتنع أمره ونهيه ومدحه وذمه . وهذا هو شبهتهم الكبرى ، وسيجيء الجواب عنها ، إن شاء الله تعالى .
And the answer: His statement: (A thing is not described as being between heaven and earth unless it is a body), its answer is: what preceded in the explanation of the actions of servants: that it is true of them that they are on the earth. His statement: (The text indicates that He, exalted, did not create falsehood), we say: The text indicates that He, exalted, did not create anything for a false purpose. Because His statement: (false) is a [purpose] object, and with us: the matter is so. Because with us: He, exalted, does not create anything for any purpose at all. And if He has no purpose, then He has no false purpose. Rather, with us: He, exalted, creates things by virtue of divinity and sovereignty. For no one has command or prohibition over Him. And in summary: There is a difference between our saying: He does not create falsehood, and our saying: He does not create anything for a false purpose. And what is mentioned in the text is the second, not the first. That does not benefit them nor harm us. As for the rest of their questions, they are an indication that if the servant were not the one who brings his own actions into existence, then his command, prohibition, praise, and blame would be impossible. This is their major fallacy, and the answer to it will come, if God wills.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a series of answers to objections raised against the Ash'ari position that God is the sole creator of all actions, including human actions. The author begins by addressing an objection: if God is not a body, how can actions be described as 'on earth'? He replies that previous discussion on human actions established that actions are truly attributed to the earth (i.e., they occur on earth). Then, the objection that scripture says God did not create falsehood is countered by clarifying the grammatical structure: 'falsehood' in the verse is an object of purpose, meaning God did not create for a false purpose. The author asserts that God creates without any purpose at all—His actions are by virtue of His divinity and sovereignty, not subject to any external command or prohibition. Thus, the distinction is between 'creating falsehood' and 'creating for a false purpose'; only the latter is denied by scripture, and that does not contradict the Ash'ari view. Finally, the author notes that the main objection—that if God creates all actions, human moral responsibility collapses—is the opponents' major argument, but its refutation will come later.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that denying God creates for a purpose means He creates evil arbitrarily.
Correction: The author distinguishes between purpose and inherent divine wisdom; God's creation is not purposeless but not contingent on external purposes.

B) 'Creating falsehood' is denied, while 'creating for a false purpose' is affirmed.
C) The Quranic text denies 'creating for a false purpose', not 'creating falsehood' per se.
D) The author affirms both are possible for God.

Key Takeaway
God's creation is not for any purpose, but by His absolute sovereignty; the Quran only denies that He creates for a false purpose, not that He creates what may appear false to us.

Alternative Interpretations of the Verse 'Rabbana wa-j'alna muslimayni'
Three alternative interpretations of 'make us Muslims' and rejection of literal creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a Qur'anic verse about God 'making' people Muslims can be interpreted in multiple ways without implying direct creation of faith.

Core Idea
The verse about 'making Muslims' is not a proof for divine creation of faith; it can mean honor, kindness, reward, or enablement.

In this passage, the author counters an opponent who claims that the Qur'anic phrase 'make us Muslims' shows that God directly creates human faith. The author offers three or four alternative, plausible interpretations that avoid this implication. By the end, you'll see that insisting on the literal 'creation' reading is arbitrary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the author's use of 'thumma naqulu' to introduce alternatives.
2
Identify the three main interpretations
The author lists three interpretations: raising to the status of Muslims in honor, increasing kindnesses in the world, and increasing reward in the afterlife. Find each in the text.
These are numbered in the commentary.
3
Notice the fourth interpretation
After the three, the author adds a fourth about enabling Islam in the future. See how it is introduced.
The author says 'also, it is not unlikely to carry it on the enabling of Islam in future time'.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that since the verse can bear all these meanings, insisting on the literal 'creation' meaning is arbitrary.
The phrase 'تحكماً محضاً' (pure arbitrariness) is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: وأيضاً: يمكن حمل قوله: (ربنا واجعلنا مسلمين لك) على أن يكون المطلوب تنزيلهما منزلة المسلمين في المدح والتعظيم، أو في تكثير الألطاف في الدنيا، أو في تكثير الثواب في الآخرة. والمسلم إن كان يستحق كل ذلك على الله تعالى، إلا أنه لا يبعد أن يقال: إنه متى سأل الله تعالى ذلك، فإنه لأجل ذلك السؤال يستحق مزيداً في هذه الأمور. ثم نقول: أيضاً: لا يبعد حمله على التمكين من الإسلام في الـزمـان المستقبل، ولا يبعد أن يتوقف حسن تمكينها في المستقبل على إقدامها على ذلك السؤال. فثبت بما ذكرنا: أن قوله: (ربنا واجعلنا مسلمين لك) محتمل لكل واحد من هذه الوجوه الثلاثة، فكان الجرم بحمله على الإيجاد والتكوين تحكماً محضاً.
Then we say: And also: it is possible to carry His saying: (Our Lord, make us Muslims to You) such that the intended meaning is to bring them down to the station of Muslims in praise and glorification, or in multiplying divine kindnesses (al-altaf) in the world, or in multiplying reward in the hereafter. And the Muslim, even if he deserves all that from God Almighty, yet it is not unlikely that it be said: when he asks God Almighty for that, then because of that question he deserves an increase in these matters. Then we say: also, it is not unlikely to carry it on the enabling of Islam in future time, and it is not unlikely that the goodness of enabling them in the future depends on their undertaking that question. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that His saying: (Our Lord, make us Muslims to You) is open to each one of these three aspects, and thus the crime of carrying it on creation and bringing into being is pure arbitrariness.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is an argument presenting alternative interpretations of the Qur'anic verse 'Rabbana wa-j'alna muslimayni laka' (Our Lord, make us Muslims to You). The opponent had argued that this verse proves God creates human faith directly (الإيجاد والتكوين). The author responds by offering three (or four) plausible meanings that do not require that reading. First, the verse could be a request for God to raise them to the status of Muslims in terms of praise and honour (تنزيلهما منزلة المسلمين في المدح والتعظيم). Second, it could be a request for an increase in divine kindnesses (الألطاف) in this world. Third, it could be a request for an increase in reward in the afterlife. The author notes that even though a Muslim already deserves these things, the asking itself can earn a further increase. Fourth, the verse could be interpreted as a request for God to enable them to remain Muslims in the future. The author concludes that the verse is ambiguous and can bear all these meanings, so insisting on the literal meaning of 'making' as creation (الإيجاد والتكوين) is an arbitrary imposition (تحكماً محضاً).

Misconception: The verse 'Rabbana wa-j'alna muslimayni' necessarily implies that God directly creates the state of being Muslim in a person.
Correction: The author shows that the verb 'ja'ala' (make) can be interpreted in multiple ways that do not involve direct creation, such as granting honor, kindness, reward, or enablement. Thus, the verse does not prove divine creation of faith.

Key Takeaway

The Necessity of Action Through Divine Grace
Argues that when divine grace reaches a certain degree, the action becomes necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine grace (lutf) logically necessitates action through a trilemma argument.

Core Idea
Divine grace combined with motive makes action rationally necessary.

This passage presents a rigorous logical proof that when divine grace reaches a certain degree, the action becomes obligatory. The argument eliminates alternatives through a trilemma, demonstrating that the only consistent possibility is necessity. This aligns with the Ashʿarī doctrine that the combination of power and motive necessitates action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical structure and the trilemma presented.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the trilemma
The passage presents three possibilities when a certain degree of preponderance is obtained: the action becomes obligatory, impossible, or neither. Identify each branch.
The third branch is the key to the argument.
3
Analyze the third case
Focus on the case where the action is neither obligatory nor impossible. The author investigates why the action occurs at one time rather than another, leading to a contradiction unless the original grace alone causes necessity.
This step involves a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Conclude the necessity
The argument concludes that when divine grace is present, the action is obligatory; when absent, it is impossible. This supports the doctrine that power plus motive necessitates action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لتلك الألطاف أثر في الترجيح. فنقول: متى حصل الرجحان، فقد حصل الوجوب. وذلك لأن مع حصول ذلك القدر من الترجيح، إما أن يجب الفصل أو يمتنع، أو لا يجب ولا يمتنع. فإن وجب فهو المطلوب. وإن امتنع فهو مانع مرجح. وإن لم يجب ولم يمتنع، فحينئذ يمكن وقوع الفعل معه تارة، ووقوعه أخرى. فاختصاص أحد الوقتين بالوقوع والوقت الآخر باللا وقوع، إن كان لانضمام أمر آخر إليه كان اللطف المؤثر في الترجيح هو المجموع الحاصل مما حصل. ومن هذه الضمانة فلا يكون الذي فرضناه حاصلاً، مؤثراً البتة في شيء من الترجيح، وكنا فرضناه كذلك. هذا خلف. وإن لم يكن لانضمام قيد آخر إليه، لزم الرجحان من غير مرجح. وهو محال. فثبت: أن عند حصول ذلك اللطف يجب حصول الفعل، وعند عدمه يمتنع. وذلك يعود إلى ما ذكرناه من أن حصول الفعل عند مجموع القدرة والداعي واجب. وذلك هو عين مذهبنا
These divine graces (alṭāf) have an effect in giving preponderance (tarjīḥ). We say: when preponderance occurs, obligation (wujūb) occurs. That is because with the occurrence of that amount of preponderance, either the action becomes obligatory or impossible, or neither obligatory nor impossible. If it becomes obligatory, that is the desired. If it becomes impossible, then there is a preventing preponderant factor. If it is neither obligatory nor impossible, then at that time the action may occur with it sometimes and not occur at other times. The specification of one of the two times for occurrence and the other for non-occurrence—if it is due to the addition of another thing, then the divine grace effective in giving preponderance is the total obtained from what was obtained. But from this guarantee, what we assumed to be obtained is not at all effective in any preponderance, while we assumed it to be so. This is a contradiction (khulf). And if it is not due to the addition of another condition, then preponderance would occur without a preponderator (murjīḥ), which is impossible. Thus it is established that when that divine grace is obtained, the occurrence of the action is obligatory, and when it is absent, it is impossible. This returns to what we mentioned: that the occurrence of the action with the combination of power (qudra) and motive (dāʿī) is obligatory. That is precisely our doctrine.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents a logical proof for the necessity of action when divine grace (lutf) is present. The author begins by asserting that these graces have an effect in giving preponderance (tarjīḥ) to an action. The argument proceeds through a trilemma: once a certain degree of preponderance is obtained, either the action becomes obligatory (wājib), impossible (mumtaniʿ), or neither. If obligatory, that is the desired conclusion. If impossible, that indicates a preventing preponderant factor. If neither, then the action can occur at one time and not at another. The author then focuses on this third case: why does the action occur at one time rather than another? If it is because of another factor added to the grace, then the effective grace is actually the combination, meaning the originally assumed grace alone was not sufficient—this contradicts the initial assumption that the grace itself was the cause of preponderance. If there is no additional factor, then preponderance would occur without any preponderator, which is impossible. Therefore, the only consistent possibility is that when that grace is present, the action becomes obligatory, and when absent, it becomes impossible. This conclusion aligns with the author's doctrine that the combination of power (qudra) and motive (dāʿī) necessitates the action.

Key Terms
لطف
ترجيح
وجوب
قدرة
داعي
مانع مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine grace compels action by force, eliminating human choice.
Correction: The argument does not claim that God compels action; rather, it shows that divine grace combined with motive makes action necessary within the causal framework.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a certain degree of preponderance is obtained and the action is neither obligatory nor impossible, what problem arises?
A) The action must be impossible.
B) The action must be obligatory.
C) The specification of one time over another would require either an additional factor or preponderance without a preponderator.
D) Divine grace is ineffective.

Key Takeaway
Divine grace, when present, makes the action obligatory because any other alternative leads to contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Look honestly at your own life: are you relying more on your own strength to obey Allah, or are you actively seeking His divine grace (lutf) to make obedience flow from you? What would change if you truly believed that without His grace, your obedience is impossible?

The argument destroys every excuse: once grace and motive are present, action is obligatory. Are there areas in your life where you have all the motive and yet you act as if grace is insufficient? Proof from Verse 'And Turn in Mercy to Us' for Divine Creation of Repentance
Argues that repentance is created by God using verse and rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural and rational arguments for the Ashʿarī position that repentance is created by God, and how the objection from God's command to repent is resolved.

Core Idea
Repentance is from God: He must create the motive in us for it to happen.

This lesson presents a second proof from the Qur'an and reason that repentance (tawba) is created by God. The author cites a verse where Prophet Abraham and Ishmael ask God to 'turn in mercy to us,' arguing that if repentance were the servant's own act, such a request would be absurd. Then an objection is raised from God's command to repent, and the author provides a rational explanation showing why repentance must ultimately be from God's causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: verse citation, objection, reconciliation, and rational proof.
Pay attention to the logical flow: scriptural argument → objection → response.
2
Identify the scriptural proof and objection
Find where the author uses the verse 'And turn in mercy to us' to argue that repentance must be from God. Then identify the objection based on the command to repent.
The objection seems to create a contradiction; note how the author handles it.
3
Understand the rational proof
The author provides a rational argument based on motive (dāʿī). Understand the two premises: (1) a capable agent cannot act without a motive, (2) when a preponderant motive is present, action is necessary. Then see how this leads to the conclusion that repentance is created by God.
This is a key Ashʿarī argument about human action and divine causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: قوله تعالى في آخر هذه الآية: (وتب علينا. إنك أنت التواب الرحيم) ولو كانت التوبة بخلق العبد، لكان طلبها من الله جارياً مجرى أن يقول العبد: يا إلهي أفعل ما أنا فاعله. فإن قيل: هذا معارض بما أن الله تعالى طلب التوبة منا. فقال: (يا أيها الذين آمنوا توبوا إلى الله توبة نصوحاً) ولو كانت التوبة فعلاً لله تعالى، لكان طلبها من العبد محالاً. ولما ثبت التعارض، وجب التوفيق بينهما. فنحمل قوله تعالى: (وتب علينا) على التوفيق، وفعل الألطاف، أو على أن الله تعالى يقبل التوبة من العبد. والجواب: الترجيح معنا. لأن دليل العقل يقوي قولنا: إن التوبة لا تحصل إلا بخلق الله تعالى. وبيانه من وجهين: الأول: إنا أقمنا الدلائل القاهرة على أن القادر يمتنع أن يصدر عنه الفعل إلا عند حصول الداعي، وأقمنا الدلائل القاهرة على أن عند حصول الداعي المرجح يجب الفعل. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: إنه تعالى عندما لم يخلق للعبد داعية إلى التوبة، امتنع صدور التوبة عنه، ولما خلق فيه تلك الداعية، وجب صدور التوبة عنه. وعلى هذا التقدير تكون التوبة إنما حصلت بتحصيل الله تعالى. إما بواسطة، أو بغير واسطة
The second proof: The statement of God Almighty at the end of this verse: 'And turn in mercy to us. Indeed, You are the Oft-Returning, the Merciful.' If repentance (tawba) were by the creation of the servant, then requesting it from God would be like the servant saying: 'My God, do what I am doing.' If it is said: This is contradicted by the fact that God Almighty demanded repentance from us. He said: 'O you who have believed, repent to God with sincere repentance.' If repentance were an act of God Almighty, then demanding it from the servant would be impossible. Since contradiction is established, reconciliation between them is necessary. So we interpret God's statement 'And turn in mercy to us' as referring to granting success (tawfīq) and performing divine graces (afʿāl al-alṭāf), or that God Almighty accepts repentance from the servant. The answer: Preponderance is with us. Because the evidence of reason (dalīl al-ʿaql) strengthens our statement: that repentance only occurs through the creation of God Almighty. Its explanation has two aspects: First: We have established conclusive proofs that the capable agent (qādir) cannot produce an action except when a motive (dāʿī) is present, and we have established conclusive proofs that when the preponderant motive (al-dāʿī al-murajjiḥ) is present, the action becomes necessary. When this is established, we say: When He Almighty did not create a motive for repentance in the servant, the occurrence of repentance from him is impossible; and when He created that motive in him, the occurrence of repentance from him becomes necessary. On this basis, repentance occurs only through God's causation (taḥṣīl). Either through an intermediary or without an intermediary.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof for the doctrine that repentance (tawba) is created by God. The author cites the verse 'And turn in mercy to us' (Quran 2:128) and argues: if repentance were the servant's own creation, then asking God for it would be absurd—like asking God to do what the servant himself does. Then an objection is raised: God also commands repentance from humans (e.g., 'Repent to God with sincere repentance'), which would be meaningless if repentance were God's act. The objector suggests reconciliation: interpret the verse about God 'turning' as granting success or outpouring graces, or as God accepting repentance. The author responds that preponderance lies with his position because reason supports it. He then provides two rational proofs (though they are combined in one explanation). The first (or both) is that a capable agent cannot act without a motive, and when a preponderant motive exists, action is necessary. Therefore, God must create the motive for repentance in the servant; without it, repentance is impossible; with it, it becomes necessary. Consequently, repentance is ultimately caused by God, either directly or indirectly. This reinforces the Ashʿarī position that human actions are created by God and that human acquisition (kasb) is not independent.

Key Terms
توبة
توفيق
ألطاف
داعي
قادر
تحصيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the objection is about God commanding what He creates (e.g., commanding the impossible), but the real issue is inconsistency between asking God for repentance and being commanded to repent.
Correction: The author resolves this by showing that the command and the creation are distinct: God commands us to repent, but the actual ability to repent comes from God creating the motive in us.

Checkpoint
According to the author's rational proof, why must repentance be created by God?
A) Because the servant cannot repent without God's grace.
B) Because repentance is a physical act that requires divine power.
C) Because the servant can only act when God creates a motive in him, otherwise action is impossible.
D) Because the verse 'And turn in mercy to us' explicitly states so.

Key Takeaway
Repentance is created by God because the servant cannot act without God creating the motive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The proof shows that when God does not create the motive, repentance is impossible. Think of a sin you struggle with. Are you waiting for God to create the motive, or are you trying to force it yourself? How does this truth change your prayer tonight?
?
The verse 'And turn in mercy to us' is a plea for God to create repentance. Read that verse as if it were your own prayer. Do you truly believe that your repentance depends entirely on God? If so, what will you ask Him for specifically when you pray?

The Preserved Tablet and the Divine Source of Motives
Argument from Preserved Tablet and refutation of causal link between miracles and enmity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all motives, whether love or enmity, are created by God, and that prophetic miracles themselves do not necessitate enmity.

Core Idea
Motives of love and enmity come directly from God, not from outward gifts.

This passage addresses a subtle theological question: if God grants prophets miracles and virtues, why do some people oppose them? The author argues that those gifts do not cause enmity; rather, God Himself casts motives of love or enmity into hearts. This is supported by a rational argument and the Quranic verse about every prophet having enemies. The discussion also touches on the Preserved Tablet and the necessity of what is written therein.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from the Preserved Tablet argument to the discussion of motives.
2
Identify the two main arguments
The passage has two parts: (1) the necessity of what is in the Preserved Tablet, and (2) the refutation of the idea that prophetic gifts cause enmity. Find where each part begins and ends.
3
Reflect on the conclusion that all motives are from God
The author concludes that everything is from God. Consider how this connects to the Quranic verse mentioned. Understand why this is considered a decisive rational proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في اللوح المحفوظ، كان حصوله واجباً ، وتركه ممتنعاً . لما بينا أن تركه يقتضي انقلاب خبر الله ، الصدق : كذباً . وإنه محال . وأما الذي ذكروه في الوجه الثاني فضعيف جداً . وذلك لأن تخصيص الرسل بتلك الكرامات وتلك الفضائل لا يوجب حصول العداوة . ألا ترى أن تلك الفضائل هي التي صارت أسباباً قوية الحصول المحبة الشديدة في قلوب الأولياء . ولو كان حصول تلك الفضائل ، موجباً لحصول العداوة ، لكان السبب الواحد موجباً حصول ضدين . وهو محال . بل الحق : أن ذلك لا يوجب ، لا حصول المحبة ، ولا حصول العداوة . وأن الموجب لحصول المحبة : إلقاء دواعي المحبة في القلوب ، والموجب لحصول العداوة : إلقاء دواعي العداوة في القلوب . وعلى هذا التقدير يكون الكل من الله . وعند استحضار مسألة الداعي على الوجه الذي لخصناه ، يظهر أن ظاهر قوله تعالى : ( وكذلك جعلنا لكل نبي عدوا من المجرمين ) متأكداً هذا البرهان العقلي القاطع ، الذي لا يقدر أحد على التشكيك فيه بوجه من الوجوه . وبالله التوفيق .
In the Preserved Tablet, its occurrence was necessary, and its omission was impossible. Because we have shown that its omission would necessitate the change of God's truthful word into falsehood, and that is impossible. As for what they mentioned in the second aspect, it is very weak. That is because specifying the messengers with those miracles and those virtues does not necessitate the occurrence of enmity. Do you not see that those virtues are what have become strong causes for the occurrence of intense love in the hearts of the saints? And if the occurrence of those virtues necessitated the occurrence of enmity, then one cause would necessitate the occurrence of two opposites, and that is impossible. Rather, the truth is that that does not necessitate either the occurrence of love or the occurrence of enmity. And that what necessitates the occurrence of love is the casting of motives of love into hearts, and what necessitates the occurrence of enmity is the casting of motives of enmity into hearts. On this basis, everything is from God. And when considering the issue of the motive (al-dāʿī) in the manner we have summarized, it becomes evident that the apparent meaning of His statement, the Exalted: (And thus We have made for every prophet an enemy from among the criminals) is confirmed by this decisive rational proof, which no one is able to cast doubt upon in any way. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents an argument about the nature of motives and divine causation. It begins by stating that if something were predetermined in the Preserved Tablet, its occurrence is necessary and its omission impossible, because omission would turn God's truthful report into falsehood. Then it addresses a second objection: the special gifts given to prophets (miracles and virtues) do not necessarily cause enmity. If they did, one cause would produce two opposites (love in saints and enmity in others), which is impossible. The correct view is that these gifts themselves do not cause love or enmity; rather, love is caused by God casting motives of love into hearts, and enmity by casting motives of enmity. Thus, all actions and motives ultimately come from God. This rational argument supports the Quranic verse that God made enemies for every prophet. The author emphasizes that this is a decisive rational proof that no one can doubt.

Correction: The gifts themselves do not cause enmity; rather, God casts motives of enmity or love into hearts. One cause cannot produce two opposite effects.

The Tenth Proof: 'Jacl' as Transformation in Quran 5:60
The author presents the tenth proof from Quran 5:60 and argues that 'jacl' must mean transformation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses Quran 5:60 to argue that the verb 'jacl' must mean transformation, not mere judgment, and why this is a key proof for divine causation of actions.

Core Idea
The same verb 'jacl' in a verse cannot mean different things—it must consistently mean transformation.

This lesson introduces the tenth proof from the author's work, which centers on Quran 5:60. The verse describes Allah making some people into apes, swine, and worshippers of Taghut. The author argues that the verb 'jacl' (made) must be interpreted consistently across the verse as transformative 'making' rather than mere judgment or naming. This interpretation is crucial for understanding divine action in creating human attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and the author's claim
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning to the point where the author states that 'jacl' cannot mean judgment or naming. Notice how he bases his argument on the parallel use of the verb for apes, swine, and worshippers.
The verse is Quran 5:60, a clear statement of divine making.
2
Understand why 'jacl' cannot mean mere judgment
The author explains that when 'jacl' is used for apes and swine, it clearly means transformation (taṣbīr and qalb). To interpret it differently for 'worshippers of Taghut' would be inconsistent.
The principle is that a single word in one utterance cannot be used in both literal and metaphorical senses simultaneously.
3
Reflect on the consistency principle
Consider why the author insists on the same meaning for all three instances. This is a key logical rule in kalam: avoiding istiʿmāl al-lafẓ al-wāḥid fī ḥaqīqatihi wa-majāzihi maʿan.
This principle prevents ambiguity and ensures coherent interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : قوله تعالى : قل : هل أنبئكم بشر من ذلك ، مثوبة عند الله من لعنه الله ، وغضب عليه ، وجعل منهم القردة والخنازير ، وعبد الطاغوت ) وهذا تصريح بأنه تعالى جعلهم عبدة الطاغوت . ولا يمكن تفسير هذا الجعل بالحكم والتسمية . لأنه تعالى ذكر صيغة جعل ، ثم أسند هذه الصيغة إلى القردة ، ثم إلى الخنازير، ثم إلى عبدة الطاغوت . ولا شك أن المراد من جعل ، عند إسنادها إلى القردة والخنازير، هي التصبير ، والقلب من صفة إلى صفة . فوجب أن يكون المراد بإسنادها إلى عبدة الطاغوت هذا المعنى أيضاً ، وإلا لزم استعمال اللفظ الواحد دفعة في حقيقته ومجازه معاً . وإن ذلك محال . فإن قيل : النص دل على أنه تعالى جعل من عبد الطاغوت . فصيغة جعل ههنا متعدية إلى مفعول واحد ، فكانت بمعنى الإحداث . فيكون المعنى : أنه تعالى خلق من عبد الطاغوت . ولا نزاع فيه . وليس في الآية دلالة على أنه تعالى خلق عبادة الطاغوت فيهم . والجواب عنه من وجهين : الأول : إنه تعالى قال : ( وجعل منهم القردة ) وجعل الشيء من شيء آخر. معناه : إزالة الصفة الأولى ، وإحداث الصفة الثانية . وإذا كان كذلك ، لم يكن هذا الجعل بمعنى الإحداث، بل بمعنى تصيير الشيء شيئا آخر . وإذا كان من الجعل المذكور في هذه الآية هذا المعنى ، وجب أيضاً أن يكون هو المواد في قوله : ( وعبد الطاغوت ) وإلا لزم استعمال اللفظ المشترك في كلا مفهوميه معاً . وإنه لا يجوز . وإذا ثبت هذا وجب أن يكون معنى الآية : إنه تعالى جعلهم عبد الطاغوت ) وعند هذا يحصل المطلوب . الثاني : إنه تعالى إنما ذكر ذلك في معرض الذم لهم . ولو كان المقصود منه أنه تعالى خلق ذواتهم، لما كان فيه مذمة لهم البتة . لأن المذمة في أن يقال : إنه تعالى جعلهم موصوفين بعبادة الطاغوت. وحينئذ يحصل المقصود . وبالله التوفيق
The tenth proof: His statement, the Exalted: 'Say: Shall I inform you of something worse than that as recompense from Allah? Those whom Allah has cursed and with whom He is angry and made of them apes and swine and worshippers of Taghut.' This is a clear statement that Allah made them worshippers of Taghut. And it is not possible to explain this 'making' as judgment and naming, because Allah mentioned the form of 'making,' then attributed this form to apes, then to swine, then to worshippers of Taghut. And there is no doubt that the intended meaning of 'making' when attributed to apes and swine is transformation and change from one attribute to another. So it must be that the intended meaning when attributed to worshippers of Taghut is this same meaning as well, otherwise it would entail using the same word once in its literal sense and in its metaphorical sense together, and that is impossible. If it is said: The text indicates that Allah made from those who worship Taghut. The form of 'making' here is transitive with one object, so it means creation. The meaning would be: Allah created the one who worships Taghut. There is no dispute about that. And there is no evidence in the verse that Allah created the worship of Taghut in them. The answer is from two aspects: First: Allah said 'And made of them apes' — and making a thing from another thing means removing the first attribute and creating the second attribute. If that is so, then this making is not in the sense of creation, but rather in the sense of turning a thing into another thing. And since the 'making' mentioned in this verse has this meaning, it must also be the intended meaning in His statement 'and worshippers of Taghut,' otherwise it would entail using the ambiguous word (mushtarak) in both of its meanings together, and that is not permissible. And when this is established, the meaning of the verse becomes: Allah made them worshippers of Taghut, and at this point the desired conclusion is obtained. Second: Allah only mentioned this in the context of blame for them. And if the intended meaning were that He created their persons, there would be no blame in that at all, because the blame is in saying that Allah made them described with worship of Taghut. Thus the desired conclusion is obtained. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof (al-ḥujja al-ʿāshira) from Quran 5:60. The author argues that the verb 'jacl' (made) is used in the verse to refer to transforming people into apes and swine and also making them worshippers of Taghut. He first cites the verse: 'Say: Shall I inform you of something worse than that as recompense from Allah? Those whom Allah has cursed and with whom He is angry and made of them apes and swine and worshippers of Taghut.' The author asserts that this clearly shows Allah made them worshippers of Taghut. He then argues that it is impossible to interpret this 'jacl' as mere judgment or naming, because the same verb form is used for apes, swine, and worshippers. When applied to apes and swine, 'jacl' means transformation (taṣbīr) and change (qalb) from one attribute to another. To avoid using the same word in both its literal and metaphorical senses simultaneously, the same meaning of transformation must apply to 'worshippers of Taghut.' An objection is then raised: the text only says 'made of them' (jaʿala minhum) which is transitive with one object, meaning creation (īḥdāth) — i.e., Allah created the persons who worship Taghut, not the act of worship itself. The author responds with two arguments: (1) The phrase 'made of them apes' (wa-jaʿala minhum al-qirada) means removing the first attribute (humanity) and creating the second (apeness), which is transformation (taṣyīr), not mere creation. Therefore, the same meaning applies to 'worshippers of Taghut' to avoid using 'jacl' in two different senses (ambiguous word, mushtarak). Thus the verse means Allah made them into worshippers of Taghut. (2) The verse is in a context of blame (dhamm). If it only meant creating their persons, there would be no blame; the blame lies in attributing to them the quality of worshipping Taghut, which proves that Allah directly made them have that attribute.

Key Terms
الحجة العاشرة
جعل
تصبير
قلب
لفظ مشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'jacl' in the verse means mere judgment or naming, not actual transformation.
Correction: The author argues that since 'jacl' is used in the same sentence for apes and swine (where it undoubtedly means transformation), it must also mean transformation for worshippers of Taghut, otherwise the word would be used in two different senses.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author insists that 'jacl' must mean transformation for 'worshippers of Taghut'?
A) Because the verse explicitly says so.
B) To avoid using the same word in its literal and metaphorical senses together.
C) Because 'jacl' always means transformation in the Quran.
D) Because the author prefers a metaphorical interpretation.

Key Takeaway
The consistency of the verb 'jacl' across the verse requires that it mean transformation in all three instances, a key proof for divine making of attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever interpret God's actions in your life as mere labels rather than actual transformations? Reflect on a time you might have dismissed a change in your character as just a label, but this verse demands you see it as His direct making. What would change if you truly believed that every attribute you have is directly made by Allah?
?
If Allah's making of people into worshippers is real, how does that confront your own sense of free will? Are you comfortable with the idea that He transforms hearts directly, including yours? Commit to one area today where you will recognize His direct hand in your current state and ask Him to change it.
?
The consistency of 'jacl' demands that you see every attribute you have as directly made by Allah. Which attribute in your life do you find hardest to accept as His creation, and what would change if you truly believed it? Write down one specific struggle and pray for the grace to see it as His making.

Objection and Response: Defending the Transformative Meaning of 'Jacl'
The author addresses an objection and provides two responses to uphold the transformative meaning of 'jacl'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that 'jacl' in the verse could mean mere creation, and the author's two sophisticated responses to show it must be transformative.

Core Idea
Even if 'jacl' can mean creation, the context of transformation and blame forces a transformative reading.

Having established that 'jacl' must mean transformation, the author now anticipates an objection: perhaps 'jacl' here simply means 'created' the persons of the worshippers, not that He made them into worshippers. This would weaken the proof. The author counters with two arguments: one based on the linguistic structure of 'made of them apes' implying change, and another based on the blame context of the verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic from 'فإن قيل' to 'والجواب عنه من وجهين'. Notice the objection states that 'jacl' with one object means creation (īḥdāth), so the verse only says Allah created persons who worship, not that He created the worship itself.
This is a common counter-argument from those who deny Allah creates human actions.
2
Understand the first response
The first response focuses on the phrase 'made of them apes' (jaʿala minhum al-qirada). The author explains that 'making a thing from another thing' implies removing the first attribute and creating the second—i.e., transformation, not mere creation.
This response relies on the structure 'jaʿala min' which indicates change from a prior state.
3
Understand the second response
The second response argues from context: the verse is in a blame (dhamm) context. If 'jacl' only meant creating the persons, there would be no blame. The blame lies in becoming worshippers of Taghut, so 'jacl' must mean making them into worshippers.
This is a contextual argument based on the purpose of the verse.
4
Reflect on the principle of mushtarak
The author also invokes the principle that an ambiguous word (mushtarak) cannot be used in both its meanings at once, so 'jacl' must have a single meaning here—and that meaning is transformation.
This reinforces the consistency argument from the previous lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
