The tenth proof: His statement, the Exalted: 'Say: Shall I inform you of something worse than that as recompense from Allah? Those whom Allah has cursed and with whom He is angry and made of them apes and swine and worshippers of Taghut.' This is a clear statement that Allah made them worshippers of Taghut. And it is not possible to explain this 'making' as judgment and naming, because Allah mentioned the form of 'making,' then attributed this form to apes, then to swine, then to worshippers of Taghut. And there is no doubt that the intended meaning of 'making' when attributed to apes and swine is transformation and change from one attribute to another. So it must be that the intended meaning when attributed to worshippers of Taghut is this same meaning as well, otherwise it would entail using the same word once in its literal sense and in its metaphorical sense together, and that is impossible. If it is said: The text indicates that Allah made from those who worship Taghut. The form of 'making' here is transitive with one object, so it means creation. The meaning would be: Allah created the one who worships Taghut. There is no dispute about that. And there is no evidence in the verse that Allah created the worship of Taghut in them. The answer is from two aspects: First: Allah said 'And made of them apes' — and making a thing from another thing means removing the first attribute and creating the second attribute. If that is so, then this making is not in the sense of creation, but rather in the sense of turning a thing into another thing. And since the 'making' mentioned in this verse has this meaning, it must also be the intended meaning in His statement 'and worshippers of Taghut,' otherwise it would entail using the ambiguous word (mushtarak) in both of its meanings together, and that is not permissible. Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof (al-ḥujja al-ʿāshira) from Quran 5:60. The author argues that the verb 'jacl' (made) is used in the verse to refer to transforming people into apes and swine and also making them worshippers of Taghut. An objection is then raised: the text only says 'made of them' (jaʿala minhum) which is transitive with one object, meaning creation (īḥdāth) — i.e., Allah created the persons who worship Taghut, not the act of worship itself. The author responds with two arguments: (1) The phrase 'made of them apes' (wa-jaʿala minhum al-qirada) means removing the first attribute (humanity) and creating the second (apeness), which is transformation (taṣyīr), not mere creation. Therefore, the same meaning applies to 'worshippers of Taghut' to avoid using 'jacl' in two different senses (ambiguous word, mushtarak). Thus the verse means Allah made them into worshippers of Taghut. (2) The verse is in a context of blame (dhamm). If it only meant creating their persons, there would be no blame; the blame lies in attributing to them the quality of worshipping Taghut, which proves that Allah directly made them have that attribute.

Key Terms
جعل
إحداث
تصيير
لفظ مشترك
عبدة الطاغوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'jacl' in the verse simply means 'created' the persons, and does not imply making them worshippers.
Correction: The author shows that 'jacl' is used for apes and swine as transformation, and the blame context requires that the same transformative meaning applies to worshippers.

Checkpoint
What is the first response the author gives to the objection that 'jacl' means creation?
A) The word 'jacl' is ambiguous and can mean both.
B) The phrase 'made of them apes' implies removal of prior attribute and creation of new one, i.e., transformation.
C) The verse is metaphorical and not literal.
D) The objector's interpretation is heretical.

Key Takeaway
The objection that 'jacl' means mere creation is refuted by the transformative sense required by the verb's construction and the verse's blame context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Allah made them worshippers of Taghut, do you ever use your own 'جعل' as an excuse to avoid blame? How will you now accept responsibility for the attributes you acquire?
?
The author argues that blame only makes sense if 'جعل' means transformation. Think of a time you blamed yourself for a sin. Did you attribute it to your own choice or to Allah's creation? How does this lesson change your view of that moment?
?
If someone claims their sins are because Allah created them as sinners, how would you respond using the linguistic argument from this lesson? Will you actually do that when you encounter this excuse?

The Prayer for Steadfastness Proves God Creates Deviation
Author proves God creates guidance and misguidance from the prayer for steadfastness and begins refuting al-Jubba'i's interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'anic prayer 'Do not cause our hearts to deviate' demonstrates that God creates both guidance and misguidance, and why alternative interpretations are insufficient.

Core Idea
Praying 'Don't let us deviate' proves God is the one who causes deviation.

The author uses a familiar verse from the Qur'an to argue that God is the creator of human actions, including the heart's inclination to faith or disbelief. He presents two rational arguments based on the verse, then supports them with prophetic hadiths. The passage also introduces an objection from al-Jubba'i, which is cut off, leaving the refutation incomplete. This lesson focuses on the author's positive proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and the first argument
Read the Arabic of the verse and the author's first argument: if God never causes deviation, the prayer would be meaningless (asking for what is already the case).
This is a simple logical point: the prayer implies possibility.
2
Understand the second argument on the need for a preponderant factor
The heart is equally capable of faith and disbelief; it cannot incline without a motive (da'iya) and will (irada) created by God. This leads to God creating both the motive for faith (tawfiq) and for disbelief (khidhlan).
Notice the author's use of infinite regress to show the motive cannot come from another will.
3
Examine the prophetic hadiths
The Prophet's saying 'The heart of the believer is between two fingers of the Most Merciful' and his prayer 'O Turner of hearts' are cited to reinforce the idea that God directly controls the heart's direction through these motives.
The author calls this a 'rational, demonstrative, decisive speech'.
4
Consider the objection and its incomplete response
An objector argues that since God cannot cause deviation, the verse must be interpreted, and al-Jubba'i's interpretation is mentioned but the passage cuts off. Observe how the author frames the objection and note that the refutation is not fully given here.
This is a reminder of the importance of checking the original source for completion.

الحجة الثالثة عشر : قوله تعالى : (ربنا لا تزغ قلوبنا بعد إذ هديتنا ، وهب لنا من لدنك رحمة ) والاستدلال بهذه الآية من وجهين : الأول : إنه تعالى إن كان لا يزيغ القلوب البتة ، ولا يجوز أن يصدر عنه هذا الفعل ، كان هذا الدعاء طلباً لتحصيل الحاصل . وهو محال . الثاني : إن القلب صالح ، لأن يميل إلى الإيمان ، وصالح لأن يميل إلى الكفر . ويمتنع أن يميل إلى أحد الجانبين إلا لمرجح . وذلك المرجح هو حدوث داعية وإرادة في القلب. ولا يمكن أن يكون حدوث تلك الإرادة ، لإرادة أخرى . وإلا وقع التسلسل . فهي إرادة تحدث بإحداث الله تعالى . ثم تلك الداعية وتلك الإرادة . إن كانت داعية الكفر. فهي الخذلان والإزاغة والصد والختم والطبع والدين والقسوة والوقر والكنان. وغيرها من الألفاظ الواردة في القرآن . وإن كانت تلك الداعية داعية الإيمان، فهي التوفيق والإرشاد والهداية والتسديد والعصمة . وغيرها من الألفاظ الواردة في القرآن . وكان رسول الله يقول : ( قلب المؤمن بين اصبعين من أصابع الرحمن ، والمراد من هاتين الإصبعين : هاتان الداعيتان . فكما أن الشيء الذي يكون بين إصبعي الإنسان يقلب كما يقلبه الإنسان بواسطة ذينك الإصبعين فكذلك القلب لوقوعه بين داعيتي الفعل والترك يتقلب كما يقلبه الحق - سبحانه - بواسطة تينك الداعيتين. ومن أنصف ولم يتعسف ، وجرب نفسه ، وجد هذا المعنى كالشيء المحسوس . ولو جوز العاقل حدوث إحدى الداعيتين من غير محدث ، لزمه نفي الصانع . وكان صلوات الله عليه يقول : « يا مقلب القلوب ، ثبت قلبي على دينك ، ومعناه : ما ذكرناه . فلما أخبر الله تعالى عن الراسخين في العلم أنهم يقولون : « آمناً ، بالمحكمات والمتشابهات، وأن كلها و من عند الله ، حكي عنهم أنهم تضرعوا في أن لا يجعل قلبهم مائلاً إلى الباطل ، بعد أن جعله مائلاً إلى الحق. فهذا كلام عقلي برهاني قاطع متأكد بهذا التقرير القرآني . فإن قيل : لما دلت الدلائل على أن الزيغ لا يجوز أن يخلقه الله تعالى ، وجب حمل هذه الآية على التأويل . وهو من وجوه : الأول : قال ( الجبائي ) : المراد بقوله : ( لا تزغ قلوبنا ) أي لا تمنعها الألطاف التي معها تستمر القلوب على صفة الإيمان . وذلك لأنه تعالى لما منعهم
The thirteenth proof: His saying Almighty: 'Our Lord, do not cause our hearts to deviate after You have guided us, and grant us from Yourself mercy.' And the deduction from this verse is from two aspects: First: If He, the Exalted, does not cause hearts to deviate at all, and it is not permissible that this act proceeds from Him, then this prayer would be a request for obtaining what is already obtained, which is impossible. Second: The heart is capable of inclining towards faith and capable of inclining towards disbelief. And it is impossible for it to incline to either side except due to a preponderant factor. That preponderant factor is the occurrence of a motive (da'iya) and an will (irada) in the heart. And it is impossible for that will to occur due to another will, otherwise there would be infinite regress. So it is a will that occurs by the creation of Allah Almighty. Then that motive and will: if it is a motive of disbelief, it is khidhlan (abandonment), izaghah (causing deviation), sadd (blocking), khatm (sealing), tab' (stamping), dayn (debt?) qaswa (hardness), wiqr (heaviness), kinan (covering), and other terms that appear in the Qur'an. And if that motive is a motive of faith, it is tawfiq (success), irshad (guidance), hidaya (guidance), tasdid (rectification), 'isma (protection), and other terms in the Qur'an. And the Messenger of Allah used to say: 'The heart of the believer is between two fingers of the fingers of the Most Merciful.' The meaning of these two fingers is these two motives. Just as a thing that is between a person's fingers is turned as the person turns it by means of those two fingers, so too the heart, because it is situated between the two motives of action and abandonment, is turned as the Truth turns it by means of those two motives. Whoever is fair and not biased, and examines himself, will find this meaning like something perceptible. If a rational person were to allow the occurrence of one of the two motives without a creator, he would necessarily negate the Maker. And he used to say, peace be upon him: 'O Turner of hearts, make my heart firm upon Your religion.' Its meaning is what we mentioned. When Allah Almighty informed about the deeply rooted in knowledge that they say: 'We believe in the clear and ambiguous verses, and all are from our Lord,' He related that they supplicated that He not make their hearts incline to falsehood after He made them incline to truth. So this is a rational, demonstrative, decisive speech, reinforced by this Qur'anic exposition. If it is said: Since the evidences indicate that deviation cannot be created by Allah Almighty, then this verse must be interpreted. And it is from aspects: The first: Al-Jubba'i said: The meaning of 'Do not cause our hearts to deviate' is: Do not prevent from them the kindnesses (altaf) by which hearts continue upon the state of faith. And that is because when He, the Exalted, prevented them...

Commentary
This passage presents the thirteenth proof, which is from the verse 'Our Lord, do not cause our hearts to deviate after You have guided us.' The author deduces two arguments. First, if God never causes deviation, then praying for Him not to cause deviation would be asking for what is already the case, which is absurd. Second, the heart is naturally capable of both faith and disbelief, and a preponderant factor (مرجح) is required for it to incline either way. That factor is a motive (داعية) and will (إرادة) created by God in the heart. If the motive is towards disbelief, it is called by various Qur'anic terms; if towards faith, it is called tawfiq, etc. The author then cites prophetic hadith about the heart being between two fingers of the Merciful, explaining the fingers as these two motives. The heart is turned by God via these motives. He argues that if one allows a motive to occur without a creator, that leads to atheism. The prayer 'O Turner of hearts, make my heart firm' confirms this. The firmly rooted scholars also pray against deviation, showing that God can cause deviation. The author concludes this is a rational, definitive proof. Then an objection is raised: since evidence shows God cannot cause deviation, the verse must be interpreted. The objector mentions al-Jubba'i's interpretation: the prayer means not to prevent the kindnesses (اللطاف) that maintain faith. The passage cuts off during this interpretation, so the full refutation is not given. However, the author's own position is clear: God creates both guidance and misguidance.

Misconception: The prayer 'Do not cause our hearts to deviate' is merely a request for continued guidance and does not imply that God can cause deviation.
Correction: The author argues that if God never causes deviation, then praying for Him not to do so would be a request for what is already true, which is absurd. Therefore, the prayer logically entails that God can cause deviation, and we ask Him not to.

Key Takeaway

The Fifth Argument: God's Knowledge of Evil Prevents Him from Creating It
Enumerates the fifth argument with two premises and their proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the fifth argument that God, because He knows evil and is independent, cannot be the creator of evil acts.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of evil and His independence make evil acts impossible from Him.

This passage presents a sophisticated argument that God's omniscience and self-sufficiency logically preclude the possibility of Him committing or creating evil. The argument is built on two premises: first, that God knows the ugliness of evil and His independence from it, and second, that such knowledge necessarily prevents any agent from performing evil. Let's unpack this reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the overall argument
Read the entire passage once to grasp the overall claim: God cannot create evil acts. Notice that it is built on two premises, each requiring justification.
The conclusion is stated at the beginning and end.
2
Examine the first premise and its three sub-points
The first premise states that God knows evil's ugliness and His independence. This is supported by three claims: evil is ugly by nature, God is independent, and God knows all things. Identify these three.
The author mentions that the first sub-point will be elaborated later.
3
Analyze the second premise and its reasoning
The second premise says that anyone with such knowledge cannot do evil. The reasoning involves incentive (dā'ī) and repellent (ṣārif) — one knowledge cannot be both.
This is the key logical move: a single piece of knowledge cannot simultaneously motivate and prevent an action.
4
See how the conclusion follows
Combine both premises: since God has the knowledge and independence, He necessarily cannot perform evil acts. Therefore, He did not create evil.
The argument is deductive: if both premises are true, the conclusion is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن الله تعالى عالم بقبح القبائح ، وعالم بكونه غنياً عن فعلها . وكل من كان كذلك ، امتنع كونه فاعلاً لهذه القبائح . وذلك يدل على أنه تعالى ما أوجد هذه القبائح البتة . أما المقدمة الأولى : وهي قولنا : إنه تعالى عالم بقيح القبائح وعالم بكونه غنياً عنها ، فتقريره يتوقف على إثبات أمور ثلاثة : الأول : أن القبائح إنما تقبح لوجوه عائدة إليها - وتقريره سيجيء في مسألة الحسن والقبح - . وثانيها : إنه تعالى غني عن الحاجات وثالثها : إنه تعالي عالم بجميع المعلومات. وحينئذ يحصل من مجموع هذه المقدمات الثلاثة ، قولنا : إنه تعالى عالم يقبح القبائح ، وعالم بكونه غنياً عنها . وأما المقدمة الثانية : وهي قولنا : كل من كان كذلك ، فإنه لا يفعل القبيح البتة . فتقريره : إن صدور الفعل عن القادر يتوقف على حصول الداعي ، والعلم بقبح القبيح مع العلم بالاستغناء عنه : جهة صرف وامتناع . وكون العلم الواحد داعياً صارفاً مقتضيا مانعا : محال . فثبت : أنه تعالى يمتنع أن يكون فاعلا لهذه القبائح .
The fifth proof: God is knower of the ugliness of ugly acts, and knower of His being independent of performing them. And everyone who is like that, it is impossible for him to be a doer of these ugly acts. And that indicates that God did not originate these ugly acts at all. As for the first premise, which is our saying: He is knower of the ugliness of ugly acts and knower of His being independent of them, its establishment depends on proving three matters: first: ugly acts are ugly due to aspects returning to them — and its elaboration will come in the issue of good and evil. Second: He is independent of needs. Third: He is knower of all known things. Then from the sum of these three premises results our saying: He is knower of the ugliness of ugly acts and knower of His being independent of them. As for the second premise, which is our saying: everyone who is like that, he does not do the ugly act at all. Its establishment: the occurrence of an act from a capable agent depends on the presence of an incentive, and knowledge of the ugliness of the ugly act together with knowledge of being independent of it is a factor of repulsion and impossibility. And that a single knowledge be both an incentive and a repellant, a requisite and a preventer, is impossible. Thus it is established that it is impossible for God to be a doer of these ugly acts.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument, which aims to prove that God cannot be the creator of evil acts. The argument is built on two premises. The first premise is that God knows the ugliness of evil acts and knows that He is independent of any need to perform them. Establishing this premise requires proving three things: (1) evil acts are evil due to their own inherent aspects (a position the author promises to defend later in the discussion of good and evil), (2) God is independent of all needs, and (3) God knows all things. From these three, it follows that God knows the ugliness of evil and His independence. The second premise is that anyone who has such knowledge will never perform an evil act. The reasoning is that the performance of an act by a capable agent requires an incentive (dā'ī). But knowledge of the act's ugliness combined with knowledge of being independent of it constitutes a repelling factor (ṣārif) and an impossibility (imtinā'). A single piece of knowledge cannot simultaneously be an incentive and a repellant, as that would be contradictory. Therefore, it is impossible for God to commit evil acts.

Key Terms
قبح (ugliness)
قبائح (ugly acts)
داعي (incentive)
صارف (repellant)
غني (independent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's creation of evil contradicts His goodness, so He cannot be all-good.
Correction: The argument here does not focus on goodness but on knowledge and independence: God knows evil is ugly and is independent, so He has no incentive to do it and every reason to avoid it.

Checkpoint
What is the second premise of the fifth argument?
A) God is independent
B) Evil acts are evil by essence
C) Anyone who knows an act is ugly and is independent will not do it
D) God knows all things

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument demonstrates that God's omniscience and self-sufficiency logically preclude the possibility of Him creating evil, as He would have no incentive and every reason to avoid it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that God cannot do evil because He knows its ugliness and has no need for it. But you are not like that: you sometimes choose what you know is wrong. What does that choice reveal about what you are ignoring or desiring? Will you today take one specific step to let the knowledge of God's purity repel you from a sin you are tempted by?
?
If God's omniscience and independence make evil impossible from Him, then the evil you face must come from elsewhere—your own choices or others'. How does accepting this change the way you respond to personal suffering? Do you still complain against God, or do you turn inward and ask Him for strength?
?
The argument uses the concepts of 'incentive' and 'repellent'. What is the strongest incentive driving your life right now? Is it drawing you toward or away from God? Can you name one repellent—based on this lesson—that you can use to turn away from a recurring sin?

The Fifth Proof: God's Knowledge and Self-Sufficiency Prevent Evil
The author proves God cannot commit evil due to His knowledge of evil and independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses God's knowledge of evil's ugliness and His self-sufficiency to prove that God cannot commit evil acts.

Core Idea
God's knowledge and independence make evil impossible for Him.

This lesson presents the fifth proof from the author, which argues from God's attributes of knowledge and self-sufficiency to show that evil cannot be attributed to Him. The proof is built on two premises, each requiring careful justification. We will walk through each step, from establishing that God knows evil is ugly and He is independent, to showing that such knowledge eliminates any motive to do evil.

Guided Path
1
Understand the overall argument
Read the entire passage once. The proof has two main premises: (1) God knows evil is ugly and knows He is independent of it, (2) anyone with these qualities cannot do evil. The conclusion is that God has not created evil.
Notice that the proof uses logical deduction from divine attributes.
2
Examine the first premise and its three sub-points
The first premise is that God knows evil's ugliness and His independence. This is established by three sub-points: evil is intrinsically ugly, God is independent of needs, and God knows everything. Combine them to get the first premise.
The author says the proof of intrinsic ugliness will come later.
3
Understand the second premise about motive and repulsion
The second premise argues that knowing evil's ugliness and one's independence from it creates a repulsion (صارف) that prevents any motive (داعي) to do evil. A single piece of knowledge cannot be both a motive and a repeller, so God cannot have a motive to do evil.
This hinges on the idea that action requires a motive, and the knowledge here acts as a repeller.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن الله تعالى عالم بقبح القبائح ، وعالم بكونه غنياً عن فعلها . وكل من كان كذلك ، امتنع كونه فاعلاً لهذه القبائح . وذلك يدل على أنه تعالى ما أوجد هذه القبائح البتة . أما المقدمة الأولى : وهي قولنا : إنه تعالى عالم بقبح القبائح وعالم بكونه غنياً عنها ، فتقريره يتوقف على إثبات أمور ثلاثة : الأول : أن القبائح إنما تقبح لوجوه عائدة إليها - وتقريره سيجيء في مسألة الحسن والقبح - . وثانيها : إنه تعالى غني عن الحاجات . وثالثها : إنه تعالي عالم بجميع المعلومات. وحينئذ يحصل من مجموع هذه المقدمات الثلاثة ، قولنا : إنه تعالى عالم بقبح القبائح ، وعالم بكونه غنياً عنها . وأما المقدمة الثانية : وهي قولنا : كل من كان كذلك ، فإنه لا يفعل القبيح البتة . فتقريره : إن صدور الفعل عن القادر يتوقف على حصول الداعي ، والعلم بقبح القبيح مع العلم بالاستغناء عنه : جهة صرف وامتناع . وكون العلم الواحد داعياً صارفاً مقتضيا مانعا : محال . فثبت : أنه تعالى يمتنع أن يكون فاعلا لهذه القبائح .
The Fifth Proof: Indeed, God, exalted, knows the ugliness of ugly deeds, and knows that He is independent of doing them. And whoever is like that, it is impossible for him to be a doer of these ugly deeds. And that indicates that He, exalted, has not originated these ugly deeds at all. As for the first premise: which is our statement: that He, exalted, knows the ugliness of ugly deeds and knows that He is independent of them, its exposition depends on establishing three matters: First: that ugly deeds are ugly due to aspects returning to them – and its exposition will come in the issue of good and evil (الحسن والقبح). Second: that He, exalted, is independent of needs. Third: that He, exalted, knows all known things. Then from the total of these three premises, our statement results: that He, exalted, knows the ugliness of ugly deeds, and knows that He is independent of them. And as for the second premise: which is our statement: that whoever is like that, then he does not do the ugly deed at all. Its exposition: that the occurrence of an act from the capable (القادر) depends on the presence of a motive (الداعي), and the knowledge of the ugliness of the ugly with the knowledge of independence from it is a ground of repulsion (جهة صرف) and prevention (امتناع). And that a single knowledge being both a motive (داعياً) and a repeller (صارفاً), a requirer (مقتضياً) and a preventer (مانعاً) is impossible. So it is established that He, exalted, is impossible to be a doer of these ugly deeds.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is a detailed proof from divine attributes. The author argues that God knows evil is evil and that He is utterly self-sufficient and has no need for it. Anyone with these two qualities necessarily refrains from evil. To establish the first premise (that God knows evil's ugliness and His independence), he lists three sub-points: (1) evil acts are intrinsically evil (to be explained later in the discussion on good and evil), (2) God is independent of all needs, and (3) God knows all things. Together these prove the first premise. The second premise (that such a being cannot commit evil) is justified by analyzing action: for an agent to act, a motive (داعي) is required. Knowledge of an act's ugliness combined with knowledge of independence from it constitutes a repelling factor (صارف) and prevents the act. A single piece of knowledge cannot simultaneously be a motive and a repeller, so God cannot have a motive to do evil; thus He cannot commit it.

Key Terms
القبائح
العلم بالاستغناء
الداعي
الصارف
المقتضي
المانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that because God is all-powerful, He can do evil if He wills. But the proof argues that His knowledge of evil's ugliness and His self-sufficiency eliminate any motive to do evil, making it impossible, not merely improbable.
Correction: The proof does not deny God's power but shows that His perfect knowledge and independence make evil acts incompatible with His nature.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth proof, why is it impossible for God to commit evil acts?
A) Because He is not powerful enough.
B) Because His knowledge of evil's ugliness and His independence from it prevent any motive to do evil.
C) Because evil does not exist.
D) Because He has delegated creation to humans.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of evil's ugliness and His utter independence from it preclude any motive to commit evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God cannot even conceive of doing evil—that His knowledge and independence make it impossible—how would that change your reaction when you face personal tragedy or injustice? Will you still entertain thoughts that God has wronged you?
?
This proof shows that God's knowledge of evil's ugliness acts as a repeller, not a motive. How does this challenge your own tendency to blame God for the evil in the world? What specific thought will you replace that blame with, starting today?
?
God's independence from all needs means He has no motive to harm you. How does that truth confront your deepest fears about God's intentions toward you? Are you willing to let go of any lingering suspicion that God might be indifferent or cruel? If so, what concrete step will you take to trust Him more this week?

Praise, Blame, and Reward Require Free Will
Sanusi argues that moral accountability requires free choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why praise, blame, reward, and punishment are only meaningful if humans have genuine free choice.

Core Idea
Praise and blame only make sense if you could have done otherwise.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah argues that moral accountability presupposes genuine ability to act or refrain. Before we can discuss divine justice, we must recognize that praise and blame are empty without free will. The author presents a clear argument against theological determinism (jabr).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the author's direct style.
The passage contrasts two scenarios: one with free choice, one without.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the terms for praise (مدح), blame (ذم), promise (وعد), threat (وعيد), ability (قدرة), and choice (اختيار). Understand their role in the argument.
These terms recur in kalam discussions of justice.
3
Reflect on the implication for divine justice
Consider the author's claim that punishing without free will is 'pure lying, injustice, and foolishness'. Ask yourself why he uses such strong language.
This directly challenges the Ash'ari position on kasb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المدح والذم والوعد والوعيد فكل ذلك إنما يحسن إذا كان المخاطب قادراً على الفعل والترك ، متمكناً منهما . حتى أنه إذا أطاع باختياره مدح وأثيب ، وإن عصى باختياره ذم وعوقب فأما إذا خلق الكفر فيه ، شاء أم أب ، ثم يعاقبه عليه أبد الآباد ، ويذمه عليه ، ويقول : لم فعلت ؟ فهذا محض الكذب ، ومحض الظلم والسفه . وأما القصص المذكورة في كتب الله المنزلة . فالفائدة منها : أن يعتبر المكلف بها ، فيقدم على الطاعة ، وينزجر عن المعصية . كما قال تعالى : ( لقد كان في قصصهم عبرة لأولي الألباب ) وهذه الفائدة إنما تحصل إذا كان العبد قادراً على الاختيار أولاً ، وقادراً على الطاعة والمعصية ثانياً . فأما إذا كان لا قدرة له على الطاعة ولا على المعصية البتة ، كان ذكر الاعتبار عبثاً محضاً . لأن الله تعالى إن خلق الطاعة فيه ، فهي حاصلة . سواء حصل ذلك الاعتبار أو لم يحصل ، وإن لم يخلقها امتنع حصولها . سواء حصل هناك اعتبار ، أو لم يحصل . فكان ذكر القصص الإرادة الاعتبار عبثاً محضاً . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن المقصود من بعثة الأنبياء وإنزال الكتب عليهم : ليس إلا هذه الأمور ، وثبت : أنها بأسرها لا تقرر إلا على القول بالعدل ، وإبطال القول بالجبر والجور . فثبت : أن جميع كتب الله ناطقة بفساد القول بالجبر
As for praise and blame and promise and threat, all of that is only good if the addressed person is able to do and leave, capable of both. So that if he obeys by his choice, he is praised and rewarded, and if he disobeys by his choice, he is blamed and punished. But if He created disbelief in him, whether he wills or refuses, then punishes him for it forever, and blames him for it, and says: 'Why did you do it?' — this is pure lying, pure injustice and foolishness. As for the stories mentioned in the revealed books of God, the benefit from them is that the accountable person may take heed, so that he proceeds to obedience and refrains from sin, as God Most High said: 'Indeed in their stories there is a lesson for those of understanding.' And this benefit is only obtained if the servant is able to choose firstly, and able to obey and disobey secondly. But if he has no ability at all for obedience nor for disobedience, then mentioning the lesson is pure futility. Because if God Most High creates obedience in him, it occurs whether that lesson occurs or not, and if He does not create it, its occurrence is impossible whether there is a lesson or not. So mentioning the stories [for the purpose of] heedfulness was pure futility. So it is established by what we have mentioned that the purpose of sending prophets and revealing books to them is nothing but these matters, and it is established that all of them are only affirmed on the doctrine of justice ('adl) and the nullification of the doctrine of jabr (compulsion) and injustice. So it is established that all of God's books speak of the corruption of the doctrine of jabr.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument that praise, blame, reward, punishment, and the instructive purpose of scriptural stories all presuppose human free will. The author first states that praise and blame are only appropriate if the person has genuine ability to act or refrain. If God creates disbelief in a person and then punishes him eternally, that is pure lying, injustice, and foolishness. Then he turns to the stories in scripture, citing the Quranic verse that they are for reflection. But such reflection only benefits if the person has the ability to choose and act. Without that ability, the stories are useless. Hence, the entire purpose of prophethood and revelation—guidance, warning, promise—depends on affirming human capacity and rejecting jabr. Therefore, all scriptures themselves refute jabr.

Key Terms
مدح
ذم
وعيد
قدرة
اختيار
جبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God can punish humans for actions He creates without injustice.
Correction: The author argues that is pure injustice and lying, because the person had no real choice.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is praise and blame appropriate?
A) Only when God wills
B) When the person has ability to do and leave
C) Only for believers
D) Only in this world

Key Takeaway
Praise and blame only make sense if humans have genuine free will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever blamed someone for something you knew they couldn't help? How does this passage challenge your own tendency to assign blame without considering whether the person had genuine choice? Will you commit to checking your own heart before blaming others?
?
This passage says that if God punishes without giving free will, it would be 'pure injustice.' Do you truly believe that God is just in your own life, especially when you face trials that seem undeserved? What would it mean for your relationship with Him if you were not free?
?
Sanusi says praise and reward only make sense if you could have done otherwise. In what areas of your life are you relying on your own 'choice' to earn God's pleasure, and in what areas are you using the excuse of predestination to avoid responsibility? Which will you change today?

Scriptural Stories Affirm Free Will
Sanusi demonstrates that scriptural stories require free will for their moral purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the instructive purpose of Quranic stories depends on human free will.

Core Idea
Stories are useless if outcomes are predetermined.

The author now turns to the scriptural stories, arguing that their purpose—to take heed—presupposes free choice. Without it, the stories become futile. This reinforces the overall rejection of jabr and establishes that all scriptures uphold human capacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the Qur'anic citation (12:111) used as evidence.
The author builds on the previous argument about praise and blame.
2
Identify the logical structure
The author first states the benefit of stories, then the condition for that benefit, then the absurdity without free will, and finally the conclusion. Trace each step.
This is a classic conditional argument.
3
Connect to the larger theme
Link this argument to the broader claim that the purpose of prophethood and revelation is only meaningful with free will. Understand why the author concludes that all scriptures refute jabr.
The conclusion ties together moral accountability and revelation.

The Mu'tazilite Definition of 'Ilah' and Its First Proof
Mu'tazilites define Ilah as deserving worship and argue against jabr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite definition of 'Ilah' as the one deserving worship, and their argument that this definition is incompatible with predestination.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites define Ilah as deserving worship, which they use to argue against predestination.

The Mu'tazilites begin by defining 'Ilah' in a way that seems natural—God deserves worship. But they build a case that this definition forces a rejection of predestination (al-jabr). This passage presents their two key arguments, including a citation from the Mu'tazilite scholar al-Jubba'i.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite claim and first argument
Read the passage where the Mu'tazilites state their definition and link it to jabr. Focus on the first argument: if God creates disbelief and punishes, He is not beneficent and thus not deserving of worship.
Notice how they use the threat of apostasy to undermine the idea of deserving worship.
2
Examine the second argument about inability
The second argument: if the servant cannot perform worship, then only God can worship Himself, which is absurd. This forces the conclusion that the servant must have free will.
This argument directly attacks the Ash'ari doctrine of acquisition (kasb).
3
Consider the al-Jubba'i quote
Al-Jubba'i's interpretation of the Quranic verse shows how the Mu'tazilites saw predestination as incompatible with God's praiseworthiness.
This illustrates the theological stakes behind a seemingly linguistic definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة : الإله هو الذي تحق له العبادة وهذا المعنى لازم على القول بالجبر . وبيانه من وجهين : الأول : إنه تعالى إنما يكون مستحقاً للعبادة ، لأنه صدر عنه غاية الإنعام والإحسان . ولو أنه خلق فيهم الكفر ، ليستدرجهم إلى النار ، لما كان منعماً على العبيد . وأيضا : فكل من آمن في الحال، لم يأمن أن تكون عاقبة أمره : الردة والكفر . وحينئذ يصير كل ما هو فيه وبالاً عليه . ومن كان كذلك ، فهو أعظم المفترين ، لأعظم المنعمين. فكيف يستحق العبادة والتعظيم ؟ ولهذا السبب قال الجبائي في تفسير قوله : نعم المولى ونعم النصير : إن كان تعالى يخلق الكفر في الكافر في الدنيا ثم يعذبه عليه أبد الآباد في الآخرة ، فحينئذ لا يكون هو - تعالى - نعم المولى ونعم النصير بل يكون : بئس المولى وبئس النصير . الثاني : إنه - تعالى - إنما يستحق العبادة على العبد ، لو كان العبد قادراً على فعل العبادة . أما إذا كان العبد غير قادر على العبادة . بل يكون القادر على العبادة هو الله تعالى لزم أن يقال : إنه استحق على نفسه أن يعبد نفسه ، وذلك غير معقول قال أهل السنة : كلامكم مبني على أن لفظ الله ، معناه : المستحق للعبادة . وهذا باطل . ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : إنا بينا في تفسير أسماء الله : أن « الله » : اسم علم غير مشتق الثاني : لو سلمنا أنه مشتق ، لكن لا نسلم أن معناه : أنه الذي يستحق العبادة . ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : إنه لو كان كذلك ، لوجب أن لا يكون إلهاً للجمادات والبهائم ، لأنه - تعالى - يستحق عليها العبادة ، ولزم أن لا يكون إلهاً في الأزل لم يخلق أحداً ، ولم ينعم على أحد فلم يكن مستحقاً للعبادة الثاني : إنا بينا : أن العقل لا يدل على حصول الاستحقاق . لأنه لا يتفاوت حال المعبود بسبب هذه العبادة - وهي شاقة على العابد - فوجب أن لا يحكم العقل بوجوبه إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : الإله هو القادر على الاختراع . والدليل عليه : أنه تعالى إنما ظهر الامتياز بينه وبين سائر الذوات بصفته الخلاقية . فقال : أفمن يخلق كمن لا يخلق ؟ ولما كان المذكور في معرض الامتياز هو هذه الصفة ، علمنا : أن معني الإلهية ليس إلا الخلاقية . فلو كان العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه ، لكان خالقاً لها ، وحينئذ يبطل الفرق بين الإله ، وبين العبد في
The Mu'tazilites said: al-Ilah is the one to whom worship is due. And this meaning is necessary on the doctrine of predetermination (al-jabr). Its explanation is from two aspects: First: He, exalted, only deserves worship because from Him proceeds utmost grace and beneficence. But if He created disbelief in them (the servants) to lead them gradually to the Fire, then He would not be beneficent to the servants. Also: every one who believes at present is not safe from that his final outcome might be apostasy and disbelief. In that case everything that he was upon would become a burden upon him. Whoever is like that is the greatest slanderer against the greatest benefactor. So how can He deserve worship and glorification? For this reason al-Jubba'i said in the interpretation of His statement "What an excellent Master and what an excellent Helper": if He, exalted, creates disbelief in the disbeliever in this world and then punishes him for it forever in the afterlife, then He, exalted, would not be "what an excellent Master and what an excellent Helper" but rather "what an evil master and what an evil helper." Second: He, exalted, deserves worship from the servant only if the servant is able to perform worship. But if the servant is not able to perform worship, rather the one able to worship is God, then it would be necessary to say that He deserves from Himself to worship Himself, and that is not reasonable. The people of the Sunna said: Your statement is based on the word "Allah" meaning the one deserving worship. This is false, and two aspects indicate it: First: we have shown in the interpretation of the names of God that "Allah" is a proper name, not derived. Second: if we grant that it is derived, we do not grant that its meaning is the one who deserves worship. Two aspects indicate that: First: if that were so, then it would be necessary that He is not an ilah for inanimate objects and animals, because He, exalted, deserves worship from them, and it would be necessary that He is not an ilah in pre-eternity when He had not created anyone and had not bestowed favor on anyone, so He would not be deserving of worship. Second: we have shown that the intellect does not indicate the attainment of deservingness. Because the state of the worshipped does not change due to this worship—while it is burdensome on the worshiper—so it is necessary that the intellect does not rule it as obligatory. When this is established, we say: al-Ilah is the one powerful over creation. The proof for it is that He, exalted, only manifested the distinction between Himself and other essences by His attribute of creatorship. He said: "Is He who creates like one who does not create?" And since this attribute is mentioned in the context of distinction, we know that the meaning of divinity (al-ilahiyya) is nothing other than creatorship. So if the servant were the originator of his own actions, he would be a creator of them, and then the distinction between al-Ilah and the servant would be nullified in
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage presents a debate between the Mu'tazilites and the Sunnis (Ash'aris) over the definition of "Ilah". The Mu'tazilites define Ilah as the one who deserves worship, and they argue that this definition is incompatible with the doctrine of predestination (al-jabr) because if God predestines disbelief, He would not be deserving of worship. They give two proofs: first, if God creates disbelief and then punishes, He is not beneficent; second, if the servant cannot perform worship, then only God can worship Himself, which is absurd. The Sunnis respond by refuting the Mu'tazilite definition of the word "Allah". They first assert that "Allah" is a proper name, not derived from any root. Second, even if derived, it does not mean "deserving worship". They provide two arguments: (i) if it meant that, then inanimate objects and animals would also owe worship, and God in pre-eternity would not be Ilah; (ii) the intellect does not dictate deservingness because worship is burdensome and does not benefit the worshipped. Then the Sunnis establish their own definition: Ilah is the one powerful over creation (al-qadir ala al-ikhtira'). The proof is that the Quran distinguishes the Creator from other beings, so the essence of divinity is creatorship. Therefore, if humans create their own actions, they would share in the divine attribute and the distinction collapses—thus arguing against Mu'tazilite free will.

Key Terms
الإله
العبادة
الجبر
الاستحقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazilite definition of Ilah is simply linguistic and unrelated to free will.
Correction: They explicitly use the definition to argue against predestination, making it a theological claim about divine justice.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilites, what is the meaning of 'Ilah'?
A) The one powerful over creation
B) The one to whom worship is due
C) The one who creates actions in servants
D) The one who is worshipped by all

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites define Ilah as deserving worship, and they argue that this definition logically requires rejecting predestination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly worship God as the One who deserves worship because of His grace, or do you sometimes feel that your actions are forced and thus shift responsibility away from yourself? How does this affect your sincerity in worship?
?
If you believed that all your actions were predestined, would your worship become mechanical? Reflect on the Mu'tazilite argument: does predestination undermine gratitude? In your own heart, do you feel genuine gratitude for your ability to choose to worship?
?
The Mu'tazilites argue that if God creates disbelief and then punishes, He would be an evil master. How does this challenge your understanding of divine justice? Do you ever question God's justice when seeing suffering? How do you reconcile it with predestination, and what does this lesson demand of you in your trust in God?

6 min
The Sunni Refutation and Alternative Definition of 'Ilah'
Sunnis refute Mu'tazilite definition and define Ilah as Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Sunnis refute the Mu'tazilite definition of 'Ilah' and establish their own definition as the Creator.

Core Idea
The Sunnis refute the Mu'tazilite definition by arguing 'Allah' is a proper name not derived, and propose 'power over creation' as the core meaning.

After critiquing the Mu'tazilite definition of 'Ilah', the Sunni scholars present a two-pronged refutation. First, they argue that 'Allah' is a proper name, not derived from any root. Second, even if derived, it does not mean 'deserving worship'. They then provide their own positive definition: Ilah is the one who has power over creation, proven by the Quranic verse distinguishing the Creator from His creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Sunni refutation of the Mu'tazilite definition
The Sunnis first argue that 'Allah' is a proper name (ism 'alam), not derived. Then, even assuming derivation, they give two arguments against the meaning 'deserving worship'.
Note the structure: two overall responses, each with two sub-arguments.
2
Examine the two arguments against derivational meaning
First: if 'Ilah' meant 'deserving worship', then inanimate objects would also be required to worship, and God in pre-eternity would not be Ilah. Second: the intellect does not indicate deservingness because worship burdens the worshipper without benefiting God.
These arguments are logical, not scriptural.
3
Learn the Sunni positive definition
The Sunnis define Ilah as the one powerful over creation (al-qadir ala al-ikhtira'), with proof from the Quran: 'Is He who creates like one who does not create?' This shows that the essence of divinity is creatorship.
This definition directly counters the Mu'tazilite emphasis on free will; if humans create their own actions, they share in divinity.

قالت المعتزلة : الإله هو الذي تحق له العبادة وهذا المعنى لازم على القول بالجبر . وبيانه من وجهين : الأول : إنه تعالى إنما يكون مستحقاً للعبادة ، لأنه صدر عنه غاية الإنعام والإحسان . ولو أنه خلق فيهم الكفر ، ليستدرجهم إلى النار ، لما كان منعماً على العبيد . وأيضا : فكل من آمن في الحال، لم يأمن أن تكون عاقبة أمره : الردة والكفر . وحينئذ يصير كل ما هو فيه وبالاً عليه . ومن كان كذلك ، فهو أعظم المفترين ، لأعظم المنعمين. فكيف يستحق العبادة والتعظيم ؟ ولهذا السبب قال الجبائي في تفسير قوله : نعم المولى ونعم النصير : إن كان تعالى يخلق الكفر في الكافر في الدنيا ثم يعذبه عليه أبد الآباد في الآخرة ، فحينئذ لا يكون هو - تعالى - نعم المولى ونعم النصير بل يكون : بئس المولى وبئس النصير . الثاني : إنه - تعالى - إنما يستحق العبادة على العبد ، لو كان العبد قادراً على فعل العبادة . أما إذا كان العبد غير قادر على العبادة . بل يكون القادر على العبادة هو الله تعالى لزم أن يقال : إنه استحق على نفسه أن يعبد نفسه ، وذلك غير معقول قال أهل السنة : كلامكم مبني على أن لفظ الله ، معناه : المستحق للعبادة . وهذا باطل . ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : إنا بينا في تفسير أسماء الله : أن « الله » : اسم علم غير مشتق الثاني : لو سلمنا أنه مشتق ، لكن لا نسلم أن معناه : أنه الذي يستحق العبادة . ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : إنه لو كان كذلك ، لوجب أن لا يكون إلهاً للجمادات والبهائم ، لأنه - تعالى - يستحق عليها العبادة ، ولزم أن لا يكون إلهاً في الأزل لم يخلق أحداً ، ولم ينعم على أحد فلم يكن مستحقاً للعبادة الثاني : إنا بينا : أن العقل لا يدل على حصول الاستحقاق . لأنه لا يتفاوت حال المعبود بسبب هذه العبادة - وهي شاقة على العابد - فوجب أن لا يحكم العقل بوجوبه إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : الإله هو القادر على الاختراع . والدليل عليه : أنه تعالى إنما ظهر الامتياز بينه وبين سائر الذوات بصفته الخلاقية . فقال : أفمن يخلق كمن لا يخلق ؟ ولما كان المذكور في معرض الامتياز هو هذه الصفة ، علمنا : أن معني الإلهية ليس إلا الخلاقية . فلو كان العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه ، لكان خالقاً لها ، وحينئذ يبطل الفرق بين الإله ، وبين العبد في
The Mu'tazilites said: al-Ilah is the one to whom worship is due. And this meaning is necessary on the doctrine of predetermination (al-jabr). Its explanation is from two aspects: First: He, exalted, only deserves worship because from Him proceeds utmost grace and beneficence. But if He created disbelief in them (the servants) to lead them gradually to the Fire, then He would not be beneficent to the servants. Also: every one who believes at present is not safe from that his final outcome might be apostasy and disbelief. In that case everything that he was upon would become a burden upon him. Whoever is like that is the greatest slanderer against the greatest benefactor. So how can He deserve worship and glorification? For this reason al-Jubba'i said in the interpretation of His statement "What an excellent Master and what an excellent Helper": if He, exalted, creates disbelief in the disbeliever in this world and then punishes him for it forever in the afterlife, then He, exalted, would not be "what an excellent Master and what an excellent Helper" but rather "what an evil master and what an evil helper." Second: He, exalted, deserves worship from the servant only if the servant is able to perform worship. But if the servant is not able to perform worship, rather the one able to worship is God, then it would be necessary to say that He deserves from Himself to worship Himself, and that is not reasonable. The people of the Sunna said: Your statement is based on the word "Allah" meaning the one deserving worship. This is false, and two aspects indicate it: First: we have shown in the interpretation of the names of God that "Allah" is a proper name, not derived. Second: if we grant that it is derived, we do not grant that its meaning is the one who deserves worship. Two aspects indicate that: First: if that were so, then it would be necessary that He is not an ilah for inanimate objects and animals, because He, exalted, deserves worship from them, and it would be necessary that He is not an ilah in pre-eternity when He had not created anyone and had not bestowed favor on anyone, so He would not be deserving of worship. Second: we have shown that the intellect does not indicate the attainment of deservingness. Because the state of the worshipped does not change due to this worship—while it is burdensome on the worshiper—so it is necessary that the intellect does not rule it as obligatory. When this is established, we say: al-Ilah is the one powerful over creation. The proof for it is that He, exalted, only manifested the distinction between Himself and other essences by His attribute of creatorship. He said: "Is He who creates like one who does not create?" And since this attribute is mentioned in the context of distinction, we know that the meaning of divinity (al-ilahiyya) is nothing other than creatorship. So if the servant were the originator of his own actions, he would be a creator of them, and then the distinction between al-Ilah and the servant would be nullified in
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage presents a debate between the Mu'tazilites and the Sunnis (Ash'aris) over the definition of "Ilah". The Mu'tazilites define Ilah as the one who deserves worship, and they argue that this definition is incompatible with the doctrine of predestination (al-jabr) because if God predestines disbelief, He would not be deserving of worship. They give two proofs: first, if God creates disbelief and then punishes, He is not beneficent; second, if the servant cannot perform worship, then only God can worship Himself, which is absurd. The Sunnis respond by refuting the Mu'tazilite definition of the word "Allah". They first assert that "Allah" is a proper name, not derived from any root. Second, even if derived, it does not mean "deserving worship". They provide two arguments: (i) if it meant that, then inanimate objects and animals would also owe worship, and God in pre-eternity would not be Ilah; (ii) the intellect does not dictate deservingness because worship is burdensome and does not benefit the worshipped. Then the Sunnis establish their own definition: Ilah is the one powerful over creation (al-qadir ala al-ikhtira'). The proof is that the Quran distinguishes the Creator from other beings, so the essence of divinity is creatorship. Therefore, if humans create their own actions, they would share in the divine attribute and the distinction collapses—thus arguing against Mu'tazilite free will.

العبادة
الاستحقاق
الخلاقية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the debate is merely about the etymology of the word 'Allah'.
Correction: The debate is fundamentally about the nature of divine attributes and the implication for human free will; the Sunnis' definition of Ilah as Creator directly refutes Mu'tazilite free will.

Checkpoint
According to the Sunnis in this passage, what is the correct meaning of 'Ilah'?
A) The one deserving worship
B) The one powerful over creation
C) The one who creates actions in servants
D) The one who is worshipped by all

Key Takeaway
The Sunnis define 'Ilah' as the Creator, refuting the Mu'tazilite definition based on deservingness, thereby defending divine uniqueness against human free will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your daily struggles and achievements, do you genuinely feel that every breath, every thought, and every action is given by the Creator alone? Or do you secretly take credit for your own 'creations'? This lesson demolishes the illusion of self-creation—will you now surrender every part of your life to Allah as the sole Creator and Sovereign?
?
Imagine yourself before Allah on the Day of Judgment, standing with nothing but His mercy. Did your worship ever make you feel entitled to Paradise? This lesson teaches that Allah deserves worship not because you benefit Him, but because He is the Creator. How will you change your worship to be purely out of recognition of His right, not your own expectation?
?
If you were to claim any power over your life independent of Allah—even the power to choose to read this lesson—you would be blurring the line between Creator and created. What specific area of your life do you most need to admit that you are not in control, and how will you actively submit that to Allah today?

Mu'tazila Objection on Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim and the Sunni Response
Mu'tazila objection and partial Sunni response regarding divine names Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that God's names Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim are incompatible with predestination, and the initial Sunni rebuttal based on the dilemma of power and motive.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's objection against predestination backfires through the dilemma of necessary versus coincidental acts.

In this passage, the Mu'tazila argue that if all actions are from God, then God's punishment outweighs His mercy, contradicting His names Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim. They present their own view that evil comes from the servant's choice. The Sunni response turns the tables by exposing a dilemma in the Mu'tazila's own position regarding power and motive.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila objection
Carefully read the Mu'tazila's argument that God's mercy is contradicted if He creates disbelief. Understand their supporting verses.
2
Identify the Sunni response's first aspect
Find where Ahl al-Sunna begin their rebuttal. They present a dilemma about power and motive.
The passage cuts off, but the dilemma is clear.
3
Analyze the dilemma
Consider the two options: either the act is necessary given power and motive, or it is not. Both lead to problems for the Mu'tazila.
4
Reflect on the incompleteness
Note that the full Sunni argument is not present; only the first aspect is given. The lesson presents this partial argument.
This is due to the source cutting off.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث في المباحث الواقعة في قولنا الرحمن الرحيم . قالت المعتزلة : لو كان الكفر والفسق كله من الله ، لا من العبد ، لكان المغضوب عليه من الخلق ، أكثر من المرحوم . لأن أهل النار أكثر من أهل الجنة . ومن كان عقابه وضرره أكثر من رحمته وإحسانه ، لم يكن رحماناً . لأن الرحمن هو الكامل في الرحمة، والرحيم من صفة الرحمة بشرط أن لا يحصل نقيضه . قالوا : فأما على مذهبنا فإنه يتم لنا القول بأنه الرحمن الرحيم ، لأنه تعالى خلق الخلق كلهم للنفع والرحمة، وأما الذين كفروا وفسقوا فمن جهة أنفسهم . ونقول في ذلك كما قال تعالى : ( إن الله لا يغير ما بقوم ، حتى يغيروا ما بأنفسهم (١)) وقال : ما يفعل الله بعذابكم . إن شكرتم وآمنتم (۲) ) وقال : ( قل : ما يعبثوا بكم ربي . لولا دعاؤكم ) (۳) فثبت : آن کونه رحمانا رحيماً لا يثبت إلا على قولنا . قال أهل السنة والجماعة : هذا الذي أوردتم علينا ، وارد عليكم وبيانه من وجوه : الأول : عند حصول القدرة والداعية . إما أن يجب الفعل ، أو لا يجب . فإن وجب ، لزمكم ما ألزمتموه علينا . لأنه تعالى هو الخالق لتلك القدرة ولتلك الداعية . وهما يوجبان الكفر والفسق . فكان ذلك إجبار للعبد على القبح . وأما إن قلتم : إن عند حصول القدرة والداعية لا يجب الفعل فحينئذ يكون صدور الفعل اتفاقياً ، والاتفاقي لا يكون مقدوراً للعبد . فيترتب
Third chapter on investigations concerning our saying 'Ar-Rahman Ar-Rahim'. The Mu'tazila said: If disbelief and sin were entirely from God, not from the servant, then those of creation with wrath upon them would be more than the mercifully treated. Because the people of the Fire are more than the people of Paradise. And whoever's punishment and harm are more than His mercy and beneficence, he would not be Rahman. Because Ar-Rahman is the perfect in mercy, and Ar-Rahim is an attribute of mercy with the condition that its opposite does not occur. They said: As for our doctrine, the saying that He is Ar-Rahman Ar-Rahim is established for us, because He the Exalted created all of creation for benefit and mercy; as for those who disbelieve and sin, it is from their own selves. And we say in that as Allah the Exalted said: 'Indeed, Allah does not change the condition of a people until they change what is in themselves.' And He said: 'What would Allah do with your punishment if you are grateful and believe?' And He said: 'Say: My Lord would not care for you were it not for your supplication.' So it is established that His being Rahman and Rahim is only established according to our doctrine. Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a said: This which you have brought against us is brought back against you. Its explanation is from several aspects: The first: When power and motive are present, either the act is necessary or not necessary. If it is necessary, then what you have charged us with applies to you. Because He the Exalted is the creator of that power and that motive, and they necessitate disbelief and sin. So that would be compulsion of the servant to evil. And if you say that when power and motive are present the act is not necessary, then the occurrence of the act would be coincidental, and the coincidental is not within the servant's power. So it follows...
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer incompletely presented. It begins with the Mu'tazila objection: if all actions (including disbelief and sin) are from God, then God punishes more than He rewards, contradicting the names Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim. They support their view that evil comes from the servant's own choice, citing Quranic verses. Then Ahl al-Sunna respond by turning the argument back on the Mu'tazila. They present the first of several aspects: the problem of power and motive. If the Mu'tazila hold that when power and motive are present, the act becomes necessary, then since God creates both power and motive, He would be compelling the servant to evil – the very charge they leveled against the Sunnis. If instead they say the act is not necessary, then the act would be coincidental, which cannot be within the servant's power. The passage is cut off here, but the argument is that the Mu'tazila cannot avoid the dilemma.

Correction: The Sunni response shows that the Mu'tazila face the same dilemma: their own position either leads to compulsion or to an unintelligible accidental act.

C) Either the servant has free will or not
D) Either the Quran is created or uncreated

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila objection that God's mercy is incompatible with predestination is countered by the Sunni dilemma: the Mu'tazila's own view cannot escape either compulsion or accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the names Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim, do you truly trust that God's mercy is perfect even when you see suffering? This lesson shows that the Mu'tazila objection fails; but more importantly, does your heart rest in God's mercy despite not fully understanding His decree? What would change in your life if you truly believed that His mercy is not diminished by allowing disbelief?
?
Consider the dilemma: either God compels or actions are accidental. The Sunnis affirm that God creates actions but man acquires them. Are you comfortable with this mystery? Do you ever feel that your sins are 'coincidental'—something that just happened—rather than taking full responsibility? How can the teaching of acquisition (kasb) make you more accountable before God?
?
The Mu'tazila used a verse about God not changing a people until they change themselves. How often do you blame external circumstances or even God's decree for your spiritual state, rather than looking at your own choices? This lesson challenges you to examine: are you truly changing what is in yourself, or are you using theological arguments as an excuse?

The Absurdity of Commanding Against God's Knowledge
First part of second Sunni proof: absurdity of commanding against God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why commanding a person God knows will not obey is rationally absurd and incompatible with mercy.

Core Idea
Commanding against God's knowledge is like asking to change the unchangeable.

This lesson examines the second Sunni proof against the Mu'tazila. The argument builds on the premise that God's knowledge is infallible: what He knows must happen, and its opposite is impossible. Therefore, commanding a person He knows will die in disbelief to believe is effectively asking him to change God's knowledge—an absurdity that cannot be merciful.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the argument starts with the impossibility of opposing God's knowledge.
The passage uses 'خلاف معلوم الله' (opposite of what God knows).
2
Identify the core claim
The claim is that commanding a known disbeliever to believe is like saying 'Change God's knowledge or I will punish you.' This is the absurd consequence.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: if God's knowledge is fixed, can a command to do otherwise be meaningful or merciful? The Sunni answer is no.
This challenges the Mu'tazila view that God must command what is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن خلاف معلوم الله ممتنع الوقوع . فإذا قال لمن علم أنه يموت على الكفر : كلفتك بالإيمان. فكأنه قال له : غير علم الله وإلا عاقبتك . ومعلوم أن هذا لا يليق بالرحمة البتة . لأن كل ما أوصل الله من الرحمات إلى عباده ، لا يخلو إما أن يقال : إنه تعالى أحوجهم إليها وجعلهم مشتهين لها ، أو ما فعل ذلك . فإن كان الأول لم يكن ذلك رحمة . لأنه تعالى لما أحوجهم إليها ، ثم أعطاهم ذلك المحتاج إليه ، جرى هذا مجرى ما إذا خرق بطن إنسان ثم يداويه . فإن ذلك لا يعد رحمة. وإن كان الثاني وهو أنه تعالى ما أحوجهم إليها ، وما جعلهم مشتهين لها ، راغبين في حصولها ، لم يكن إيصال ذلك نفعاً في حقهم. ألا ترى أن إلقاء العظم إلى الكلب إحسان إليه . لأنه يشتهي ذلك العظم . أما إلقاء قلادة من الدر إليه ، فإنه لا يكون إحساناً إليه . لأنه لا يشتهي ذلك فثبت أن على القسمين لا تعقل حصول الرحمة. وهذا بخلاف الإحسان الصادر من الخلق ، لأن الحاجة ما حصلت من قبل هذا المحسن ، بل كانت الحاجة موجودة في ذات ذلك الشخص . فإذا سعى إنسان في دفع تلك الحاجة ، كان ذلك إحساناً . أما الحق سبحانه فهو الذي أحوج إلى ذلك الشخص ، فإذا أعطى المحتاج إليه ، جرى ذلك مجرى ما إذا أساء ثم أحسن بقدر تلك الإساءة ، ومجرى ما إذا جرح ثم عالج ، فإن ذلك لا يكون إحساناً . فثبت : أن هذا الإلزام وارد عليهم
The second: Verily, the opposite of what God knows is impossible to occur. So when He says to the one whom He knows will die in disbelief: 'I charge you with faith,' it is as if He said to him: 'Change God's knowledge, or I will punish you.' And it is known that this is not suitable for mercy at all. Because all the mercies that God conveys to His servants are either such that it is said: He Almighty made them need them and made them desire them, or He did not do that. If the first, then that is not mercy. Because when He made them need them, then gave them what they needed, this is like someone who rips open a person's belly and then treats it—that is not considered mercy. And if the second, that He Almighty did not make them need them, and did not make them desire them or yearn for their acquisition, then conveying that is not a benefit to them. Do you not see that throwing a bone to a dog is kindness to it because it desires that bone? As for throwing a necklace of pearls to it, it is not kindness to it because it does not desire it. So it is established that in both cases, the occurrence of mercy is not conceivable. This is unlike beneficence from creatures, because the need did not arise from this benefactor; rather the need existed in the person himself. So if a person strives to remove that need, that is beneficence. As for the Truth, exalted, He is the one who made that person needy, so if He gives him what he needs, this is like one who does evil then does good to the extent of that evil, and like one who wounds then treats—that is not beneficence. So it is established that this objection applies to them.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is the second Sunni response. It consists of two main points. First, the Sunni scholars argue that God's knowledge is infallible: what God knows will happen must happen, and its opposite is impossible. Commanding a person He knows will die in disbelief to believe is effectively asking him to change God's knowledge, which is absurd and not merciful. Second, they analyze the concept of mercy (rahma) itself. When God grants something to a servant, either He created the need and desire for it in that servant, or He did not. If He created the need, then giving what is needed is like causing harm and then repairing it—not true mercy. If He did not create the need, then the gift is useless, like throwing pearls to a dog that desires bones. In either case, it cannot be mercy. Unlike human beneficence, where the need already exists independently of the benefactor, God is the originator of the need. Therefore, the Mu'tazila's objection that God's mercy is compromised by predestination actually applies to their own view.

Key Terms
معلوم الله
التكليف
الرحمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God can command something He knows will not happen without contradiction.
Correction: God's knowledge is immutable; commanding against it is absurd and not merciful.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does commanding a known disbeliever to believe implicitly demand?
A) That the person obey
B) That God change His knowledge
C) That the person be punished
D) That mercy be shown

Key Takeaway
Commanding what God knows will not happen is rationally absurd and cannot be merciful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation where you desperately wanted something that you knew deep down was not meant for you. How does the truth that God's knowledge is unchangeable affect the way you pray and surrender?
?
This lesson shows that it is absurd to command someone to change God's knowledge. In what areas of your life are you still trying to 'change God's knowledge' by resisting His decree? What would it mean for you to accept His knowledge with trust?
?
If mercy cannot be built on an absurd demand, then what kind of mercy does God actually offer you? How can you stop demanding that God be merciful in your terms, and instead receive His mercy as He has defined it?

Redefining Mercy: the Dog Analogy
Second part of proof: analysis of mercy and analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni argument that true mercy requires the recipient to have a genuine need and desire for the gift.

Core Idea
True mercy requires need and desire; otherwise it's a useless gift.

After establishing the absurdity of commanding against God's knowledge, this lesson unpacks the deeper analysis of mercy. The passage argues that for a gift to be merciful, it must either be needed and desired (but then God created that need, making the gift like repairing a self-inflicted wound) or not needed (making it useless, like pearls to a dog). This redefinition shows that the Mu'tazila's understanding of mercy is flawed.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage on mercy
Read the Arabic passage from 'لأن كل ما أوصل الله' to the end. Notice the two cases and the analogy.
2
Understand the two cases
Case 1: God creates the need and then fulfills it – like ripping a belly then treating it, not mercy. Case 2: God does not create the need – then the gift is not beneficial, like pearls to a dog.
3
Contrast with human beneficence
Unlike humans, God is the originator of needs. So giving what He Himself made necessary is not mercy but repair of His own doing.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that the Mu'tazila objection actually applies to their own view. Mercy is redefined consistently with God's sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن خلاف معلوم الله ممتنع الوقوع . فإذا قال لمن علم أنه يموت على الكفر : كلفتك بالإيمان. فكأنه قال له : غير علم الله وإلا عاقبتك . ومعلوم أن هذا لا يليق بالرحمة البتة . لأن كل ما أوصل الله من الرحمات إلى عباده ، لا يخلو إما أن يقال : إنه تعالى أحوجهم إليها وجعلهم مشتهين لها ، أو ما فعل ذلك . فإن كان الأول لم يكن ذلك رحمة . لأنه تعالى لما أحوجهم إليها ، ثم أعطاهم ذلك المحتاج إليه ، جرى هذا مجرى ما إذا خرق بطن إنسان ثم يداويه . فإن ذلك لا يعد رحمة. وإن كان الثاني وهو أنه تعالى ما أحوجهم إليها ، وما جعلهم مشتهين لها ، راغبين في حصولها ، لم يكن إيصال ذلك نفعاً في حقهم. ألا ترى أن إلقاء العظم إلى الكلب إحسان إليه . لأنه يشتهي ذلك العظم . أما إلقاء قلادة من الدر إليه ، فإنه لا يكون إحساناً إليه . لأنه لا يشتهي ذلك فثبت أن على القسمين لا تعقل حصول الرحمة. وهذا بخلاف الإحسان الصادر من الخلق ، لأن الحاجة ما حصلت من قبل هذا المحسن ، بل كانت الحاجة موجودة في ذات ذلك الشخص . فإذا سعى إنسان في دفع تلك الحاجة ، كان ذلك إحساناً . أما الحق سبحانه فهو الذي أحوج إلى ذلك الشخص ، فإذا أعطى المحتاج إليه ، جرى ذلك مجرى ما إذا أساء ثم أحسن بقدر تلك الإساءة ، ومجرى ما إذا جرح ثم عالج ، فإن ذلك لا يكون إحساناً . فثبت : أن هذا الإلزام وارد عليهم
The second: Verily, the opposite of what God knows is impossible to occur. So when He says to the one whom He knows will die in disbelief: 'I charge you with faith,' it is as if He said to him: 'Change God's knowledge, or I will punish you.' And it is known that this is not suitable for mercy at all. Because all the mercies that God conveys to His servants are either such that it is said: He Almighty made them need them and made them desire them, or He did not do that. If the first, then that is not mercy. Because when He made them need them, then gave them what they needed, this is like someone who rips open a person's belly and then treats it—that is not considered mercy. And if the second, that He Almighty did not make them need them, and did not make them desire them or yearn for their acquisition, then conveying that is not a benefit to them. Do you not see that throwing a bone to a dog is kindness to it because it desires that bone? As for throwing a necklace of pearls to it, it is not kindness to it because it does not desire it. So it is established that in both cases, the occurrence of mercy is not conceivable. This is unlike beneficence from creatures, because the need did not arise from this benefactor; rather the need existed in the person himself. So if a person strives to remove that need, that is beneficence. As for the Truth, exalted, He is the one who made that person needy, so if He gives him what he needs, this is like one who does evil then does good to the extent of that evil, and like one who wounds then treats—that is not beneficence. So it is established that this objection applies to them.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is the second Sunni response. It consists of two main points. First, the Sunni scholars argue that God's knowledge is infallible: what God knows will happen must happen, and its opposite is impossible. Commanding a person He knows will die in disbelief to believe is effectively asking him to change God's knowledge, which is absurd and not merciful. Second, they analyze the concept of mercy (rahma) itself. When God grants something to a servant, either He created the need and desire for it in that servant, or He did not. If He created the need, then giving what is needed is like causing harm and then repairing it—not true mercy. If He did not create the need, then the gift is useless, like throwing pearls to a dog that desires bones. In either case, it cannot be mercy. Unlike human beneficence, where the need already exists independently of the benefactor, God is the originator of the need. Therefore, the Mu'tazila's objection that God's mercy is compromised by predestination actually applies to their own view.

الرحمة
الحاجة
الإحسان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That mercy means simply giving something good regardless of the recipient's state.
Correction: True mercy requires the recipient to have a genuine need and desire; otherwise it's not mercy.

Checkpoint
In the dog analogy, why is throwing a pearl necklace not kindness?
A) Because pearls are worthless
B) Because the dog does not desire pearls
C) Because the necklace is too heavy
D) Because dogs only like bones

Key Takeaway
Mercy from God requires genuine need and desire; otherwise, it is either repair of self-inflicted harm or useless gift.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you receive a blessing from God, do you ever stop to ask yourself whether you truly desired it, or are you simply taking it as a routine provision? How does the dog-pearl analogy challenge your perception of God's gifts?
?
Think of a time you felt God was unjust because He gave you something you didn't ask for. Does this passage convict you of ingratitude? How can you shift from entitlement to recognizing that even the creation of your need is a divine act?
?
The passage says God's mercy is not like human beneficence. How does that change your reliance on God? Are you ready to accept that your very neediness is from Him, and that every gift is a response to a need He created? What would it mean for you to live in full awareness of this dependency?

Fourth Refutation: Does God's Foreknowledge Make Creation Futile?
Fourth proof: God's knowledge of the disbeliever's fate undermines mercy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial premise of the fourth Sunni refutation against the Mu'tazilite claim that creation and obligation are merciful.

Core Idea
God's foreknowledge of disbelief shows creation is not purely merciful.

This lesson introduces the fourth argument presented by Sunni theologians against the Mu'tazilite assertion that God's creation and obligation are fundamentally merciful. The argument begins with a simple yet profound premise: God knows from eternity that certain individuals will never believe and will only gain eternal punishment. This challenges the notion that creation for such individuals is an act of mercy. The passage is incomplete, but the direction of reasoning is clear.

The argument likely concludes that such creation and obligation cannot be attributed to a merciful God if they only lead to punishment. The incomplete nature of the text leaves the full reasoning for the next page, but the direction is clear.
