First Proof: God's Handiwork Is Good
First Quranic proof that God's acts are good

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two Quranic verses establish that everything God makes is good, and why evil cannot be attributed to Him.

Core Idea
God's perfect handiwork excludes all evil.

The author begins the discussion on divine values by citing two verses that declare God's creation to be perfect and good. From these verses, he draws a logical inference: if God's handiwork is exclusively good, then anything that is not good cannot come from God. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its implications for understanding the origin of evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the two cited verses
Read the Arabic of the two verses: 'صنع الله الذي أتقن كل شيء' and 'الذي أحسن كل شيء خلقه'. Pay attention to the words 'أتقن' (perfected) and 'أحسن' (made good).
These verses are from Surah an-Naml (27:88) and Surah as-Sajdah (32:7).
2
Understand the logical inference
The author says: These verses show that everything that is God's handiwork is good. Therefore, anything that is not good cannot be God's handiwork. Follow the 'if-then' structure here.
3
Apply to evil and obscenities
Ugly deeds and obscenities are not described as good, so they cannot be acts of God. This is the key conclusion of the proof.
This refutes the idea that God creates both good and evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع العاشر القيم
الآيات الدالة على أن أفعاله حسنة، ومنزهة عن التفاوت والعبث والباطل فإحداها : قوله : ( صنع الله الذي أتقن كل شيء (۱) ) - ( الذي أحسن كل شيء خلقه (۲) ) فهاتان (۳) الآيتان دالتان على أن كل ما كان صنعاً الله ، فهو حسن . وهذا يقتضي أن لا يكون حسناً ، لم يكن صنعاً الله تعالى فالقبائح والفواحش لما لم تكن موصوفة بالحسن . وجب أن لا تكون فعلا الله تعالى .
وثانيها : قوله تعالى : ( وما خلقنا السموات والأرض وما بينهما باطلاً ذلك ظن الذين كفروا (٤) ) فنص على أن من اعتقد أنه تعالى خلق باطلا ، فقد كفر
وثالثها : قوله تعالى : ( ربنا ما خلقت هذا باطلا (ه) ) دل هذا الكلام على تنزيه الله تعالى عن خلق الباطل، فوجب أن يقال : إن كل ما كان باطلاً ، فإنه لا يكون مخلوقاً .
The tenth type, the values. The verses indicating that His actions are good and free from inequality, frivolity, and falsehood. The first of them: His saying: (The handiwork of Allah who has perfected everything (1)) - (Who has given everything its creation (2)). These two (3) verses indicate that everything that is the handiwork of Allah is good. This requires that whatever is not good is not the handiwork of Allah, the Exalted. So ugly deeds and obscenities, since they are not described as good, must not be an act of Allah, the Exalted. The second of them: His saying, the Exalted: (And We did not create the heavens and the earth and whatever is between them in vain; that is the opinion of those who disbelieve (4)). So He stated that whoever believes that He, the Exalted, created in vain has indeed disbelieved. The third of them: His saying, the Exalted: (Our Lord, You did not create this in vain (5)). This speech indicates the transcendence of Allah, the Exalted, above creating falsehood. So it must be said: Indeed, everything that is false is not created.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents three Quranic proofs (adillah) that God's actions are good (ḥasan) and free from imperfection, frivolity, and falsehood (bāṭil). The author first announces the topic as 'the tenth type, the values' (al-nawʿ al-ʿāshir al-qiyam), indicating a classification of proofs regarding divine acts. The first proof cites two verses: 'The handiwork of Allah who has perfected everything' (Sūrat al-Naml 27:88) and 'Who has given everything its creation' (Sūrat al-Sajdah 32:7). The author argues that these verses show that everything that is God's handiwork (ṣunʿ Allāh) is good. Therefore, anything not good cannot be God's handiwork. Consequently, ugly deeds and obscenities (al-qabāʾiḥ wa-l-fawāḥish) — since they are not described as good — must not be acts of God. The second proof quotes 'And We did not create the heavens and the earth and whatever is between them in vain' (Sūrat Ṣād 38:27), noting that the verse explicitly declares that believing God created in vain is disbelief (kufr). The third proof cites the supplication 'Our Lord, You did not create this in vain' (Sūrat Āl ʿImrān 3:191), concluding that this verse establishes God's transcendence (tanzīh) above creating falsehood, so everything false cannot be created by God. Taken together, the three proofs form a cumulative argument: God's acts are exclusively good, and evil or falsehood cannot be attributed to Him as His creation.

Key Terms
حسن
صنع الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that God creates both good and evil equally.
Correction: The verses show that God's handiwork is exclusively good; evil cannot be His act.

Checkpoint
What logical conclusion does the author draw from the first two verses?
A) That God creates both good and evil.
B) That only what is made by God is good, so evil cannot be from God.
C) That God creates everything including evil.
D) That these verses are not relevant to the discussion.

Key Takeaway
God's acts are perfectly good, so evil cannot be attributed to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship or witness tragedy, do you ever feel that God has wronged you? Based on this lesson, how must you realign your heart to see that any pain is not from His handiwork? Will you today trust that His handiwork is only good, even when you cannot see it?
?
Have you ever blamed your sinful tendencies on your nature or upbringing, as if God created you that way? The lesson teaches that ugly deeds are not from God. Will you take full responsibility for your actions and seek His help to change, knowing He only creates good?
?
If you truly believe that everything God does is good, why do you still complain about your circumstances? What would change in your daily life if you internalized that every event from Allah is perfect? Commit to praising Him in every situation for the next week.

Second Proof: Creation Not in Vain
Second Quranic proof that creation is not in vain

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how verse 38:27 refutes the belief that God created the universe in vain, thereby establishing that evil cannot be part of God's creation.

Core Idea
Believing God created in vain is disbelief.

The second proof shifts to a direct Quranic statement about the purposefulness of creation. The verse declares that God did not create the heavens and earth in vain, and labeling such an opinion as disbelief. This lesson explores how this verse reinforces the idea that God's acts are good and meaningful.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse
Read the Arabic: 'وما خلقنا السموات والأرض وما بينهما باطلاً ذلك ظن الذين كفروا'. Notice the word 'باطلاً' (in vain/falsehood).
This is from Surah Sad (38:27).
2
Understand the implication
The verse denies that creation is vain (باطل). Since evil is often considered vain or purposeless, this verse implies that evil is not part of God's creation.
The author emphasizes that believing otherwise is kufr (disbelief).
3
Connect to the overall argument
This proof complements the first: since God's creation is not in vain, everything He creates has purpose and goodness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

4 min
Third Proof: God's Transcendence Above Falsehood
Third Quranic proof that God does not create falsehood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the supplication in verse 3:191 confirms that God is free from creating falsehood, and how this completes the proof that evil is not created by God.

Core Idea
God is transcendent; He does not create falsehood.

The third proof comes from a verse where the believers praise God, declaring that He did not create anything in vain. The author argues that this statement implies God's transcendence above falsehood, so everything false cannot be created by Him. This lesson ties together the theme that God's acts are exclusively good.

Guided Path
1
Read the supplication
Read the Arabic: 'ربنا ما خلقت هذا باطلا'. Notice the address 'ربنا' (Our Lord) and the negation 'ما خلقت' (You did not create).
This is from Surah Al Imran (3:191).
2
Recognize the logical implication
The author says this speech indicates Allah's transcendence (تنزيه) above creating falsehood. Therefore, anything false (باطل) is not created by Him.
This is similar to the first proof's structure.
3
Synthesize all three proofs
Each proof eliminates falsehood, inequality, and frivolity from God's actions. Together they form a strong argument that evil is not from God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع العاشر القيم
الآيات الدالة على أن أفعاله حسنة، ومنزهة عن التفاوت والعبث والباطل فإحداها : قوله : ( صنع الله الذي أتقن كل شيء (۱) ) - ( الذي أحسن كل شيء خلقه (۲) ) فهاتان (۳) الآيتان دالتان على أن كل ما كان صنعاً الله ، فهو حسن . وهذا يقتضي أن لا يكون حسناً ، لم يكن صنعاً الله تعالى فالقبائح والفواحش لما لم تكن موصوفة بالحسن . وجب أن لا تكون فعلا الله تعالى .
وثانيها : قوله تعالى : ( وما خلقنا السموات والأرض وما بينهما باطلاً ذلك ظن الذين كفروا (٤) ) فنص على أن من اعتقد أنه تعالى خلق باطلا ، فقد كفر
وثالثها : قوله تعالى : ( ربنا ما خلقت هذا باطلا (ه) ) دل هذا الكلام على تنزيه الله تعالى عن خلق الباطل، فوجب أن يقال : إن كل ما كان باطلاً ، فإنه لا يكون مخلوقاً .
The tenth type, the values. The verses indicating that His actions are good and free from inequality, frivolity, and falsehood. The first of them: His saying: (The handiwork of Allah who has perfected everything (1)) - (Who has given everything its creation (2)). These two (3) verses indicate that everything that is the handiwork of Allah is good. This requires that whatever is not good is not the handiwork of Allah, the Exalted. So ugly deeds and obscenities, since they are not described as good, must not be an act of Allah, the Exalted. The second of them: His saying, the Exalted: (And We did not create the heavens and the earth and whatever is between them in vain; that is the opinion of those who disbelieve (4)). So He stated that whoever believes that He, the Exalted, created in vain has indeed disbelieved. The third of them: His saying, the Exalted: (Our Lord, You did not create this in vain (5)). This speech indicates the transcendence of Allah, the Exalted, above creating falsehood. So it must be said: Indeed, everything that is false is not created.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents three Quranic proofs (adillah) that God's actions are good (ḥasan) and free from imperfection, frivolity, and falsehood (bāṭil). The author first announces the topic as 'the tenth type, the values' (al-nawʿ al-ʿāshir al-qiyam), indicating a classification of proofs regarding divine acts. The first proof cites two verses: 'The handiwork of Allah who has perfected everything' (Sūrat al-Naml 27:88) and 'Who has given everything its creation' (Sūrat al-Sajdah 32:7). The author argues that these verses show that everything that is God's handiwork (ṣunʿ Allāh) is good. Therefore, anything not good cannot be God's handiwork. Consequently, ugly deeds and obscenities (al-qabāʾiḥ wa-l-fawāḥish) — since they are not described as good — must not be acts of God. The second proof quotes 'And We did not create the heavens and the earth and whatever is between them in vain' (Sūrat Ṣād 38:27), noting that the verse explicitly declares that believing God created in vain is disbelief (kufr). The third proof cites the supplication 'Our Lord, You did not create this in vain' (Sūrat Āl ʿImrān 3:191), concluding that this verse establishes God's transcendence (tanzīh) above creating falsehood, so everything false cannot be created by God. Taken together, the three proofs form a cumulative argument: God's acts are exclusively good, and evil or falsehood cannot be attributed to Him as His creation.

Key Terms
باطل
تنزيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion the author draws from the verse 'ربنا ما خلقت هذا باطلا'?
A) That God created falsehood as a test.
B) That everything false is not created by God.
C) That the verse is just a prayer, not a proof.
D) That falsehood is created but not by God.

Key Takeaway
God is transcendent; He does not create falsehood, so evil is not His creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own life, where have you been creating or perpetuating falsehood—lies, excuses, or deceits—while believing it is somehow from Allah? Will you repent now and stop attributing falsehood to Him?
?
If everything false is not created by Allah, then every injustice, every gossip, every broken promise originates from you. How does this truth shift your responsibility for your actions today?
?
Are there areas where you blame Allah for bad outcomes, ignoring that falsehood comes from your own choices? Will you now take full accountability and seek His forgiveness for the falsehood you have created?

Proof from Qur'anic Challenge and Objection on Human Creative Power
Presents an objection and answer about the implication of the Qur'anic challenge for human creative power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'anic challenge is used to argue for human creative power and how the Ash'ari school answers this objection.

Core Idea
The Qur'anic challenge implies human ability, but not independent creation.

In this passage, the author presents a key objection against the Ash'ari position on human action. The Qur'an challenges people to produce a surah like it, which seems to require that humans have genuine creative power. If they do not, the challenge would be meaningless. The Ash'ari answer defends the position that human power combined with motive can produce actions, without requiring ex nihilo creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read carefully the Arabic text and its English rendering. Notice the structure: citation, inference, objection, answer.
2
Understand the inference from the challenge
The author explains that a challenge is meaningful only if the challenged person has some ability. The inability to produce a surah despite general ability indicates a special quality of the Qur'an.
This sets up the objection.
3
Examine the objection
The objection argues that if humans cannot originate or create at all, then the challenge fails. But since the miracle is established, humans must be creators.
4
Analyze the Ash'ari answer
The answer concedes that power with motive can produce some actions. The difference in ease comes from a special quality in the act, not from lack of human power.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider how this argument fits into the kasb theory of acquisition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني عشر القوم

الآيات الدالة على التحدي بالقرآن

قال تعالى : ( وإن كنتم في ريب مما نزلنا على عبدنا فأتوا بسورة من مثله ) إلى قوله : ( فإن لم تفعلوا ولن تفعلوا فاتقوا النار التي وقودها الناس والحجارة )

ووجه الاستدلال به : إن التحدي مع من يقدر على شيء من الأفعال . لأنه لما كان قادراً على الفعل في الجملة ، ثم عجز عن فعل له معنى خاص . دل ذلك على أن لذلك الفعل خصوصية. لأنه لو عجز ذلك الفاعل عن الإتيان بمسألة ، ولا يقدر على الفعل أصلا فإن التحدي معه لا يفيد الفائدة المطلوبة . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إذا لم يكن العبد قادراً على الإيجاد والتكوين أصلاً ، لم يحصل التحدي بالقرآن على كونه معجزاً . ولما كان ذلك باطلاً بإجماع الأمة ، علمنا : أن العبد موجد .

والجواب : إن هذا الإشكال غير وارد علينا . لانا سلمنا أن مجموع القدرة مع الداعي قد يوجب بعض الأفعال . وإذا لم يؤثرا في أمر من الأمور ، علمنا : أن ذلك المتيسر ممتاز عن ذلك المتعسر لمزيد خاصية ، لأجلها حصل ذلك اليسر .
The twelfth type, the people. The verses indicating the challenge with the Qur'an. He said Most High: 'And if you are in doubt about what We have sent down upon Our servant, then bring a surah like it' until His saying: 'But if you do not – and you will never do – then fear the Fire whose fuel is people and stones.' And the way of inference by it: that the challenge is with one who is able to do something of actions. Because when he is able to act in general, then becomes incapable of an act with a specific meaning, that indicates that that act has a particularity. Because if that doer were incapable of performing a matter, and he cannot do the act at all, then the challenge with him does not yield the intended benefit. If this is established, we say: If the servant is not capable of origination and creation at all, then the challenge with the Qur'an as being a miracle would not occur. And since that is false by consensus of the community, we know that the servant is a creator. And the answer: This objection is not directed at us. Because we concede that the combination of power with motive may necessitate some actions. And if they do not affect any matter, we know that that which is easy is distinguished from that which is difficult due to an additional particularity, because of which that ease occurred.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer within a kalam discussion on human creative power. First, the author cites the Qur'anic challenge verses (Surah al-Baqarah 2:23-24). The inference is explained: a challenge is only meaningful when directed at someone capable of the action. If a person is generally able but fails at a specific act, that act has a special quality. If the person had no ability at all, the challenge would be pointless. From this, an objection is raised: if humans are not capable of origination (ijad) and creation (takwin) whatsoever, then the Qur'anic challenge would not be a miracle. But since the miracle is established, it follows that the servant (al-`abd) is a creator (mujid). The answer refutes this objection for the Ash'ari position: the Ash'aris hold that the combination of power (qudra) with motive (da`i) does produce some actions. Therefore, the difference between an easy action and a difficult one arises from a special quality (khassiya) in the act, not from the lack of human power. Thus, the challenge remains valid without requiring that humans create ex nihilo.

Key Terms
تحدي
إيجاد
تكوين
معجز
قدرة
داعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument proves that humans create independently of God.
Correction: The Ash'ari answer demonstrates that human action is a combination of power and motive, not independent creation ex nihilo. It supports the theory of kasb (acquisition).

Checkpoint
According to the objection in the passage, what would be the consequence if the servant is not capable of origination and creation at all?
A) The Qur'an would not be a miracle.
B) The challenge would be with someone who can do everything.
C) The servant would be equal to God.
D) The servant would not be responsible.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic challenge does not prove independent human creation; rather, it is compatible with human kasb as combination of power and motive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform any action—speaking, writing, even thinking—do you truly sense that your power is not independent but conjoined with a divine motive? What would it take for you to live each moment acknowledging that your every ability is a borrowed gift from Allah?
?
The Qur'anic challenge confronts you with a choice: either admit that the Qur'an is from God or attempt to produce its like. Have you ever let the weight of this challenge sink into your heart, moving you from passive belief to active submission? If not, what is holding you back?
?
Consider a time when you felt self-sufficient or proud of your own achievements. In light of this lesson's insistence that even the 'ease' of ordinary speech comes from Allah, how can you reorient your gratitude so that you constantly attribute every success—big or small—to Him alone?

Objection from Quranic Verses on Divine Purpose and the Response
Objection from Quranic verses on divine purpose and response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that divine creation of disbelief contradicts Quranic statements about God's purpose for obedience, and the response that an affirmative proposition suffices for action.

Core Idea
God's purpose for obedience is valid even if He creates disbelief; the affirmative command stands.

This passage presents a theological objection that seems to pit God's absolute power against His revealed purposes. The objector quotes several Quranic verses to argue that if God creates disbelief, He cannot genuinely desire obedience. The author then offers a concise response, appealing to the nature of affirmative divine commands. As we read, consider how the logical structure of divine commands might resolve this apparent tension.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection's structure
Read the objection carefully. Notice that it uses Quranic verses to claim that God's purpose in sending prophets is undermined if He also creates disbelief. Identify the logical connection between the verses and the conclusion.
The objector frames it as an impossibility: God cannot both create disbelief and desire obedience.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence
The objector cites multiple verses: 4:94, 51:56, and the phrase 'that they might become righteous' and 'perhaps he may be reminded or fear.' Reflect on how these verses imply that God wants certain actions from humans.
These verses are crucial because they appear to ascribe purpose and desire to God regarding human actions.
3
Analyze the response's core argument
The author answers with a logical point about 'affirmative propositions' (al-qadiyya al-mujiba). Understand that an affirmative command is valid for action even if the action is not self-produced. The response also mentions God making everyone a believer 'in the value.'
This defense preserves both divine sovereignty and the seriousness of the command.
4
Consider the objection's counter-move
The objector had a further point: if obedience is created by God, the verse should say 'perhaps I would be reminded and fear' (referring to God). Notice how the response avoids this by focusing on the nature of the command.
This highlights the debate about God's speech and whether it implies possibility on His part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التنوع الرابع علم اللقيم
قوله تعالى : ( وما أرسلنا من رسول إلا ليطاع بإذن الله )
دلت الآية على أن مقصوده تعالى من بعثة الأنبياء إقدام الخلق على الطاعة . فلو كان تعالى يخلق الكفر فيهم ، لامتنع أن يكون غرضه من البعثة حصول الطاعة . وهذا التمسك بقوله تعالى : ( وما خلقت الجن والإنس إلا ليعبدون ) وهكذا الاستدلال بكل ما جاء في القرآن من قوله : ( لعلهم يتقون ) - ( فقولا له : قولاً ليناً . لعله يتذكر أو يخشى ) فإن هذه النصوص وما يشبهها نصوص صريحة في أنه تعالى يريد [ من عباده فعل(*) ] الطاعة والتقوى [ ولو كانت الطاعة والتقوى ] من خلق الله تعالى لكان يجب أن يقول : لعلي أتذكر وأتقي
والجواب : إن القضية الموجبة يكفي في العمل بها ، حصول مستمر من وقت واحد وعندنا : أنه تعالى يجعل الكل مؤمناً عارفاً في القيمة
The fourth type: 'Ilm al-Laqim'. His statement, Most High: 'And We did not send any messenger except to be obeyed by permission of Allah.' The verse indicates that His purpose, Most High, from sending the prophets is the creatures' undertaking of obedience. For if He, Most High, were to create disbelief in them, it would be impossible that His purpose from the sending should be the occurrence of obedience. And this is the holding fast to His statement, Most High: 'And I did not create jinn and mankind except to worship Me.' And similarly the inference from every [verse] that has come in the Quran from His statement: 'that they might become righteous' — 'And speak to him with gentle speech that perhaps he may be reminded or fear Him.' For these texts and what resembles them are explicit texts that He, Most High, wants [from His servants the act of] obedience and piety. [And if obedience and piety were from the creation of Allah, Most High, He would have to say: 'Perhaps I would be reminded and fear.'] And the answer: The affirmative proposition (al-qadiyya al-mujiba) suffices for acting upon it; obtaining continuity from one time. And with us: that He, Most High, makes everyone a believer, knowing, in the value.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. It begins by quoting Quran 4:94, which states that messengers are sent to be obeyed. The objector infers that the purpose of prophethood is to elicit obedience from people. If God Himself were to create disbelief in them, then the purpose would be impossible—since the very obedience He aims for would be prevented by His own act. The objector then supports this with other verses, such as Quran 51:56 (creation for worship), and verses that express hope for righteousness or fear (e.g., Quran 6:51, 20:44). The claim is that these texts plainly show God wants obedience and piety from His servants. The objector adds that if obedience and piety were actually created by God, the verses would have said 'perhaps I would be reminded and fear'—meaning God would be the one acting, not the servant. The answer, though brief, argues that an affirmative proposition (like 'God wants obedience') is sufficient for acting upon it; it does not require that the obedience be self-generated. The phrase 'obtaining continuity from one time' likely means that the intention of obedience is established at a moment, and that suffices. The answer concludes by asserting that God makes everyone a believer and knowing 'in the value'—possibly referring to the afterlife or to the inherent worth of obedience. The response seems to defend the orthodox Ash'ari position that human acts are created by God yet the divine purpose remains valid.

Key Terms
القضية الموجبة
الطاعة
التقوى
خلق الكفر
بعثة الأنبياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God creates disbelief in a person, then God cannot genuinely desire obedience from that person.
Correction: The response shows that an affirmative command (e.g., 'obey') remains valid as a divine intention and a basis for reward or punishment, even if the act is created by God. The command itself is what matters, not the origin of the act.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the objection presented in this passage?
A) That God's purpose in sending prophets is to create disbelief.
B) That if God creates disbelief in people, His purpose of obedience would be impossible.
C) That the Quranic verses are not explicit about divine will.
D) That God's answer to the objection is that He makes everyone a believer immediately.

Key Takeaway
Divine commands and purposes remain valid even if God is the ultimate creator of human actions, because an affirmative proposition does not require independent human agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever feel that God's command to obey was somehow invalid because you failed? Reflect on the truth that God's affirmative command stands regardless of your actions. How will this change your attitude toward repentance?
?
If God's purpose is fulfilled in the very command itself, not in your obedience, what does that imply about your responsibility? Does this free you from guilt or magnify your accountability? Be honest.
?
The objector assumed God's purpose must be realized universally. Have you ever doubted God's wisdom when you see widespread disbelief? How does this lesson's response help you trust in His decree while still feeling the urgency to call others to faith?

Refuting the Objection Against Divine Mercy from Creating Disbelievers
Objection-answer unit on divine mercy and punishment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the argument that God's mercy is incompatible with creating disbelievers and punishing them eternally, using scriptural counterarguments and theological reasoning.

Core Idea
God's mercy is not negated by His creating some for punishment; scripture affirms both.

This passage presents a powerful objection from the opponents against the Ash'ari view that God creates all actions, including disbelief, and punishes the disbeliever eternally. The author not only states the objection clearly but also provides a concise refutation using two key scriptural proofs. Understanding this refutation is essential for defending the compatibility of divine mercy with divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice how the objection is framed: the Qur'an describes God as merciful, yet He creates disbelievers and punishes them eternally.
The objection uses verse 4:147 to highlight an apparent contradiction.
2
Identify the two counterarguments
The author gives two answers. Find the first one: citing verse 7:179. Then find the second one: referring to an earlier explanation in Surat al-Fatihah. How do these answer the objection?
3
Reflect on the theological principle
Ask yourself: does God's mercy mean He cannot punish anyone? Or is His mercy understood in a broader context, encompassing both temporal and eternal aspects? The commentary will help clarify.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع السابع عشر للقوم
إن القرآن مملوء من كونه تعالى رحيماً كريماً جواداً محناً، بل الكتب الإلهية بأسرها مملؤة من هذا المعنى، والخلق مطبقون على كونه أكرم الأكرمين، وأرحم الراحمين من ذلك قوله تعالى: ( ما يفعل الله بعذابكم إن شكرتم ) فإن كان هو تعالى يخلق الإنسان ويسلط عليه في الدنيا الفقر والزمانة والمرض والعمى، ثم يخلق فيه الكفر، ثم يخرجه من الدنيا إلى الآخرة، ويدخله في النار أبد الآباد ويعذبه أشد العذاب . فكيف يليق به أن يكون كريماً رحيماً، فضلا عن أن يكون أكرم الأكرمين، وأرحم الراحمين ؟
واعلم : أن هذا نوع من الاستدلال، من أراد تطويله وتشعيبه قدر عليه. والجواب عنه إنه معارض بقوله: ( ولقد ذرأنا لجهنم كثيراً من الجن والإنس ) ثم الجواب عنه ما ذكرناه في سورة الفاتحة، في قوله: الرحمن الرحيم .
The seventeenth type of the people. Indeed the Qur'an is filled with that He, exalted, is Merciful, Generous, Munificent, Benign; rather all the divine books are filled with this meaning, and creation is unanimous upon His being the Most Generous of the generous and the Most Merciful of the merciful. From that is His saying, exalted: 'What would God do with your punishment if you are grateful?' (4:147). For if He, exalted, creates man and inflicts upon him in the world poverty, chronic illness, disease, and blindness, then creates in him disbelief, then takes him out from the world to the Hereafter, and enters him into the Fire for all eternity and punishes him with the most severe punishment, then how is it fitting for Him to be Generous and Merciful, let alone to be the Most Generous of the generous and the Most Merciful of the merciful? And know that this is a type of reasoning; whoever wishes to prolong it and branch it out is able to do so. And the answer to it is that it is countered by His saying: 'And We have certainly created for Hell many of the jinn and mankind' (7:179). Then the answer to it is what we mentioned in Surat al-Fatihah, in His saying: 'The Most Gracious, the Most Merciful' (1:1).
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage presents the seventeenth objection of the opponents (likely Mu'tazila or other rationalists) against the Ash'ari view that God creates all actions, including disbelief, and then punishes the disbeliever eternally. The author structures it as an objection-answer unit. 

First, he states the objection: The Qur'an and all divine books are filled with descriptions of God as Merciful, Generous, etc., and all people agree He is the Most Merciful and Most Generous. He cites the verse 'What would God do with your punishment if you are grateful?' (4:147) implying that punishment only occurs when ingratitude exists, but if God Himself creates the ingratitude, then punishment seems unjust. The objection then argues: If God creates man, afflicts him with poverty, chronic illness, disease, and blindness in this world, then creates disbelief in him, then takes him to the Hereafter and condemns him to eternal punishment in the Fire, how can He be called Merciful and Generous, let alone the Most Merciful and Most Generous?

Second, the author notes that this is a type of reasoning that one could elaborate if desired, but he provides a concise answer. The answer is twofold: 1) The objection is countered by another verse: 'And We have certainly created for Hell many of the jinn and mankind' (7:179), which explicitly states that God created some people with the ultimate destination of Hell. This shows that God's mercy is not incompatible with creating some for punishment. 2) The fuller answer is what was already explained in the commentary on Surat al-Fatihah regarding the names 'ar-Rahman ar-Raheem' (the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful), presumably indicating that God's mercy is inclusive of both believers and disbelievers in this world, but in the Hereafter it is specific to believers, and that His actions are just and wise beyond human comprehension.

The author thus refutes the objection by invoking scriptural authority and earlier theological explanation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this objection is unanswerable or that God's mercy logically contradicts eternal punishment, implying God is not truly merciful.
Correction: The author shows that the objection is answered by scripture (7:179) that states God created some for Hell, and by the theological principle that God's mercy in the Hereafter is specific to believers, while His justice is perfect.

Checkpoint
What is the primary scriptural counterargument the author gives against the objection that God's mercy is incompatible with creating disbelievers and punishing them eternally?
A) God's mercy is limited to this world only.
B) The verse (4:147) shows God does not punish the grateful.
C) The verse (7:179) states God created many for Hell, showing He may create some for punishment.
D) The objection is invalid because God is not truly merciful.

Key Takeaway
The objection that divine mercy is incompatible with creating disbelievers and eternally punishing them is refuted by scriptural evidence that God explicitly created some for Hell and by the nuanced understanding of God's mercy as both universal in this world and specific in the Hereafter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that God created some people for Hell, does it unsettle you or deepen your awe? How does that reaction reveal what you truly believe about God's mercy and justice?
?
If you truly accept that God's mercy is not diminished by His creating disbelievers for punishment, how does that change the way you pray for non-believers? Do you see them as recipients of God's mercy in this world, or only as objects of wrath?
?
Think of a moment when you questioned God's mercy because of suffering you saw. How does this lesson's answer—that mercy and justice coexist by divine wisdom—reshape your trust in God's character? What will you do the next time doubt arises?

Reconciling Divine Mercy with Predestination
The author presents an objection to God's mercy and gives a twofold Quranic answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author answers the objection that God's inflicting suffering and punishment contradicts His attribute of mercy.

Core Idea
God's mercy does not negate His justice; both are affirmed in the Quran.

This passage presents a powerful objection against God's mercy, then answers it with two Quranic references. The objection seems logical: if God creates people, afflicts them, creates disbelief in them, and then punishes them eternally, how can He be called 'Most Merciful'? The author shows that this objection is countered by Quranic verses that affirm God's decree and by a deeper understanding of His names Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objection in the source passage. Notice how it builds from the Quran's emphasis on mercy to the apparent contradiction with punishment.
The objection is a common one; it's important to grasp its force.
2
Identify the two parts of the answer
The author gives two answers: first, a Quranic verse (7:179) that counters the objection; second, a reference to an earlier discussion on Ar-Rahman Ar-Rahim. Find both.
The second answer is not detailed here; it refers back to a prior explanation.
3
Reflect on the logic
Ask yourself: How does the verse 'We have created for Hell many of the jinn and mankind' serve as a counter? What does it imply about God's mercy?
The answer lies in the fact that God's mercy and justice are not mutually exclusive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع السابع عشر للقوم
إن القرآن مملوء من كونه تعالى رحيماً كريماً جواداً محناً ، بل الكتب الإلهية بأسرها مملؤة من هذا المعنى ، والخلق مطبقون على كونه أكرم الأكرمين ، وأرحم الراحمين من ذلك قوله تعالى : ( ما يفعل الله بعذابكم إن شكرتم ) فإن كان هو تعالى يخلق الإنسان ويسلط عليه في الدنيا الفقر والزمانة والمرض والعمى ، ثم يخلق فيه الكفر ، ثم يخرجه من الدنيا إلى الآخرة ، ويدخله في النار أبد الآباد ويعذبه أشد العذاب . فكيف يليق به أن يكون كريماً رحيماً ، فضلا عن أن يكون أكرم الأكرمين ، وأرحم الراحمين ؟
واعلم : أن هذا نوع من الاستدلال، من أراد تطويله وتشعيبه قدر عليه. والجواب عنه أنه معارض بقوله : ( ولقد ذرأنا لجهنم كثيراً من الجن والإنس ) ثم الجواب عنه ما ذكرناه في سورة الفاتحة ، في قوله : الرحمن الرحيم ) .
The seventeenth type for the people: Indeed the Quran is filled with Him, exalted, being merciful, generous, bountiful, beneficent; rather all the divine books are filled with this meaning, and creation is unanimous upon Him being the Most Generous of the generous and the Most Merciful of the merciful. Among that is His saying, exalted: 'What would God do with your punishment if you are grateful?' (4:147) So if He, exalted, creates the human and inflicts upon him in this world poverty, chronic illness, disease, and blindness, then creates disbelief in him, then takes him out of this world to the hereafter, and enters him into the Fire forever and ever and punishes him with the severest punishment, then how is it fitting for Him to be generous and merciful, let alone to be the Most Generous of the generous and the Most Merciful of the merciful? And know: this is a type of reasoning; whoever wishes to elaborate and branch it out is able to do so. The answer to it is that it is countered by His saying: 'And indeed We have created for Hell many of the jinn and mankind' (7:179). Then the answer to it is what we mentioned in Surah al-Fatihah, in His saying: 'Ar-Rahman Ar-Rahim'.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against God's mercy and provides a twofold answer. The objection begins by noting that the Quran and all divine books are filled with descriptions of God as merciful and generous, and that all of creation agrees that He is the Most Merciful. However, if we consider the actual course of events: God creates a human, afflicts him with poverty, disease, and blindness in this world, then creates disbelief in him, then takes his soul and casts him into eternal hellfire with the worst punishment—how can such actions befit a merciful and generous being, let alone the Most Merciful? The author then notes that this is a type of reasoning that could be elaborated further. The answer is given in two parts. First, the objection is countered by the Quranic verse 'And indeed We have created for Hell many of the jinn and mankind' (7:179), which indicates that God's creation of some for punishment does not contradict His mercy; rather, it is a manifestation of His wisdom and justice. Second, the author refers to an earlier discussion in Surah al-Fatihah regarding the divine names 'Ar-Rahman Ar-Rahim' (The Most Gracious, The Most Merciful), suggesting that the full explanation of how God's mercy is reconciled with His punishment has been laid out there. Thus, the objection is not left unanswered but is grounded in Quranic texts and prior theological reasoning.

Key Terms
الرحمن
الرحيم
الاستدلال
الكتب الإلهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's eternal punishment contradicts His mercy, making Him not truly merciful.
Correction: The passage shows that God's mercy is not negated by punishment; both are affirmed in the Quran and reconciled through divine wisdom and justice.

Checkpoint

A) God's mercy is only in this world.
B) The objection is countered by the verse 'And indeed We have created for Hell many of the jinn and mankind.'
C) God does not actually punish anyone.
D) The answer is given in Surat al-Fatihah.

Key Takeaway
God's mercy and justice are not contradictory; both are affirmed in the Quran, and apparent conflicts are resolved through His wisdom and revealed texts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Proof that God is a Blessing to the Disbeliever through Life
The author proves that God is a blessing to the disbeliever by identifying life as the origin of all blessings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why life is considered the origin of all blessings and how God's gift of life to the disbeliever proves that God is a blessing to them.

Core Idea
Life is the foundation of all blessings, even for the disbeliever.

The author argues that God's blessings extend even to the disbeliever, with life being the primary blessing. Without life, no other benefit is possible. This lesson explores that foundational concept and its Quranic basis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
The passage is cut off but the main argument is clear.
2
Identify the central claim
Notice that the author states God is a blessing for the disbeliever, indeed countless blessings. This is the claim to be proven.
3
Trace the reasoning
The author explains that life is the origin of all blessings, because without life no benefit can be obtained. This logical step is key.
Life is the foundation (أصل) of all blessings.
4
Examine the Quranic evidence
The author cites Quran 2:28 to support the argument: 'How can you disbelieve in God when you were dead and He gave you life?' This shows that even the disbeliever receives life from God.
The verse is incomplete in the passage, but the reference is clear.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider that the very existence of life in the disbeliever is a divine blessing, regardless of their disbelief. This challenges the notion that only believers receive God's blessings.
This connects to the common misunderstanding addressed.

For this reason God Almighty began its mention saying: 'How can you disbelieve in God when you were dead and He gave you life?' Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is a positive proof that God is indeed a blessing to the disbeliever. The author states that God is a blessing, indeed countless blessings, arranged in the Quran in a rational order. The key point is that the origin of all blessings is life itself. Without life, no benefit or enjoyment is possible. Therefore, the fact that God grants life to the disbeliever is the foundational blessing. The author quotes Quran 2:28 to support this: 'How can you disbelieve in God when you were dead and He gave you life?' This verse shows that even the disbeliever receives life from God, which is the basis for all other blessings. Then, the author notes that after mentioning life, God follows it with mentioning what is benefited by it (i.e., the various provisions and blessings of this world), though the quote is cut off. The argument emphasizes that the very existence of life in the disbeliever is a divine blessing, regardless of his disbelief.

The Eighth Proof: Justice in the Hadith of Distress
Narrated hadith and its immediate theological evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith of distress serves as a proof for divine justice by asserting that Allah's decree is just and free from injustice.

Core Idea
The phrase 'Just in Your decree' proves Allah’s justice in all decrees.

The eighth proof draws upon a well-known hadith taught by the Prophet (peace be upon him) to those in distress. The key phrase for theological reasoning is 'عدل في قضائك' (Just in Your decree), which implies that Allah’s judgments are inherently just and contain no injustice. This lesson presents the hadith and its direct evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Hadith Carefully
Read the full hadith of distress to understand its context and content. Notice the phrase 'عدل في قضائك' which is the focal point.
The hadith is a supplication for anxiety and grief.
2
Identify the Point of Evidence
Identify the author's point: the statement 'عدل في قضائك' indicates that there is no injustice in Allah's decree.
The word 'عدل' (just) implies the absence of 'جور' (injustice).
3
Reflect on the Link to Divine Justice
Consider how this hadith affirms divine justice. The supplicant acknowledges that Allah's decree is just, which aligns with the principle that Allah does not wrong anyone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : روى ابن مسعود أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : « إذا قال العبد عندما يصيبه هم أو حزن : اللهم إني عبدك وابن عبدك وابن أمتك . ناصيتي بيدك ، ماض في حكمك ، عدل في قضائك . أسألك بكل اسم هو لك سميت به نفسك ، وأنزلته في كتابك ، وعلمته أحداً من خلقك ، واستأثرت به في علم الغيب عندك ، أن تجعل القرآن ربيع قلبي ، ونور بصري ، وجلاء حزني ، وذهاب همي » أذهب الله همه ، وأبدل مكان حزنه فرحاً ، ووجه الاستدلال به : أن قوله : ( عدل في قضائك ، يدل على أن لا جور في قضائك . ولا يقال : إن كل ما يفعله الله تعالى بعبده فهو عدل ، لأنه يتصرف في ملك نفسه . لأنا نقول : فعلى هذا التقدير يمنع أن يكون له ظلم على العبد ، يتملح بذلك . لأن من لم يقدر على الظلم فلم يظلم، لم يكن ذلك قدحاً في حقه . فوجب أن لا يكون ذلك قدحاً في حقه . ولقائل أن يقول : يشكل هذا بقوله تعالى : ( ما كان الله أن يتخذ من ولد ) فإن هذا في معرض المدح، مع أن إيجاد الولد له ممتنع في نفسه
The eighth proof: Ibn Mas'ud narrated that the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'When the servant says when anxiety or grief befalls him: O Allah, I am Your servant, son of Your servant, son of Your maidservant. My forelock is in Your hand, Your command is in effect, Your decree is just. I ask You by every name that is Yours, which You have named Yourself, or revealed in Your book, or taught to any of Your creation, or kept hidden in the knowledge of the unseen with You, to make the Qur'an the spring of my heart, the light of my sight, the dispeller of my grief, and the remover of my anxiety' — Allah removes his anxiety and replaces his grief with joy. The point of evidence from it: His saying 'Just in Your decree' indicates that there is no injustice in Your decree. And it is not said: Everything that Allah does to His servant is justice because He acts in His own kingdom. For we say: On this assumption, it is prevented that He should have injustice towards the servant, seeking praise thereby. Because one who is not capable of injustice and thus does not commit injustice, that is not a defect in his right. Therefore it must not be a defect in His right. And one might say: This is problematic because of His statement: 'It is not for Allah to take a son' (Maryam 35), for this is in the context of praise, even though producing a son for Him is impossible in itself.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof, which cites a hadith about a supplication for anxiety. The key phrase for evidence is 'عدل في قضائك' (Just in Your decree), indicating that Allah's judgment is just with no injustice. The author then addresses an objection: one might say that everything Allah does to His servant is justice because He owns the kingdom. The author responds that this would imply that Allah cannot be unjust, and being incapable of injustice is not a defect in Him; rather, it is a praise. Then a further objection is raised: the verse 'ما كان الله أن يتخذ من ولد' (It is not for Allah to take a son) is also praise, even though producing a son is impossible in itself. This objection seems to challenge the logic that impossibility of injustice is not praise. The author does not provide an explicit answer here, but leaves it as an objection.

Key Terms
عدل في قضائك
الجور
الظلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Allah's decree includes injustice because of the existence of suffering.
Correction: The hadith teaches us to affirm that Allah's decree is just ('عدل'), meaning it is free from injustice. This is a foundational belief.

Checkpoint
What is the key phrase in the hadith used as evidence for divine justice?
A) 'ناصيتي بيدك'
B) 'عدل في قضائك'
C) 'ماض في حكمك'
D) 'اللهم إني عبدك'

Key Takeaway
The hadith of distress provides a direct scriptural basis for affirming that Allah's decree is just and contains no injustice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a severe trial befalls you, do you truly believe in your heart that Allah's decree in that moment is 'عدل' (just)? What would it take for you to internalize this truth so deeply that your first reaction is to say this du'a with conviction?

The Prophet taught us to say 'عدل في قضائك' even before asking for relief. Are you willing to affirm Allah's justice before you see the outcome? Will you commit to reciting this du'a the next time you feel distressed, and let it reshape your understanding of hardship?
?
If you truly believed that every decree is perfectly just, how would your complaining attitude change? What specific worry or resentment do you need to surrender to Allah today, acknowledging that His decree is just?

Objections to the Proof and Their Response
Objection-response dialectic on the hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised against the hadith-based proof for divine justice and how they are addressed, including a challenging objection from a Quranic verse.

Core Idea
Inability to be unjust is a perfection, not a flaw.

After presenting the hadith evidence for divine justice, the author anticipates and refutes an objection. The objection suggests that Allah's justice is merely a consequence of His sovereignty, but the author argues that being incapable of injustice is actually a praiseworthy attribute. Then a further objection is posed using a Quranic verse, which remains for the reader to consider.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection
Read the objection: 'Everything Allah does is justice because He owns the kingdom.' Understand the reasoning.
This objection implies that calling Allah 'just' is trivial if He cannot be unjust.
2
Study the author's response
The author argues that if Allah cannot be unjust, then that is not a defect but a perfection. The inability to do injustice is praiseworthy.
3
Examine the second objection
Read the second objection citing the verse 'It is not for Allah to take a son'. Notice how the objector challenges the logic that impossibility of injustice is praise.
The author does not provide an answer here, leaving it as a point of reflection.

Ali's Definitions of Tawhid and 'Adl
Ali defines tawhid and 'adl with the phrases 'do not imagine Him' and 'do not accuse Him'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concise definitions of tawhid and 'adl given by Ali ibn Abi Talib and the rational basis for denying injustice to God.

Core Idea
Tawhid means not imagining God; 'adl means not accusing Him of injustice.

Ali ibn Abi Talib, a pivotal figure in early Islam, was once asked about the two foundational principles of tawhid (monotheism) and 'adl (divine justice). His reply is remarkably concise yet profound. This lesson unpacks his words and the theological reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text aloud, then the literal translation. Notice the brevity of Ali's response.
The phrase 'do not imagine Him' is key to tawhid.
2
Understand the two definitions
Identify how Ali defines tawhid and 'adl. Reflect on what it means to 'not imagine' God and to 'not accuse' Him.
The commentator explains the second definition in detail.
3
Explore the explanation of 'do not accuse Him'
Read the commentary's expansion: accusing God means attributing ugly deeds or injustice to Him. The companions add that injustice from God is impossible.
The reasoning: God owns everything, so His actions are disposals of His own property.
4
Connect to the broader lesson
Ask yourself how this definition of 'adl differs from common understandings of justice. Why is it crucial to deny injustice to God?
This guards against anthropomorphism and ensures God's absolute sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعنه أنه سأل عن التوحيد والعدل. فقال: «التوحيد أن لا تتوهمه، والعدل أن لا تتهمه، وهذا كلام في غاية الجلالة. والمراد من قوله: (أن لا تتهمه) أي لا تتهمه بأنه يفعل القبائح والفواحش وظلم العباد. وقال أصحابنا: المراد منه: أن الظلم من الله محال. لأن كل ما يفعله فإنه تصرف في ملك نفسه، وليس ذلك بظلم
For it is reported that he was asked about tawhid and 'adl. He said: 'Tawhid is that you do not imagine Him, and 'adl is that you do not accuse Him.' This speech is of utmost majesty. And the meaning of his saying 'do not accuse Him' is that you do not accuse Him of committing ugly deeds, obscenities, and injustice to servants. And our companions said: The intended meaning is that injustice from God is impossible. Because everything He does is a disposal in His own kingdom, and that is not injustice.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage presents a definition from Ali ibn Abi Talib (radiyallahu 'anhu) on the two foundational principles of tawhid and 'adl. The passage begins by reporting that he was asked about these two concepts. His response is concise and profound: 'Tawhid is that you do not imagine Him, and 'adl is that you do not accuse Him.' The commentator then explains the second part: 'do not accuse Him' means not accusing God of doing evil or injustice to His servants. He then adds the position of the author's school (Ash'ari or Maturidi? The text says 'our companions') that injustice from God is impossible, because all His actions are His own disposal over His own property, and that cannot be considered injustice. This establishes that God's actions are not subject to moral categories of injustice.

Key Terms
توحيد

ظلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'do not accuse Him' means we should not question God's actions at all, or that God could potentially commit injustice but refrains.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'do not accuse Him' specifically means not attributing any injustice to God, and that injustice from God is absolutely impossible because He owns everything and His actions are disposals of His own property.

Checkpoint
According to Ali ibn Abi Talib, what does 'adl mean?
A) God does not punish sinners
B) Do not accuse God of injustice
C) God rewards all equally
D) God's justice is unknowable

Key Takeaway
Ali's definition of tawhid and 'adl hinges on negating imagination and accusation, with the rational foundation that injustice from God is impossible because everything He does is within His own dominion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you struggle to trust God's plan after a personal loss, whose voice whispers accusation against Him? How will you silence that voice using the truth that He owns everything and can never be unjust?
?
In your moments of prayer, do you ever catch yourself imagining a form or shape for God? What concrete step will you take today to train your mind to simply affirm His existence without any mental image?
?
If God truly has absolute rights over His creation, does anything in your life—your health, wealth, or relationships—feel like it is 'yours' by right rather than a gift? How does the statement 'disposal in His own kingdom' reshape your gratitude and contentment today?
